SPATIAL TEMPORAL PATTERNS OF 
PEDESTRIAN ACCIDENTS IN DENMARK 
 
 
 
 
Alexandre Aurélien Patrier  [s141349]

Thesis supervisors:

==PAGE:3==
3 
 
Abstract 
 
Pedestrians are vulnerable road users accounting for a large share of road accidents all over 
the world. In Europe, targeting this exposed population to make roads safer has become a 
priority defined by the European Commission. This goal has a specific echo in Denmark 
which mainly focused on cyclists accidents for the past years, neglecting the issue of 
pedestrian crashes. Interestingly, a rather steady state of pedestrian accidents was 
observed in Denmark for the recent years, with a significant share of crashes involving 
drivers leaving the accident scene (hit-and-runs). 
The present thesis thus aimed at studying the issue of pedestrian accidents in Denmark, in 
order to unveil crash patterns and investigate the factors influencing the severity level of 
these crashes. Moreover, the project intended to provide insights for the hit-and-run 
accidents, a specific issue which had been barely studied before. Lastly, the obtained 
results were employed to point out and prioritize specific pedestrian safety issues, as well 
as providing efficient countermeasures to tackle pedestrian accidents. 
The study focused on single pedestrian – single vehicle accidents which occurred in 
Denmark between 2006 and 2015 and reported by the police. For modelling purposes, hit-
and-run accidents were studied separately from the remaining accidents. Two approaches 
including socio-economic and land use variables as well as spatial analysis of crashes were 
employed to analyse the data. Firstly, latent class analysis, an unsupervised clustering 
technique, was used to unveil crash patterns. Secondly, three logit models were developed 
to analyse the factors influencing the severity level of the accidents. A multinomial logit 
model aimed at uncovering the significant factors influencing the severity level, while a 
mixed multinomial logit model was employed to take into account heterogeneity among 
observations. Lastly, a spatial logit model aimed at studying the effect of spatial correlation 
in the data.  
The latent class analysis unveiled specific crash patterns: 8 clusters were identified for the 
non-hit-and-run dataset and 5 for the hit-and-run one. Variables which allowed 
differentiating the clusters were the urban/rural indicator, the location of the accident, the 
vehicle driver’s intoxication and action prior the accident, and the vehicle type hitting the 
pedestrian. The clusters were mapped to identify spatial patterns, and the severity within 
each cluster was analysed. The three severity models yielded interesting results which 
complied with the existing body of knowledge. The multinomial logit model unveiled many 
significant variables, including socio-economic and land use factors. The mixed logit model 
significantly improved the results by incorporating 8 distributed parameters. Finally, the 
==PAGE:4==
4 
 
spatial logit model demonstrated the relevance of taking into account spatial correlation of 
observations. 
The latent class analysis as well as the different severity models provided a complete view 
of pedestrian accidents in Denmark. The study unveiled clear crash types as well as 
manifest spatial patterns, and revealed the relevance of including land use and socio-
economic variables. The different approaches were finally linked together to suggest policy 
implications for the different clusters identified, and shed new lights on hit-and-runs 
accidents.

1. Introduction 
1.1 Motivations 
1.1.1 
The issue of pedestrian safety 
 
The recent years have seen the development of measures to promote active transportation, 
namely walking and cycling. These policies range from advertisement campaigns to 
infrastructure building, for instance with the development of pedestrian streets or cycle lane 
networks. Reasons to promote these modes of transportation mostly rely on health 
concerns ((Mueller et al. 2015), (World Health Organization 2013)) as well as the support of 
more sustainable means of transportation ((European Commission 2009), (Elvik 2009) 
(Frank et al. 2010), (Arsenio et al. 2016)).  
These measures tend to provide significant results and lead to a significant share of trips 
performed by bike or on foot. In Europe, between 20 and 40 % of the trips were for 
instance travelled by cycle or on foot as for 2009 (SafetyNet 2009). 
  
However, pedestrian safety constitutes an important issue when promoting active 
transportation (Sleet et al. 2010), with a particular concern related to the efficiency of 
traffic safety measures targeting non-road users (Mader & Zick 2014). Pedestrians indeed 
are considered as vulnerable road users (Zegeer & Bushell 2012), thus requiring specific 
attention. Indeed, the vulnerability of pedestrians arises from their inability to protect 
themselves against the speed and mass of the other road users (SafetyNet 2009).  
As a consequence, pedestrians face a considerable higher exposure to road accidents 
compared to motorists. (Elvik 2009) estimated for instance the risk of injury per walked-km 
to be four times higher than per driven-km in Norway between 1998 and 2005. In 1995, 
the risk per walked-km in Denmark was estimated to be eight times higher than per driven-
km (Jensen 1998).  Moreover, pedestrians often suffer from serious consequences when 
involved in road crashes ((European Commission 2009), (Danish Road Safety Commission 
2013)). Thus, the European commission recognised the problem of vulnerable road users as 
particularly worrying (European Commission 2009).  
 
Statistics about road accidents clearly point out this issue. In Europe, cyclists and 
pedestrians represented 27 % of road deaths and even 47 % in urban areas in 2008. 
(European Commission 2009).  
==PAGE:14==
14 
 
Beyond the high representation of pedestrians among road accidents, the number of 
fatalities and serious injuries faced by pedestrians is not decreasing as fast as for other road 
user categories in Europe. Indeed, pedestrians face an average decrease of –21 % of 
fatalities between 2001 and 2008 in the EU, compared to – 29 % for all road users as 
shown in Figure 1. 
 
Figure 1: Evolution of road user fatalities in Europe, 2001 – 2008  
[Reproduction from (European Commission 2009)] 
 
Acknowledging the issue of vulnerable road users, the European commission derived 
specific measures to target these types of crashes. Since 2003, legislation has been 
introduced in all member states to reduce injury risks face by vulnerable road users, such as 
energy absorbing car-front structures, advanced braking systems or blind-spot mirrors. In a 
future perspective, further actions are planned to be taken to minimise the risk faced by 
vulnerable road users, such as improved visibility,  speed management or separation of 
dangerous mixed traffic (European Commission 2009).  
 
For all these reasons, it is highly relevant to investigate the risk of accidents faced by 
pedestrians. Reduction or elimination of accidents involving pedestrians is an important and 
achievable policy goal, which can be obtained through the design of countermeasures, 
better education of road users or enforcement actions. It directly contributes to the target of 
halving the number of road fatalities in the European Union by 2020 starting from 2010 
(European Commission 2009); moreover, pedestrian collisions should not be accepted as 
inevitable, as they are both predictable and preventable (World Health Organization 2013). 
 
==PAGE:15==
15 
 
1.1.2 
The case of Denmark 
 
Denmark, as a member of the European Union, is directly concerned by the goals set by the 
European Commission of reducing the risk faced by vulnerable road users, and copes with 
the same issues as the other European countries. 
 
Indeed, Denmark also tends to promote sustainable transport, and faces an increasing 
interest for walking. The Danish Travel Survey thus points out that walking accounted for 
18 % of the trips in 2013 in Denmark, with an average distance of 0.82 km/day/person 
(Transport DTU 2014). This figure slightly increased compared to a previous study carried 
out by (Jensen 1998), from which the average walked distance in 1995 could be estimated 
around 0.75 km/day/person. Thus, it represents a 9 % increase of the average walk 
distance per day and per person in Denmark between 1995 and 2013, to be compared to a 
20% decrease between 1981 and 1995 (Jensen 1998). 
 
The road accident figures in Denmark however exhibit a rather bad trend over time. If the 
rate of pedestrian accidents in Denmark is among the lowest in the OECD (International 
Traffic Safety Data and Analysis Group 2015), an increase of fatal pedestrian crashes from 
50 to 58 was observed between 2001 and 2008 (Vejdirektoratet 2011).  
Moreover, pedestrians accounted for 15 % of the road users deaths and 11 % of the 
serious or minor injuries in 2010-2011 in Denmark (Danish Road Safety Commission 2013), 
while only accounting for 2 % of the traffic-km (Transport DTU 2014). 
These figures also unveiled unexpected specificities of pedestrian accidents in Denmark. 
Indeed, a significant share of these crashes involves drivers knowingly leaving the accident 
scene without giving their name, license number and other information required by the 
police. These cases, called hit-and-runs, accounted for 14 % of all pedestrian accidents in 
Denmark and 7 % of the fatal crashes between 2006 and 2016. Comparatively, hit-and-
runs only accounted for 4.5 % of the pedestrian crashes and 7 % of the fatal ones in Japan 
between 2001 and 2010  (Fujita et al. 2014). This accident category may be of crucial 
importance, as it could for instance imply an increased risk of severe and fatal injury by 
delaying the emergency medical services due to the driver’s escape from the crash scene 
(Tay et al. 2009).  
 
Thus, pedestrian accident risk is an important issue in Denmark, which needs to be tackled. 
With respect to vulnerable road users, the country has been praised for its efforts regarding 
cycling safety, promotion and infrastructure design, and is considered as an example to 
==PAGE:16==
16 
 
follow (Pucher & Buehler 2008). Many studies related to cyclist safety in Denmark were 
published in the last years, focusing on new techniques to unveil crash patterns (Kaplan & 
Prato 2013) or exploring the factors influencing the severity of these accidents, with the 
inclusion of socio-economic, land use and spatial considerations (Kaplan & Prato 2015). 
However, less attention has been given to pedestrian safety in the country in the last years, 
the latest published study on the topic dating back from 1998, and did not use any 
advanced modelling technique, spatial consideration nor included socio-economic and land 
use variables (Jensen 1998).  
 
These different considerations recently led to a renewed interest for pedestrian safety in 
Denmark. Indeed, the European Commission encourages member states to contribute to its 
overall goal of halving the number of road fatalities by 2020 through their national road 
safety strategies by focusing their efforts on areas where their performance is the lowest 
(European Commission 2009). Following this vision, and taking into account the figures of 
pedestrian accidents in Denmark, the Danish Road Safety Commission National Action Plan 
2013 – 2020 declared pedestrians as a specific area of study (Danish Road Safety 
Commission 2013). Thus, important gaps regarding pedestrian safety need to be filled in 
Denmark. 
 
1.2 Problem statement and research questions 
 
The preliminary research conducted in the precedent section unveiled the issue of 
pedestrian accidents as an important topic to be analysed, and revealed Denmark as an 
interesting case. Several questions arose from this first analysis, of which four were 
retained in the scope of the present thesis: 
 
What are the interdependencies and dissimilarities among the pedestrian crashes in 
Denmark? Are there spatial and crash type patterns in the occurrence of these 
accidents? 
 
What are the contributing factors and their influence on the level of severity of 
pedestrian accidents? Are land use and socio-economic factors relevant to explain 
accidents? 
 
What are the specificities of pedestrian hit-and-run crashes in Denmark? 
 
Which countermeasures can be provided to alleviate the risk and the severity of 
these crashes?  
Based on these questions and the preliminary analysis of pedestrian crashes, the following 
problem was formulated and guided the present thesis: 
==PAGE:17==
17 
 
Walking in Denmark accounts for 18 % of the total trips, a comparable share compared to 
cycling. If pedestrians are considered as vulnerable road users as well as cyclists, they 
however receive less focus in Denmark, a cycling nation. Moreover, pedestrians represent 
an important share of the total number of road accidents and face a lower decreasing trend 
of crash occurrence compared to other types of road users. Lastly, hit-and-runs account for 
a significant percentage of pedestrian accidents in the country. The present study hence 
aims at analysing the pedestrian crashes in Denmark with state-of-the-art modelling 
techniques as well as the inclusion of land use, socio-economic and spatial considerations, 
in order to provide a thorough assessment of accident patterns along with the analysis of 
contributing factors to the severity level of these crashes. The results obtained will finally 
support the design of countermeasures to further reduce the risk of accidents associated 
with pedestrians in this country. 
 
1.3 Methodology  
 
Different stages were followed during the thesis, as shown in Figure 2. First, a thorough 
literature review has been carried out, in order to analyse the existing body of literature 
related to pedestrian accidents. It helped to understand how pedestrian crashes studies are 
conducted by peer researchers, especially regarding the modelling techniques used. It also 
provided a basis of knowledge about pedestrian accidents which was used to compare the 
results achieved in this thesis. Lastly, it helped to identify the knowledge gaps that were to 
be filled by the thesis. 
In a second step, data collection has been carried out. This step comprised several sub-
tasks. First of all, variables to be included in the modelling process were identified, based on 
the literature review and an in-depth consideration of the potential factors influencing 
crashes. The different sources of data were then determined and data collected. Thereafter, 
cleaning and checking for quality of data was performed. The data collected included land 
use, socio-economic and built environment variables due to their relevance suggested in the 
literature review. 
Thirdly, pedestrian crash patterns were unveiled by the means of latent class analysis, a 
method successfully used for cyclist accidents in Denmark (Kaplan & Prato 2013). The 
results allowed studying the composition, spatial distribution and severity levels of each 
cluster revealed by the classification method. 
Consequently, three severity models were developed to study the influencing factors on the 
severity levels of the accidents, with the purpose of using advanced modelling techniques 
as suggested by the trend in road safety research. A multinomial logit model aimed at 
uncovering the significant factors influencing the severity level, while a mixed multinomial 
==PAGE:18==
18 
 
logit model was employed to take into account heterogeneity among observations. Lastly, a 
spatial logit model aimed at studying the effect of spatial correlation in the data. The 
different results revealed the influencing factors on the severity level of pedestrian accidents 
as well as their magnitude. 
Lastly, the results from the latent class analysis and the severity models were analysed 
jointly in order to provide a complete view of pedestrian accidents in Denmark, in terms of 
crash type patterns, spatial distribution and contributing factors to the severity of the 
accidents. Specific policy implications were proposed for each cluster, along with new 
insights in hit-and-run accidents.

1.4 Contribution 
 
The present thesis contributes to research in pedestrian safety in several manners, which 
can be listed as follows: 
 
It presents a thorough literature review on pedestrian safety, points out the last 
advances in this research field as well as gaps to be filled; 
 
It includes land use, socio-economic characteristics as well as spatial considerations 
to provide a complete view of pedestrian accidents; 
 
It uses state-of-the-art classification technique to unveil crash patterns; 
 
It employs advanced severity models tackling the issues of heterogeneity and spatial 
correlation among observations; 
 
It provides an update regarding pedestrian safety in Denmark, thus tackling the lack 
of studies for the past years in the country; 
 
It provides meaningful policy implications by bridging the results from the accident 
classification and the severity models; 
 
It sheds new lights on hit-and-run accidents, an issue barely studied before. 
 
1.5 Outline of the thesis 
 
The report is structured in the following way. Section 2 presents the literature review about 
pedestrian accidents, and identifies the knowledge gaps. Section 3 covers the data 
collection process, including the identification of data sources, data processing and 
checking. Section 4 presents a first analysis of the data gathered and gives an overview of 
pedestrian accidents in Denmark. Section 5 focuses on the latent class analysis of 
pedestrian accidents, which aims at uncovering spatial and crash type patterns. Section 6 
handles the severity modelling part of the study, including the specification of the three 
models and their outcomes. Section 7 deals with the discussion of the different results and 
presents countermeasures for pedestrian accidents in Denmark. Lastly, section 8 comprises 
the conclusion of the study, as well as a reminder of the limitations and criticisms of the 
work carried out.

20 
 
 
 
==PAGE:21==
21 
 
2. Literature review 
2.1 Introduction 
 
The literature shows a great interest in pedestrian safety, with the overall goal of providing 
recommendations and designing countermeasures which can alleviate the risk and the 
severity of these accidents.   
The vast body of knowledge can be divided in two major areas. In a first stream of 
research, studies tend to focus on specific traits of pedestrian accidents, both in terms of 
frequency and severity levels. One can for instance cite studies targeting exposed 
populations such as children or elderly people, or papers focusing on specific factors 
affecting accidents, such as alcohol impairment or road design. In a second stream of 
research, studies tend to adopt a broader view of accidents by investigating their similarities 
and differences across a wide range of influencing parameters considered simultaneously 
(related to pedestrian, driver, road characteristics, environment, etc.), thus profiling crash 
patterns. From a temporal perspective, it can be noted that the interests of researchers tend 
to shift from the first stream of research, more traditional in terms of techniques and 
perspectives that are adopted, towards the second stream, more recent and new in its 
approach.  Indeed, if pioneer research adopted a broad view of pedestrian accidents in the 
early 1970s (Snyder & Knoblauch 1971), studies quickly tended to focus on specific types 
or peculiarities of accidents, as for instance alcohol involvement in pedestrian accidents 
(Zylman et al. 1975). The analysis of pedestrian accident patterns only gained a renewed 
interest from the late 1990s and onwards, following the example of (Fontaine & Gourlet 
1997). 
The remainder of the literature review is organised as follows. Section 2.2 provides a 
thorough description of the first stream of research, namely frequency/severity analysis of 
pedestrian accidents. Three sub-sections aim at describing in details the different focuses of 
researchers in this stream of research (section 2.2.1), the last advances adopted in terms of 
explanatory variables (section 2.2.2) and modelling techniques employed (section 2.2.3). 
Thereafter, section 2.3 describes in details the second stream of research dealing with the 
analysis of crash patterns and its developments. Lastly, the summary condenses in section 
2.4 the crucial aspects uncovered by the literature review and identifies the gaps to be filled 
by the present thesis. 
 
==PAGE:22==
22 
 
2.2 Accident frequency and severity analyses  
2.2.1 
A focus on specific traits of accidents 
 
The first area of study is the most documented regarding pedestrian accidents, and also the 
one including the oldest studies in pedestrian safety. This domain can further be refined by 
looking at the different interests shown by researchers.  
In this domain, studies usually concentrate on specific influencing factors related to 
pedestrian accidents. A first and widely covered stream of research focuses on the 
exposure of specific types of population, notably children and elderly ((Oxley et al. 1997), 
(Preusser et al. 2002), (Zajac & Ivan 2003), (Demetriades et al. 2004), (Abdel-Aty et al. 
2007), (Gorrie et al. 2008), (Niebuhr et al. 2016)), and the dominance of male pedestrian 
and driver involvement in crashes ((K. Kim et al. 2008), (Zegeer & Bushell 2012), (Tuckel et 
al. 2014)). These studies hence reveal the over-representation of children and elderly people 
among the victims of pedestrian accidents due to their physical weaknesses and likelihood 
of misjudgement (Oxley et al. 1997), along with their increased risk of facing severe or fatal 
injuries (Niebuhr et al. 2016). For their part, males are usually associated with a higher 
likelihood of adopting a dangerous behaviour, thus leading to an accident (K. Kim et al. 
2008).  
A second stream of research targets the impairment of pedestrians and/or drivers ((Fontaine 
& Gourlet 1997), (LaScala et al. 2001), (Öström & Eriksson 2001), (Clifton et al. 2009)), 
which is associated with an increased risk of accident.  
A third focus investigates the fault of the pedestrian and/or the driver prior to the accident 
((Lee & Abdel-Aty 2005), (Spainhour & Wootton 2007), (Ulfarsson et al. 2010)), which is 
usually related to alcohol impairment or exposed populations such as children or elderly 
(Ulfarsson et al. 2010).  
A fourth line focuses on the type of vehicle involved in pedestrian crashes ((Ballesteros et 
al. 2004), (Roudsari et al. 2005), (J. K. Kim et al. 2008)). For instance, (Tuckel et al. 2014) 
focus on pedestrian-cyclists accidents rates in New-York and California, which are mostly 
associated with no or light injuries, while (Lefler & Gabler 2004) concentrate on LTVs which 
are getting more popular in the USA, yet associated with higher risk of death compared to 
crashes involving regular cars. In a last stream of research, road characteristics are 
investigated, such as intersections in general ((Lee & Abdel-Aty 2005), (Castro et al. 2012)) 
or signalized intersections ((Miranda-Moreno et al. 2011), (Pulugurtha & Sambhara 2011)), 
rural roads ((Islam & Jones 2014), (Tulu et al. 2015)) or speed ((Davis 2001), (Kröyer 
2014)). (Kröyer 2014) for instance reveals that crashes at a speed over 50 km/h is most 
likely to result in fatality, while (Lee & Abdel-Aty 2005) conclude that the number of lanes 
of the road is positively correlated with the risk of accident. 
==PAGE:23==
23 
 
Besides the analysis of specific factors influencing the accidents, some studies also 
investigate particular patterns, such as hit-and-runs. (Solnick & Hemenway 1994) 
investigate the relations between the occurrence of pedestrian hit-and-runs in the USA in 
1989 – 1991 and diverse factors related to the driver (alcohol impairment, previous 
conviction for use of alcohol while driving and status of license), the pedestrian (age and 
gender) or the environment (time, day and season). The study reveals that hit-and-runs are 
more likely to occur at night during weekends, with intoxicated drivers that are more prone 
to escape. In the contrary, accidents involving children or elderly pedestrian are found to be 
less likely to result in hit-and-runs. (MacLeod et al. 2012) conduct out a similar study in the 
USA for the period 1998 – 2007, while (Fujita et al. 2014) analyse hit-and runs in Japan 
between 2001 and 2010. Both studies are consistent with the results of (Solnick & 
Hemenway 1994) with regard to the patterns of hit-and-run pedestrian accidents. Lastly, 
the influence of road characteristics and weather conditions on hit-and-runs frequency have 
been investigated in Ghana by (Aidoo et al. 2013) and revealed that darkness or bad 
weather are associated with an increased likelihood of hit-and-run accidents. 
 
2.2.2 
The introduction of new variables 
 
Most of the studies mentioned in the previous section include similar types of variables, that 
(Eluru et al. 2008) classify under few categories (pedestrian and driver characteristics and 
behaviours, vehicle characteristics, road and environment characteristics). However, the last 
development in pedestrian safety research shows an increasing interest for the study of 
other categories of variables to explain crashes: built environment characteristics, socio-
economic parameters and exposure factors. Indeed, understanding the relations between 
pedestrian accidents and built environment can help to direct public safety investments to 
reduce the risk of accident and improve the walkability of our public environment (Clifton et 
al. 2009), while studying the socio-economic characteristics can unveil some parts of the 
population as particularly vulnerable to pedestrian accidents (Noland & Quddus 2004). 
Lastly, traffic and walk volume are found to have an important impact on the occurrence of 
pedestrian accidents (Elvik 2009). 
 
2.2.2.1 
Built environment variables 
Researchers started to investigate the role of built environment on pedestrian crashes at the 
beginning of the 2000s and onwards ((LaScala et al. 2001), (Ng et al. 2002)), with different 
aspects of built environment investigated. In the first place, refinements concerning the road 
characteristics around the accidents are examined, such as the share of different road types 
around the accident ((Wier et al. 2009), (Zahabi et al. 2011), (Clifton et al. 2009)), the 
number of intersections in a buffer zone ((Wedagama et al. 2006), (Clifton et al. 2009)) or 
the length of sidewalks in the vicinity of the accidents (Moudon et al. 2011). These 
==PAGE:24==
24 
 
characteristics lead to diverse results. Most studies find a positive association between 
severe accidents and the presence of large roads ((Wier et al. 2009), (Zahabi et al. 2011), 
(Ukkusuri et al. 2012)). However, if (Wedagama et al. 2006) find a positive correlation 
between the amount of intersections and the number of fatal accidents in Newcastle, 
England, (Clifton et al. 2009) and (Zahabi et al. 2011)  conclude to a negative correlation, 
respectively in Baltimore, USA and Montreal, Canada.  
In a second stream, researchers consider the land use characteristics around the accidents. 
Most studies focus on reduced types of land use, usually categorized between residential, 
commercial, industrial or recreational areas ((Wier et al. 2009), (Miranda-Moreno et al. 
2011), (Ukkusuri et al. 2012)). However, some studies develop a much more detailed 
assessment of the land use cover, such as (Mohamed et al. 2013) in New-York, which also 
include parking facilities or public institutions, or (Ng et al. 2002) in Hong-Kong, which 
consider 27 types of land use. In a general manner, commercial areas are found to be 
positively correlated with a higher risk of accident  ((Wier et al. 2009), (Miranda-Moreno et 
al. 2011), (Ukkusuri et al. 2012)), while residential areas are usually associated with a 
lower risk ((Ng et al. 2002), (Ukkusuri et al. 2012)). 
Lastly, studies investigate the role of the presence of specific facilities in the vicinity of the 
accidents. (LaScala et al. 2001) examine the presence of bars, restaurants and off-premise 
outlets in proximity to alcohol-related accidents, and establishes a clear link between alcohol 
availability and the number of alcohol-related accidents. (Clifton et al. 2009) and  (Zahabi et 
al. 2011) investigate the problem of children accidents, by studying the influence of schools 
in close proximity of crashes on their severity level, with opposite conclusions. While 
(Clifton et al. 2009) find a positive association between the presence of schools and the 
severity level of pedestrian accidents in Baltimore, USA, (Zahabi et al. 2011) conclude to a 
negative association in Montreal, Canada. The presence of bus and rail stops are also 
investigated ((Miranda-Moreno et al. 2011), (Moudon et al. 2011)) as these facilities are 
expected to attract more pedestrians especially for commuting, while facilities such as 
restaurants, cinemas, hotel rooms are considered for their attraction of leisure-time 
pedestrians  ((Ng et al. 2002), (Moudon et al. 2011)). 
 
2.2.2.2 
Socio-economic variables 
Beyond the characteristics of the built environment, researchers also started to consider the 
influence of the population specificities as explanatory factors for pedestrian accidents.  
The characteristics of the population are used to unveil potential exposed populations. In the 
first place, the age distribution within the population has been examined ((Noland & Quddus 
2004), (Wier et al. 2009)), without a clear correlation between specific age groups over-
representation in the population and accident sample (Noland & Quddus 2004). The blend of 
==PAGE:25==
25 
 
population in terms of ethnicity is also investigated in the USA (LaScala et al. 2001), 
revealing that minorities are more exposed to accidents than the rest of the population. The 
household composition, i.e. marital status ((LaScala et al. 2001), (Al-Ghamdi 2002)) and the 
number of children (Cottrill & Thakuriah 2010) are as well considered as potential 
explanatory variables, with a positive correlation between the number of children in the 
households and the amount of accidents established by (Cottrill & Thakuriah 2010) in 
Chicago, USA. 
Lastly, a great interest is shown towards the social level of the population ((Noland & 
Quddus 2004), (LaScala et al. 2004), (Wier et al. 2009), (Cottrill & Thakuriah 2010)), 
which is measured in terms of median income (Noland & Quddus 2004); employment rate 
(Graham & Glaister 2003), car ownership ((Loukaitou-Sideris et al. 2007), (Cottrill & 
Thakuriah 2010)) or level of education ((LaScala et al. 2004), (Cottrill & Thakuriah 2010)). 
All studies conclude to an increased exposure of deprived populations to pedestrian 
accidents; for instance, (Cottrill & Thakuriah 2010) establish that citizens from deprived 
areas of Chicago, USA are 2.6 times more exposed to pedestrian accidents compared to the 
richest areas of the city. 
 
2.2.2.3 
Exposure variables 
In order to get a complete picture of pedestrian accidents, researchers also investigate the 
exposure of pedestrians to the road traffic. (Greene-Roesel et al. 2010) provide a review of 
the different possibilities to estimate pedestrian exposure: based on population density, road 
and pedestrian traffic volumes, number of trips per pedestrian and per unit of time, or 
distance and duration of trips per pedestrian and per unit of time.  
Population density is widely used in the literature to account for pedestrian exposure 
((Graham & Glaister 2003), (Noland & Quddus 2004), (Wier et al. 2009)), with diverse 
conclusions. (Wier et al. 2009) find a positive correlation between the population density 
and the number of accidents in San Francisco, while (Graham & Glaister 2003) identify a 
decreasing trend of the number of accidents above a threshold of population level in 
England. Moreover, this type of measure does not account for the number of people who 
are actually travelling in the area, nor the distance travelled or the time used to travel, hence 
poorly covering the exposure of pedestrians (Greene-Roesel et al. 2010). 
In a second stream, researchers employ road and pedestrian traffic volumes to account for 
exposure. Road traffic is usually collected in the form of AADT (annual average daily traffic) 
on road sections ((Lyon & Persaud 2002), (Loukaitou-Sideris et al. 2007), (Wier et al. 
2009), (Moudon et al. 2011)) or traffic counts at specific locations such as intersections 
((Miranda-Moreno et al. 2011), (Pulugurtha & Sambhara 2011)). Results are quite diverse: 
(Miranda-Moreno et al. 2011) find a positive correlation between road traffic and number of 
==PAGE:26==
26 
 
accidents, while (Pulugurtha & Sambhara 2011) conclude to a negative interaction, both 
studies considering intersections traffic counts respectively in Montreal, Canada and 
Charlotte, USA. Pedestrian traffic volumes are less frequently included in models, as fewer 
pedestrian traffic collection schemes are implemented by traffic safety administrations 
compared to road traffic (Greene-Roesel et al. 2010). However, some studies rely on local 
initiatives of pedestrian traffic measurements ((Leden 2002), (Lyon & Persaud 2002); 
(Jacobsen 2003), (Loukaitou-Sideris et al. 2007); (Pulugurtha & Sambhara 2011)). As for 
road traffic, diverse and conflicting results can be obtained from the literature: (Leden 2002) 
concludes to a negative relation between pedestrian traffic volume and the frequency of 
accidents at intersections, while (Pulugurtha & Sambhara 2011) find a positive correlation, 
respectively in Hamilton, Canada and Charlotte, USA. 
The conflicting results obtained regarding the link between road and pedestrian traffic 
volumes and the frequency of pedestrian accidents have been specifically investigated by 
researchers. (Leden 2002) is among the first studies to conclude to a negative effect of 
pedestrian traffic flows on the risk of accident, called “safety-in-numbers”. Several studies 
later confirmed this effect, as (Jacobsen 2003) who concludes to a lower risk of accident 
linked to a higher distance walked per person and per day in 47 Danish towns, or (Elvik 
2009) and (Elvik 2016) which support the inverse relationship between pedestrian traffic 
volumes and the number of pedestrian accidents in Norway.   
 
2.2.3 
The diversification of modelling approaches 
 
The previous sections showed the different types of analyses belonging to the first stream 
of research, in terms of areas of interests and explanatory factors related to pedestrian 
accidents. Last advances in research saw the surge of refinements in terms of modelling 
techniques, as well as the diversification of levels of spatial aggregated data. 
 
2.2.3.1 
The development of new models 
In all the studies previously mentioned, researchers focus on two different aspects of 
pedestrian accidents, namely frequency and severity analysis. Indeed, frequency analysis 
aims at analysing the factors leading to an accident, while severity modelling aims at 
investigating the variables which influence the severity level of these crashes. Both types of 
studies have seen the development of refinements in terms of modelling techniques 
employed. Regarding frequency analysis, count models such as Poisson and Negative-
Binomial models are the most documented ((Kim et al. 2006), (Miranda-Moreno et al. 
2011)). However, several concerns related to these models have been raised, such as 
underreporting of accidents for damage-only crashes, the heterogeneity of the data or 
spatial correlation of data (Cottrill & Thakuriah 2010). In order to compensate these 
problems, more advanced models have been developed, such as zero-inflated Poisson model 
==PAGE:27==
27 
 
to tackle underreporting (Wedagama et al. 2006), random parameters models to account for 
heterogeneity of data ((Ukkusuri et al. 2011), (Tulu et al. 2015)) or Bayesian Poisson 
lognormal to account for spatial correlation (Siddiqui et al. 2012). An extensive literature 
review of the different refinements in terms of frequency modelling is provided by (Lord & 
Mannering 2010).  
Regarding severity modelling, the most common models are binary logit model (Sze & Wong 
2007), multinomial logit ((Tay et al. 2011), (Rifaat et al. 2011)) or ordered logit and probit 
((Lee & Abdel-Aty 2005), (Jang et al. 2013)). As for the frequency analysis, these severity 
models have been criticised for the same modelling issues (heterogeneity, spatial 
correlation). This led to the development of new models, such as mixed generalized ordered 
logit (Eluru et al. 2008) or heteroskedastic multinomial logit (J. K. Kim et al. 2008) to 
account for heterogeneity of data. For a complete review of advances in severity analysis, 
one can refer to (Mannering & Bhat 2014) and (Islam & Jones 2014). 
 
2.2.3.2 
The introduction of spatial considerations 
The adoption of new variables regarding built environment, exposure and socio-economic 
characteristics implicitly induced the use of accidents’ geo-location in order to unveil spatial 
influence on the frequency or severity of these crashes. Thus, more and more pedestrian 
safety studies included the accidents’ GPS coordinates from the early 2000s. (LaScala et al. 
2000) is among the first studies to employ geo-located crashes to link demographic and 
environment considerations to pedestrian accidents’ severity in San Francisco, USA.  
These studies investigate the spatial influence of built environment, exposure and socio-
economic characteristics by aggregating accidents at different levels. The most common 
level of aggregation found in the literature is the census tract ((LaScala et al. 2000), 
(Loukaitou-Sideris et al. 2007), (Wier et al. 2009),  (Cottrill & Thakuriah 2010)), while some 
studies focus on different levels such as boroughs in New York (Aziz et al. 2013), wards in 
England ((Graham & Glaister 2003), (Noland & Quddus 2004)) or ZIP codes in Florida (Lee 
et al. 2015). The choice of the spatial aggregation level is important and can provide 
benefits and disadvantages; for instance, (Zahabi et al. 2011) outline that a more 
disaggregate analysis allows to retain the micro level characteristics of the accident, which 
would be lost in case of higher spatial aggregation level. On the other hand, (Aziz et al. 
2013) consider that more aggregated models can provide meaningful insights related to 
pedestrian accidents. Thus, the study develops five separate models for the different 
boroughs of New York, USA, which prove to deliver interesting and distinct results 
depending on the area considered (Bronx, Brooklyn, Manhattan, Queens or Staten Island). 
The authors assign these differences to the specificities of each borough in terms of 
population density, built environment, demographics, etc. 
==PAGE:28==
28 
 
2.3 Accident pattern analysis 
 
The development of new sets of variables as described in section 2.2.2, as well as the 
refinements in terms of modelling techniques as shown in section 2.2.3 gradually led to the 
emergence of a second major stream of research in pedestrian safety. Indeed, several 
concerns haven been raised against traditional methods employed in the first stream of 
research. As a matter of fact, explanatory factors are exploited independently rather than 
being examined jointly, hence leaving unobserved relations among crashes (Prato et al. 
2012). This issue is of great importance, as accidents are random and multifactor in their 
nature, and resulting from a chain of events. Moreover, if every crash is the result of a 
unique combination of events, several factors may be common across crash circumstances 
(Montella et al. 2011).  In turn, frequency or severity analyses of pedestrian accidents may 
be biased due to these unobserved correlations. If attempts to separate data in more 
homogenous subsets have been provided based on expert domain knowledge, 
methodological decisions (e.g. distinction or urban or rural areas, distinction of young and 
elderly pedestrians) or a focus on a specific type of accidents, no guarantee has been given 
regarding the uniformity of the considered clusters (Depaire et al. 2008). 
In order to overcome these difficulties, data mining techniques gained a greater focus in 
pedestrian accident analysis from the late 1990s (Fontaine & Gourlet 1997), following the 
international trend in road safety and statistical analysis towards the study of patterns in 
accidents (Theofilatos & Efthymiou 2012). Indeed, these techniques provide an overview of 
crashes patterns by unveiling unobserved relations among the accidents by the 
establishment of clusters, hence reducing the heterogeneity of data. Moreover, this new 
approach on pedestrian accident analysis helps to identify safety issues based on the 
patterns of crashes, thus providing new recommendations for policy makers to address the 
risks faced by pedestrians ((Depaire et al. 2008), (Theofilatos & Efthymiou 2012), 
(Sasidharan et al. 2015)).  
 
2.3.1 
The introduction of clustering methods 
 
Different techniques are employed to classify pedestrian accidents. In their pioneering work, 
(Fontaine & Gourlet 1997) perform a typology assessment of pedestrian crashes in France 
in 1990 – 1991. The study identifies four main categories of pedestrian fatalities, with clear 
and multi-faceted profiles: elderly women crossing in urban area; men walking along rural 
road, intoxicated and at night; children playing at daytime in urban area; accidents related to 
secondary accidents (loss of control, pedestrian on pavement). The development of 
computers allowed researchers to employ more advanced techniques, such as K-means 
clustering ((Kim & Yamashita 2007), (Mohamed et al. 2013)), Kohonen networks (Prato et 
al. 2012), non-parametric classification (Chang & Wang 2006), association rules (Montella 
==PAGE:29==
29 
 
et al. 2011) or two-step clustering (Theofilatos & Efthymiou 2012). These techniques allow 
unveiling different multi-faceted accident patterns, in terms of spatial, frequency or severity 
factors. (Kim & Yamashita 2007) successfully employ K-means clustering to unveil spatial 
clusters of pedestrian accidents in Hawaii, revealing ten hotspots for these accidents. (Prato 
et al. 2012) analyse pedestrian fatalities in Israel between 2003 and 2006, and unveil five 
different types of accidents (elderly in urban areas, pedestrian-two wheel vehicles, rural 
accidents at night, young pedestrians at night and rural children), followed by 
recommendations for these different crash types. The study reveals an important difference 
between urban and rural accident patterns and therefore suggests that pedestrian living and 
moving in different geographical location do not face the same risk of accidents. The 
authors additionally emphasize that the influence of the environment in the vicinity of the 
crashes, for instance land use, population or medium income in the area could be an 
interesting addition to the body of knowledge for pedestrian accidents. (Montella et al. 
2011) investigate the pedestrian crash severity in Italy over the period 2006 - 2008, and 
also identifies the overrepresentation of nigh time for higher severity of accidents. 
Pedestrian accidents involving two-wheel vehicles are found to be associated with young or 
elderly pedestrians, while the majority of accidents are found to take place in urban areas. 
 
2.3.2 
The development of latent class analysis 
 
The most recent development of pedestrian accidents patterns analysis shows an interest 
towards the latent-class clustering method. Being already used for road crashes ((Depaire et 
al. 2008), (De Oña et al. 2013), (Weiss et al. 2015)) and cyclists accidents (Kaplan & Prato 
2013), only two recent studies applying latent class analysis for pedestrian accidents were 
been found in the literature ((Mohamed et al. 2013), (Sasidharan et al. 2015)).  
(Mohamed et al. 2013) develop an analysis of pedestrian-vehicle crash severity in New York 
and Montreal between 2003 and 2006. The study first employs LCA for the case of New 
York and K-means clustering for Montreal in order to unveil unforeseen patterns in 
pedestrian accidents, followed by severity models developed for each of the uncovered 
clusters (ordered probit and multinomial logit respectively in New York and Montreal). The 
analysis shows that the clustering method could be seen as a first tool to determine 
homogenous subsets of accident patterns, which can then be further investigated by 
developing regression models of severity within the clusters. Seven clusters of accidents are 
identified in New York : accidents at signalized intersections in daylight; signalized 
intersections with illumination; missing driver; accident with a car/van/pick-up; accidents at 
two or more lanes roads outside signalized areas; parking accidents; multiple missing 
values, thus adding new patterns of accidents in urban areas to the existing body of 
knowledge. With regard to the significance of variables on the level of severity of accidents, 
the study also reveals that some variables are relevant at the whole dataset level, but not 
==PAGE:30==
30 
 
within the cluster or vice versa, thus proving that clusters have their own internal 
characteristics. The results comply with the exiting works, showing that heavy vehicle, dark 
lighting conditions, pedestrian aged below 5 or over 65 years significantly increase the risk 
of being killed in a road accident.  Some limitations may however arise from the study: the 
authors suggest that the effect of operating speed on the injury severity level should be 
investigated as well as other built environment, as the study only focuses on city centres. 
Moreover, some important variables such as alcohol consumption are not considered as 
influencing factors on the accidents.  
(Sasidharan et al. 2015) follow a similar framework as (Mohamed et al. 2013) to analyse 
pedestrian accidents in Switzerland between 2009 and 2012. An LCA model is first 
established, followed by regression models (binary logit model) for accident severity within 
the clusters. Compared to (Mohamed et al. 2013), the analysis extends the study area to an 
entire country (thus taking into account rural areas), and unveils seven patterns of 
accidents: night time midblock crashes; driver violating right of way during daytime; driver 
violating right of way during night time; weekend night time under influence; crashes 
involving old pedestrians at driveways; pedestrian violating rules, children jaywalking; 
crashes involving old pedestrians, careless reverse on driveways. These patterns comply 
with the previous findings of (Fontaine & Gourlet 1997) and (Prato et al. 2012). Factors 
increasing the risk of fatal accidents are found to be national roads, high speed limit, 
alcohol, mid-block crossing, heavy vehicles and pedestrian violation of rules. As for 
(Mohamed et al. 2013), the development of severity regression models for each cluster 
reveals that the impacts of the different variables greatly change from one cluster to 
another. Some limitations can also be found in the study. For instance, important variables 
related to the environment are left out compared to (Mohamed et al. 2013), such as built 
environment, detailed road characteristics, driver and pedestrian behaviours and locations at 
the moment of the accident.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:31==
31 
 
2.4 Summary 
 
The literature review showed an extensive study of pedestrian accidents in all regions of the 
world, with many different approaches and specific interests of researchers. Two main 
stream of research were unveiled: on the one hand, the analysis of frequency and severity 
of accidents, using traditional modelling techniques by looking at each explanatory variable 
independently; on the other hand, the identification of patterns in accidents, thus analysing 
the effects of the combination of variables on the accidents. 
The literature review also revealed that the second stream of research is the one receiving 
the more attention in the recent years, and presents some gaps to be filled. Indeed, only 
few studies have been proposed so far, covering partially some regions of the world 
(France, Italy, Greece, Switzerland, Israel, Hawaii, Taiwan, New York and Montreal). 
Moreover, most of these studies do not include detailed variables related to the built 
environment, socio-economic characteristics or exposure; they also do not include spatial 
considerations if these accidents. However, these variables, as described in sections 2.2.2 
and 2.2.3.2, have proven their relevance in numerous studies analysing frequency and 
severity of accidents.  
The analysis unveiled that studies either focus on frequency/severity analysis or patterns 
analysis of accidents. Only few studies try to connect the two approaches, or partially meet 
the goal of providing a full view of accidents. For instance, (Sasidharan et al. 2015) carries 
out a clustering analysis, followed by the construction of multinomial logit severity models 
for each clusters. This approach leads to a biased estimation of the severity models: indeed, 
sampling the dataset according to the prevalence of features in clusters distorts the 
parameters estimates of the severity models. Moreover, the study does not include land use 
and socio-economic variables, while the severity models do not take into account mixed 
effects or spatial correlation as shown in the latest trends in pedestrian safety research. 
Only four studies were found to investigate the issue of hit-and-runs accidents, despite their 
high severity level observed and covered the USA, Japan and Ghana. 
Lastly, few studies have been carried out on pedestrian safety in Denmark. Besides the 
annual reports of the Danish Road Directorate, no recent studies related to pedestrian safety 
in Denmark was found. To the best of knowledge regarding pedestrian safety, (Jensen 
1998) constitutes the latest published study in Denmark. Compared to the high interest 
shown regarding cyclist safety, an important gap has to be filled in this regards, in order to 
give an updated view of pedestrian safety in Denmark. 
As a consequence, the thesis aims at filling the previously mentioned gaps as described in 
section 1.4 by: 
==PAGE:32==
32 
 
 
Including land use, socio-economic and spatial characteristics to provide a complete 
view of pedestrian accidents; 
 
Using state-of-the-art classification technique to unveil crash patterns; 
 
Employing advanced severity models tackling the issues of heterogeneity and spatial 
correlation among observations; 
 
Providing an update regarding pedestrian safety in Denmark, thus tackling the lack of 
studies for the past years in the country; 
 
Yielding new lights on hit-and-run accidents, an accident type barely studied before. 
 
 
==PAGE:33==
33 
 
3. Data collection 
3.1 Data collection process 
 
Data collection constitutes an important phase in the study. It comprises: 
 
The identification of relevant explanatory variables related to pedestrian crashes; 
 
The identification of data sources; 
 
The extraction of the variables from the data sources; 
 
The cleaning and validation phase; 
 
The potential adaptation of the variables (mathematical transformation, discretisation 
of continuous variables, etc.). 
This phase is really important, as the outcomes of the study are directly linked to the quality 
of the inputs. As a consequence, data gathering has to be undertaken with great care.  
 
3.2 Identification of potential variables 
 
In this stage, variables related to pedestrian crashes must be identified. The determination 
of contributing factors can be either derived from the existing body of literature, but also 
from the road safety researcher’s experience and intuitions about the parameters that may 
influence the crashes. 
When extracting potential variables, it is also crucial to question the interdependence among 
them. Indeed, if two variables accounting for the same effect are included in the model, it 
would result in collinearity that would bias the results. One should therefore deeply consider 
which effects the variables account for. 
Two main types of variables are to be included in the models: 
 
The dependent variables, which describe the outcomes whose variations are studied; 
 
The independent variables (also called explanatory variables), which represent the 
potential sources of variations in the dependent variables. 
 
3.2.1 
Dependent variables 
 
As mentioned previously, the dependent variables constitute the outcomes of the studied 
phenomenon.  When it comes to the analysis of road accidents, mainly two types of 
dependent variables can be defined: 
==PAGE:34==
34 
 
 
The frequency of the crashes: the outcome of the model is then related to the 
occurrence of the crashes; 
 
The severity of the crashes: the outcome is then related to the level of severity 
sustained by the persons involved in the crash. With respect to pedestrian crashes, 
the severity is defined by the injuries incurred to pedestrians during a crash. Injuries 
are most commonly divided in four categories in road safety studies, namely no 
injury, light injury, severe injury and fatality. 
Usually, road accident studies either focus on frequency ((Kim et al. 2006), (Miranda-
Moreno et al. 2011)) or severity of the accidents ((Kim et al. 2010), (Aziz et al. 2013)). 
However, it is also possible to simultaneously consider the frequency and the severity of the 
accidents, as (Kaplan & Prato 2015) proposed in their study related to cyclists crashes in 
Copenhagen. 
Two different types of studies are carried out in the present thesis. The latent class analysis 
aims at uncovering the pedestrian crash patterns, and is therefore dealing with the 
frequency of occurrence of crashes. In a second time, the study analyses the contributing 
factors to the severity level of the crashes. 
 
3.2.2 
Independent variables 
 
The independent variables, also called explanatory variables, aim at explaining the variations 
observed in the dependent variables (frequency or severity of the pedestrian crash). As a 
consequence, it is of high importance to cover in the most complete way the potential areas 
that could influence the pedestrian crashes. 
The independent variables that are to be included in the model can be derived from the 
literature review as described in sections 2.2.1 and 2.2.2. Hence, this paragraph acts as a 
reminder and tends to summarise the previous findings with respect to the independent 
variables. Several categories of independent factors can be identified: 
 
Pedestrian characteristics: this corresponds to the most obvious category of 
variables to be included. The age of the pedestrian usually influences the crashes, for 
instance with children and elderly that are more exposed to crashes due to their 
troubles to assess the danger of the road traffic and their physical weaknesses (Lee 
& Abdel-Aty 2005).The gender of the pedestrian is also relevant and may influence 
his/her behaviour, as some studies for instance show that males may adopt a riskier 
behaviour compared to female (Ulfarsson et al. 2010). The behaviour of the 
pedestrian describes his/her action before the crash, for instance walking along the 
road, crossing at crosswalks or jaywalking (crossing outside dedicated crossing 
locations), and therefore explains his/her potential responsibility in the crash. Lastly, 
==PAGE:35==
35 
 
the intoxication of the pedestrian is an important factor that receives specific 
attention in several studies (Öström & Eriksson 2001) and increases the risk of 
occurrence of an accident; 
 
Vehicle characteristics:  the type of vehicle involved in the pedestrian crash is of high 
importance. Indeed, it is obvious that the severity of the crash is directly affected by 
the type of vehicle: a crash with a light vehicle (for instance bike, moped) would 
likely result in a less severe crash compared to a situation involving a heavy vehicle 
(bus, truck) (Ballesteros et al. 2004). The speed of the impact is also crucial, as a 
higher speed is widely associated with a higher severity of the crash (Rosen et al. 
2011).The vehicle design may also influence the crash severity, for instance with the 
implementation of different technologies to alleviate the risk and severity of a crash 
involving a pedestrian (bumpers, automatic braking system, pedestrian airbag, etc.) 
(Euro NCAP 2016). This last variable is however far less used, as police reports 
usually do not include this kind of variable ; 
 
Driver characteristics:  in a similar way as for the pedestrian, the age and gender are 
influential factors related to the driver, that may directly relate to their behaviour 
(Ulfarsson et al. 2010). Young drivers may be associated with inexperience, while 
older drivers may suffer from cognitive alteration; moreover, male drivers may be 
linked to riskier behaviours. The behaviour relates to the driver’s action before the 
crash and may give insights about the driver’s responsibility in the crash (going 
straight, overtaking, turning at intersection, violating traffic rules, etc.). Lastly, the 
intoxication  of the driver may be associated with a higher frequency of occurrence 
of the crashes (Eluru et al. 2008); 
 
Road characteristics:  this category investigates the various road designs that may 
influence the crashes (Flahaut 2004). One can directly think of the road category 
(highway, large road, narrow street, etc.), the speed limit or the presence of a 
sidewalk. However, further specifications influencing the ability for a pedestrian to 
cross the road are to be included, such as the width of road or the number of lanes 
which influence the time needed for a pedestrian to cross, the presence of pedestrian 
crosswalks and traffic lights, or even the presence of a median, which allows the 
pedestrian to pause during his/her crossing; 
 
Crash location: on top of the road characteristics, the location of the crash may also 
unveil important features of the crashes (Gitelman et al. 2012). One can think of 
crossing on straight road or outside crosswalks, which may relate to an insufficient 
provision of crossing facilities for pedestrians, or different types of intersections (4-
arms, 3-arms, roundabouts, signalised intersections, etc) and their specific risks 
associated (wait for green light, visibility in intersection, etc.); 
 
Environment: the environmental characteristics of the crashes may also be relevant 
to include (Eluru et al. 2008). One can think of the season which may be associated 
==PAGE:36==
36 
 
with more difficult road conditions (for instance snow in winter), but also weekdays 
or weekends, the latter being possibly associated with pedestrians going out in their 
leisure time. Public holidays such as Christmas, New Year’s Eve or national days may 
also increase the risk of accidents, due to important road traffic and festive 
atmosphere that they imply. Lastly, time, weather, and lighting conditions may 
significantly alter the visibility of the pedestrian from the driver’s perspective and 
influence the risk of crash; 
 
Exposure:  the risk of an accident may be related to the road traffic, usually 
measured by the AADT (Annual Average Daily Traffic) which corresponds to the 
daily number of vehicles crossing a given stretch of road, or the public transport 
traffic, driving the pedestrian demand (Moudon et al. 2011). Moreover, the 
pedestrian traffic volume may be a highly relevant factor influencing the risk of 
pedestrian crashes (Lyon & Persaud 2002); 
 
Land use: incorporated in several recent studies (Ukkusuri et al. 2012), it can be 
linked to the pedestrian traffic volume and specific crash types: for instance, 
residential areas may be linked to commuting trips, while shopping streets or 
recreational areas are obviously more related to leisure time; 
 
Presence of specific facilities: the presence of specific types of facilities may 
influence the demand for pedestrian and relate to specific types of crashes. For 
instance, the presence of schools may be directly linked to the occurrence of crashes 
involving children (Abdel-Aty et al. 2007), while accidents located at bus stops may 
be associated with pedestrians hit by busses or trying to cross the road hidden by a 
bus. A last example may be the presence of restaurants, bars and night-clubs, which 
may be associated with intoxicated pedestrians at night during weekends (LaScala et 
al. 2000); 
 
Socio-economic variables: the characteristics of the population may also influence 
the risk of pedestrian crashes (Noland & Quddus 2004). Indeed, the population 
density may be linked the pedestrian traffic volume, as well as car ownership, 
income level, or unemployment rate. For instance, one can expect that inhabitants 
from deprived areas may be less likely to own a car and therefore walk more or use 
public transports, increasing their exposure to crashes (Cottrill & Thakuriah 2010). 
 
 
As it can be seen, variables than can explain pedestrian crashes are numerous and cover a 
wide spectrum of categories. Moreover, many of these variables are spatial data (exposure, 
land use, socio-economic characteristics), thus requiring the use of GIS (Geographic 
Information System) software to collect them. It is important to cover as insofar as possible 
these different areas in order not to leave some characteristics of crashes uncovered, as 
summarised in Figure 3: 
==PAGE:37==
37 
 
 
 
Figure 3: Pedestrian accidents influencing factors 
[Adapted from (Zegeer & Bushell 2012)] 
 
3.3 Data sources  
 
In order to collect data related to pedestrian accidents in Denmark, several sources of 
information have to be considered. First of all, the Danish Road Accident Registration 
constitutes the main source of data, comprising the database of pedestrian accidents in 
Denmark. In order to gather information related to the land use, socio-economic or road 
characteristics, extra sources of information have to be considered, such as the NAVTEQ 
road network, the Danish National Transport Model, or land use layers from the European 
Environmental Agency.  
3.3.1 
Danish Road Accident Registration 
 
The first and main source of data is the Danish Road Accident Registration. In Denmark, 
every road traffic accident has to be recorded by the police, according to a data collection 
scheme established by the Danish Road Directorate. This data collection scheme is 
mandatory in every member state of the European Union, following the decision of the 
European Commission to establish a common database for road accidents all over Europe

==PAGE:38==
38 
 
(decision 93/704/EC). The records cover several aspects of the accidents, including the 
characteristics of the vehicles involved, the injuries sustained by the victims, but also the 
conditions of the accident (accident scenario, road conditions, lighting and weather 
conditions, etc.). Lastly, the GPS coordinates of each accident are captured. Data are 
transferred to the Danish Road Directorate every week (International Traffic Safety Data and 
Analysis Group 2015), which is responsible to store and maintain the records together with 
Statistics Denmark (Danmarks Statistik 2016c) (Vejdirektoratet 2016a). The records can be 
reused by the Road Directorate to spot specific issues related to road safety and derive 
specific countermeasures. Several publications and statistics are also made available to the 
public based on this database, such as yearly statistics related to road safety, allowing 
public authorities to make better informed decisions in this field. Lastly, the Danish Road 
Accident Registration is made available for road safety researchers.  
The considered dataset for the thesis is directly extracted from the Road Directorate 
database, and comprises all road traffic accidents involving pedestrians during the years 
2006 – 2015, representing 9 283 records. 
The database is made of three different files covering each different aspects of the 
accident: 
 
A crash file, recording the scenario of the accident and environmental characteristics; 
 
A person file, describing the characteristics of each victim (age, gender, injury, etc.); 
 
A unit file, describing the characteristics of each element (vehicle, bike, pedestrian) 
involved. 
As a consequence, one crash file is created per accident; however, several person and 
vehicle files can be linked to a single crash, depending on the number of vehicles and 
victims involved. 
In total, more than 90 different parameters are collected for each accident. Some of them 
may be subjective, such as the estimated speed before the crash, which relies on 
statements by witnesses. In a similar manner, the severity level of accident is estimated by 
the police officer, thus leading to a subjective judgment (International Traffic Safety Data 
and Analysis Group 2015). 
 
3.3.1.1 
Crash file 
The crash file comprises the general characteristics of the accident. First of all, geographical 
and temporal characteristics of the accident are recorded, namely the GPS coordinates 
along with date and time of the accident. Several elements of information related to the 
accident are also recorded, such as weather conditions (road surface conditions, visibility, 
lighting conditions), road characteristics (speed limit, type of road segment such as 
==PAGE:39==
39 
 
roundabout, 4-legged intersection) and environmental conditions (urban or rural areas, 
roadside building types). Lastly, the scenario of the accident is reported, characterised by a 
precise description as shown in example in Figure 4.  
 
 
Figure 4: Accident scenarios involving pedestrians 
[Reproduced from (Vejdirektoratet 2004)] 
3.3.1.2 
Person file 
The person file comprises all information related to the characteristics of the victims 
involved in the accident. Thus, it indicates the role of the victim (driver, pedestrian, 
front/back seat passenger), the consumption of alcohol or drugs, age and sex, and most 
importantly the severity level sustained by the victim, classified in four categories (no injury, 
light injury, severe injury and fatality). Fatality is defined as a death within 30 days after the 
accident, while severe and light injuries are distinguished based on the presence of 
incapacitating injuries. Lastly, the no injury state describes accidents only leading to material 
damages. 
 
3.3.1.3 
Unit file 
The unit file collects all information related to the vehicle involved and its behaviour at the 
moment of the accident. As a consequence, the vehicle type, the presence of obstacles or 
the type of road used by the vehicle are described. Moreover, extra information is provided 
when the accident involves pedestrians or cyclists, such as the use of cycle track or 
sidewalks. Lastly, manoeuvres and duty to give way for the considered element are 
provided.  
==PAGE:40==
40 
 
3.3.1.4 
Summary 
The Danish Road Accident registration tends to covers all aspects of the accident with its 
extensive data collection scheme as shown in Table 1. This completeness however comes 
at a cost, which is the complexity and the time consumption needed to accurately fill the 
form for each accident. Indeed, the data quality relies on the capacity of the police to 
collect all requested elements of information. As accidents are likely to constitute an 
emergency and stressful situation for the policemen, it can be inferred that the data 
collection may be considered as a secondary task compared to the assistance to the 
victims. Therefore, accidents reports may be subject to errors and missing information.

3.3.2 
NAVTEQ  
 
The road characteristics provided in the police records from the Danish Road Accident 
Registration are found to be missing quite often or quite unreliable. As these characteristics 
may have a great influence on the accidents, it is highly important to find a reliable 
==PAGE:41==
41 
 
alternative to the police records. In order to cope with this issue, the NAVTEQ GPS road 
network layer for Denmark is employed. 
NAVTEQ was an American company dedicated to the provision of geographic information 
system data, as well as a major provider of GPS units all over the world. Bought in 2011 by 
Nokia, it is now part of Nokia Here (HERE 2016). The NAVTEQ road network dataset used 
in the present thesis corresponds to the 2008 version for Denmark, and covers all roads of 
the country, from the small paths to the largest motorways with a great accuracy. The road 
type (motorway, regional, local road), the number of lanes per direction, the road type 
(one/two-way road), the urban/rural and paved/unpaved characteristics of the road can thus 
be retrieved thanks to NAVTEQ data. Moreover, several layers provide extra information 
related to POI (Points Of Interests) all over Denmark, such as locations of restaurants, 
schools or hospitals as shown in Figure 5. 
 
 
 
Figure 5: NAVTEQ dataset  
 
 
In this thesis, the NAVTEQ dataset is used to retrieve accurate road characteristics. 
Moreover, the location of the hospitals provided by NAVTEQ is used to compute the 
distance from every accident to the nearest hospital. 
 
==PAGE:42==
42 
 
3.3.3 
Danish National Transport Model 
 
The Danish Road Accident Registration constitutes the primary source of information related 
to the accidents in Denmark. However, no information is provided regarding the traffic 
volume on the road considered, nor information related to the socio-economic 
characteristics of the areas in the vicinity of the accidents. In this regard, the Danish 
National Transport Model constitutes a helpful and complementary source of data to retrieve 
these elements of information. 
The Danish National Transport Model (NTM) is a traffic model developed at DTU, aiming at 
improving the decision making for planning and investments in transport in Denmark. The 
model allows investigating how the development of demography and economics affect 
transport volumes, as well as transfers between modes of transportation. It is also able to 
model and forecast the traffic for roads, airports, trains, public transports and goods for all 
Denmark, with a projection of traffic until 2030 (Transport DTU 2016b). Initiated in 2009, 
the model is still under development, with the release of version 1.1 in January 2016, and 
the expected release of version 2.0 in 2017. 
In order to forecast the transport demand all over Denmark, the model has to build on 
extensive road, rail, public transport, maritime and aerial networks, as well as socio-
economic data as shown in Figure 6. As a consequence, it gathers data from the Danish 
Transport and Construction Agency, the Road Directorate and Rail Net Denmark for 
infrastructure conditions and construction plans, planning assumptions (where people live), 
as well as economic and price conditions from the Danish Ministry of Finance for GDP and 
jobs forecasts. Lastly, data from Statistics Denmark are used for projections of population 
and its spatial distribution and forecasts of driving costs in terms of fuel prices and taxes. 
 
 
Figure 6: NTM general structure 
[Reproduced from (Transport DTU 2016b)] 
==PAGE:43==
43 
 
 
3.3.3.1 
Road network 
The infrastructure network constitutes a key element of the NTM. The NTM road network is 
built on a subset of the NAVTEQ network, with increased details for the characteristics 
compared to the NAVTEQ data, such as a more precise classification of road type, as well 
as the definition of road capacity. The road network allows forecasting the traffic for a 
given scenario on Danish roads (year of projection, road projects considered), by assigning 
them an AADT value (Annual Average Daily Traffic) which corresponds to the daily average 
number of vehicles crossing a given stretch of the network.  However, because of the 
nature of the NTM model, it would be impossible to assign traffic on the whole road 
network in Denmark (for instance on small paths). Thus, this led to the development of 3 
levels of accuracy which are employed to model traffic changes at different scales (country-
wide of more locally). In this thesis, the most detailed road network of the NTM, the level 3, 
is used. It covers more than 55 000 road links all over Denmark as shown in Figure 7. All 
the links are assigned average daily traffic volumes (cars and trucks), the non-covered road 
network being primarily small local roads with low traffic volumes. 
 
 
 
Figure 7: NTM road network (level 3) 
 
==PAGE:44==
44 
 
3.3.3.2 
Public transport network 
The public transport network for the NTM contains all stops for the whole country, 
including bus, metro, S-train, trains and ferry stops. For the purpose of the thesis, only road 
(bus) and rail (metro, S-train, train) stops are relevant, representing about 22 000 stops as 
shown in Figure 8. Moreover, the NTM assigns daily boarding and alighting volumes at 
major stops in Denmark, covering about 4 500 stops. 
 
Figure 8: NTM public transport stops network 
 
 
 
3.3.3.3 
Socio-economic characteristics 
The NTM lastly builds the traffic flows based on socio-economic characteristics of the 
population. For this purpose, the country is divided in 907 zones, as shown in Figure 9. A 
base population synthesis for 2010 has been established based on concatenation of data 
from Statistics Denmark and the Danish Ministry of Finance. It has led to the establishment 
of a base matrix splitting the population in socio-economic groups, consisting of prototypical 
individuals for each zone and characterized by age, gender, income, activity (student, 
working, unemployed, retired, etc.) and children and family status. 
==PAGE:45==
45 
 
 
Figure 9: NTM zones 
 
3.3.3.4 
Summary 
The NTM model can thus provide a wide spectrum of information related to the road 
characteristics, traffic flows on the road and public transports, as well as socio-economic 
characteristics of the population, collected at a rather disaggregated spatial level. Lastly, the 
model relies on very reliable external sources of data, such as ministries of Denmark, official 
statistical organisation and infrastructure planners. 
 
3.3.4 
Statistics Denmark 
 
Statistics Denmark is a state institution under the responsibility of the Danish Ministry of 
Social Affairs and the Interior. Its main role is to provide impartial statistics on society as a 
basis for democracy and the economy (Danmarks Statistik 2016a). Statistics Denmark thus 
collects and publishes a wide spectrum of data, being made available to the public through 
its website.  
With regard to the present thesis, one would be interested in data regarding socio-economic 
characteristics of the population that are already gathered by the NTM. However, one 
important element of information is missing from the NTM, namely the car ownership. 
Statistics Denmark allows to fill this gap through the release of car ownership data at 
==PAGE:46==
46 
 
municipality level. In this regard, and in order to comply with the time representativeness of 
data gathered through the NTM, the car ownership for the 98 Danish municipalities for the 
year 2010 is collected, as shown in Figure 10. 
 
 
Figure 10: Statistics Denmark car ownership data 
[Reproduced from (Danmarks Statistik 2016a)] 
 
3.3.5 
Central Business Register 
 
The Danish Central Business Register, in Danish det Centrale Virksomhedsregister (CVR) is 
the central government register gathering primary data on all business in Denmark, 
regardless of economic and organizational structure (Det Centrale Virksomhedsregister 
2016). Thus, all job places, public administrations (police stations, schools, etc.) are 
registered in this system. Elements of information related to the number of workers as well 
as detailed working activity among 705 predefined categories are recorded for each working 
location. Transport DTU owns a geocoded version of the CVR, comprising more than 
630 000 job locations as for 2009, as shown in Figure 11. 
==PAGE:47==
47 
 
 
Figure 11: Central Business Register  
 
 
As a consequence, the CVR data can be used to retrieve information related to the 
concentration of jobs, as data is collected directly from the Danish administration, ensuring 
completeness and accuracy of information. Moreover, it can also be used to locate 
restaurants, night clubs, bars and schools, as these businesses are also recorded in the 
CVR.  
 
3.3.6 
Danish National Transport Survey 
 
The Danish National Travel Survey (TU) is an interview survey aiming at documenting the 
travel behaviour of the Danish population (Transport DTU 2016c). First initiated in 1975, it 
has been regularly run since May 2006 in its latest format: every day all year round, a 
number of Danish residents aged between 10 and 84 years are interviewed about their 
travel activities on the day before the interview. Being sampled in order to accurately 
represent the Danish population, around 12 000 persons are thus yearly interviewed in the 
scope of the TU, while elements of information related to their age, gender, income, 
education, access to transport modes and other background data are also collected. 
Several stakeholders are involved in the National Travel Survey. Transport DTU has been 
responsible of maintaining and developing the survey since 2001, on behalf of other parties 
==PAGE:48==
48 
 
such as the ministry of Transport, the Danish Road Directorate, the Danish Transport 
authorities or regions and municipalities in Denmark. 
Since 2006, interviewees are asked to answer a questionnaire covering 5 main items: 
 
How much: the distance travelled for each trip; 
 
How: which modes of transportation have been used; 
 
Where: the origin and destination location of the trips; 
 
When: the time period of the trips (morning peak, evening peak, etc.); 
 
Why: the purpose of the trips (business, leisure, etc.). 
As a consequence, the TU constitutes a unique source of data regarding the travel patterns 
of the Danish population, both in terms of time coverage and modes of transportation 
covered. It especially provides information related to foot and bike trips, which are usually 
left out of other travel behaviour studies. Moreover, the National Transport Survey is closely 
linked with the National Transport Model, and constitutes a source of data for the latter. In 
turn, locations of trips in the TU are matched with the 907 NTM zones presented in section 
3.3.3.3. 
The National Transport Survey is thus used in this thesis to gather data related to the walk 
traffic volumes within the 907 NTM zones covering the whole country, with a dataset 
covering the period May 2006 – December 2015 and comprising around 105 500 trips 
involving a walking activity reported by the interviewees. The study of this data is of great 
importance, as different patterns regarding the walking habits of Danes can be observed 
based on their residence location. For instance, the population density seems to have a 
great influence on the average daily distance walked by Danes, as suggested in Table 2.
