3.3.7 
Danish Ministry of Environment and Food 
 
In 2007, the Danish Ministry of Environment and Food (previously known as the Danish 
Ministry of Food, Agriculture and Fisheries) launched a rural development program for 
Denmark (European Commission 2016b), which has been renewed for the period 2014 – 
2020 (European Commission 2016a). In agreement with the European Commission, the 
Danish Rural Development Program is an implementation of the EU regulation on support for 
the development of rural areas, and aims at regulating and promoting sustainable 
development in rural area as a supplement to market policy through the European program 
LEADER (European Commission 2016c). Indeed, the Danish government has a goal to 
ensure good and equal conditions of life no matter where in the country people are settled. 
Therefore, certain parts of the rural development program target geographical areas where a 
total evaluation shows the largest need for support in order to ensure a positive 
development. For the period 2007 – 2013, four pillars of the program were identified: 
 
Providing a greater competitiveness in the agricultural, food and forestry sectors; 
 
Creating attractive living conditions in rural areas, which contributes to strong 
cohesiveness between countryside and town; 
 
Attracting more entrepreneurs and local jobs in rural areas; 
 
Ensuring varied landscapes, rich nature and a clean environment. 
In order to identify areas requiring the more help to ensure equality over Denmark, the 
ministry has developed a rural index for the 98 municipalities of Denmark, taking into 
account socio-economic and environmental factors to classify the municipalities in 4 
categories (urban, intermediate, rural and peripheral) as shown in Figure 12. 
 
Figure 12: Danish Ministry of Environment and Food rural index 
[Reproduced from (Danish Ministry of Food Agriculture and Fisheries 2011)] 
==PAGE:50==
50 
 
Introducing this indicator in the thesis is particularly relevant. Indeed, the rural development 
may be closely linked to the road usage habits (travel by bike, by car, by foot), job and 
public transport access, thus having a great influence of the traffic and share of transport 
modes in the municipalities. It also captures socio-economic characteristics of the Danish 
population that are not investigated in the NTM. 
 
3.3.8 
European Environmental Agency 
 
The European Environmental Agency is a European Union agency aiming at providing sound, 
independent information on the environment. It constitutes a major information source for 
stakeholders in the development, adoption, implementation and evaluation of environmental 
policy (European Environment Agency 2016b). Established in 1994 in Copenhagen, its 
mandate is: 
 
To help the Community and member countries make informed decisions about 
improving the environment, integrating environmental considerations into economic 
policies and moving towards sustainability; 
 
To coordinate the European environment information and observation network 
(Eionet). 
The CORINE program (Coordination of information on the environment) launched in 1985 
constitutes a database of land use cover for the whole Europe. Several versions of the 
datasets have been issued and made available to the public. Among them, two data sources 
are found relevant for the present thesis: 
 
A population grid layer: derived from the CORINE data layer and Eurostat population 
census. It constitutes a population density layer in 1 ha resolution  from 2000 for all 
Europe (Gallego 2010); 
 
CORINE land use cover: from 2012, characterising the land use type in 44 classes 
(from urban fabric to wetland) for all Europe in 1 ha resolution (European 
Environment Agency 2016a), as shown in Figure 13. 
==PAGE:51==
51 
 
 
Figure 13: CORINE land use 
(The legend of the 44 land use classes has voluntarily been left out) 
 
Thus, the datasets from the European Environmental Agency are used to retrieve 
information related to land use and population density.

3.4 Data format 
 
The variables related to pedestrian accidents obtained from the different data sources can 
take various forms. Indeed, one can for instance think of the gender of the pedestrian, 
which is a categorical variable, while the level of alcohol intoxication sustained by the 
pedestrian is continuous. On top of this distinction, it can be noted that variables may also 
cover different levels of aggregation: while the road characteristics are specific to the 
accident, other variables, such as income level or car ownership, are measured at a zonal 
level. The following paragraphs aim at explaining the different types of variables and 
aggregation levels that are employed in this thesis. 
 
3.4.1 
Variable types 
 
As mentioned previously, different types of variables can be encountered in modelling, and 
are summarised in Table 4.  
Most variables related to pedestrian accidents are categorical, indicating that these variables 
can only take a finite number of values (gender of pedestrian, type of road, weather, etc.). 
==PAGE:53==
53 
 
This kind of variable can be further split in two sub-types, namely nominal and ordinal 
variables. Nominal relates to variables whose categories cannot be compared between 
them. This is for instance the case for the type of buildings along the road at the location of 
the accident, for which no direct comparison among categories can be drawn. In the 
contrary, ordinal variables imply a possible comparison among categories. For instance, the 
level of injury sustained by the pedestrian is ordinal, as one can obviously compare and rank 
no injury, light injury, severe injury and fatality.  
The second type of variable that can be encountered is continuous. Related to pedestrian 
accidents, one can for instance think of socio-economic variables, such as income level, car 
ownership level or exposure, such as road traffic volume. Interval describes variables for 
which the measurement of the difference of two values is meaningful. Ratio is composed of 
variables having the properties of interval variables, with the additional characteristic that 
the value 0 indicates an absence of effect of this variable (for instance blood alcohol 
concentration). 
 
Variable type 
Description 
Example 
Categorical 
(discrete) 
Nominal 
Variables with two or more 
categories, without intrinsic order 
Vehicle type 
Ordinal

which can be ranked

Table 4: Types of variables 
 
Lastly, many variables such as socio-economic or land use characteristics are spatial data. 
Thus, it requires the use of a GIS software as well as geo-location of accidents to collect 
these elements of information. In the present thesis, the ArcGIS software is employed. 
 
3.4.2 
Normalisation 
 
When it comes to aggregated data such as socio-economic and environmental variables 
(population, traffic flows, etc.), different types of normalisation can be encountered to make 
measures comparable. For instance, variables can be normalised by dividing them by the 
population, the size of the area considered or by the time interval examined. 
 
==PAGE:54==
54 
 
3.4.3 
Aggregation levels 
 
The variables retrieved in this thesis may have different geographical coverage, as indicated 
in the literature review (section 2.2.3.2). For instance, data collected from the NTM 
regarding socio-economic characteristics of the population is gathered at zonal level, with 
the 907 zones as previously defined in section 3.3.3.3, while the rurality index is collected 
at the municipality level. 
As a consequence, a clear definition of the different levels of aggregation that are employed 
in this thesis is to be established. It is important to keep the most disaggregate level of 
information that one can get, as a higher aggregation level may lead to a loss of 
information.  
 
3.4.3.1 
Accident level 
The accident level is the most disaggregate level of information that one is able to retrieve. 
Crash data, road characteristics are to be retrieved at this level. 
 
3.4.3.2 
500 m buffers 
Buffers are used in several studies to capture socio-economic and environmental 
characteristics. For instance, (Moudon et al. 2011) define a 500 m buffer around the 
accident in order to capture socio-economic characteristics of the environment, while 
(Miranda-Moreno et al. 2011) test the influence of the buffer size (50, 150, 400 and 600 
m) on the outcomes of the models. 
The choice of the buffer size must be driven by a reasonable choice. In the case of 
pedestrian accidents, it makes sense to match the size of the buffer with the average length 
of a walking trip. Indeed, it would ensure that the buffer covers the whole area that the 
pedestrian has potentially been through before he/she had his/her accident. 
In this regard, a result from the Danish National Travel Survey published in 2013 indicates 
that 89 % of the walking trips in Denmark do not exceed 500 m of distance (Transport DTU 
2016a). As a consequence, a 500 m buffer would be representative of most walking trips in 
Denmark. 
Buffers can then be defined in two different ways in the GIS software, both definitions 
being used in road accident studies. On the one hand, buffers may be defined as aerial, thus 
only defining a circle around the accident, and not considering any environment obstacles 
(buildings, roads, etc.). On the other hand, network-based buffers are characterised by the 
area reachable from a centre point, taking into account the environment constraints (roads, 
buildings, etc.). The example shown in Figure 14 clarifies the difference between these two 
types of buffers. With the aerial buffer, the pedestrian is located within 500 m of the 
==PAGE:55==
55 
 
school. However, it is not feasible to effectively reach the school in less than 500 m 
because of the road network layout; from the network-based point of view, the school lies 
outside the buffer. Network-based buffers are thus more suitable for the definitions of areas 
reachable for a pedestrian by using the road network, while aerial ones are suitable to 
capture land use characteristics, for which the road network does not matter. 
 
Figure 14: Aerial and network-based buffer 
 
The aerial buffers are easily created thanks to the Buffer tool implemented in ArcGIS. The 
network buffers however require some more work: using the NAVTEQ road dataset as 
input, and requires the use of the Make Service Area Layer tool to compute the area 
covered within 500 m from each accident by using the road network. In order to provide 
accurate results, the NAVTEQ network constraints are adapted for the purpose of 
pedestrian use of the roads: indeed, pedestrians can walk in opposite direction of one-way 
roads, while they should not be able to use regulated roads such as motorways.  
 
3.4.3.3 
National Transport Model zones 
As mentioned in section 3.3.3.3, the National Transport Model is based on the division of 
Denmark in 907 zones. As a consequence, all elements of information related to socio-
economic characteristics of the population, but also road and pedestrian traffic flows (from 
the National Travel Survey) are gathered at this aggregation level. 
 
3.4.3.4 
Municipalities 
Denmark is divided in 98 municipalities. As indicated in section 3.3.4 and 3.3.7, car 
ownership and rurality index are gathered at this aggregation level. 
School
Road
Buffer, aerial
Buffer, network-based
==PAGE:56==
56

3.5 Data acquisition 
 
The following paragraphs aim at describing the different steps that have been followed to 
collect data, as well as the definitions of the variables employed.  
3.5.1 
Accident data 
 
Data was directly extracted from the database maintained by the Danish Road Directorate. 
All pedestrian accidents between January 2006 and December 2015 were considered. 
The three files containing the data regarding the crash, the persons involved as well as the 
units involved (i.e. the vehicles) were matched thanks to a unique crash ID given to each 
accident. 
Following this identification, four tasks were carried out: 
 
Converting the data into useable form: indeed, all data was stored in text format 
with Danish abbreviations, thus being impossible to be used. However, a converting 
table, provided by the Danish Road Directorate, allowed converting the data into 
numerical form, with a clear definition for each variable as shown in Table 6; 
LYS 
Daylight condition 
Input

Description (DK) Description (EN)

Not stated 
Table 6: Accident data adaptation 
 
 
Some variables required a reduction in the number of possible outcomes. For 
instance, 38 types of vehicle were proposed by the Danish Road Directorate. Such a 
large number of categories was however not suitable for data analysis, as it would 
have led to really small samples within the different categories; 
 
Only relevant and reliable were kept. Indeed, variables such as the direction of travel 
of the vehicle (North, East, etc) would not have added relevant information related to 
the accident, and were therefore left out. In a similar manner, variables only sparsely 
filled in the records were discarded. 
 
Lastly, the dataset was cleaned, in order to only keep complete records for the 
selected variables. 
After these different steps, 9283 complete accident records were identified. Some records 
were found involving several pedestrians or vehicles. Moreover, hit-and-run accidents (i.e. 
with drivers leaving the accident scene) were also found, for which very little data regarding 
==PAGE:58==
58 
 
the driver was recorded (only the vehicle type and the action of the vehicle at the moment 
of the crash were captured). The records were grouped as follows:

3.5.2 
Accident geo-location 
 
The 9283 accident records retrieved from the Danish Road Directorate database were all 
geo-located and could therefore be matched with other spatial data. This step was indeed 
necessary in order to be able to later retrieve data regarding road characteristics, land use 
and socio-economic factors. 
3.5.2.1 
Road network matching 
As stated in section 3.3.2, the road characteristics captured in the Danish Road Accident 
Registration system were not really reliable and detailed. Therefore, the accidents were 
matched to two external sources of information regarding the road characteristics: 
 
The NAVTEQ road network; 
 
The NTM level 3 road network. 
By superposing the accidents and the two different road networks as shown in Figure 16, 
several remarks were drawn: 
 
The NAVTEQ network was much more detailed in terms of road network coverage 
than the NTM road dataset. Therefore, an accident occurring on a major road was 
likely to be matched on the NTM network, while an accident on a small rural road 
could only be matched on the NAVTEQ network; 
 
The two networks only represented the roads by their centreline, thus leaving out 
their physical width. In turn, accidents occurring on the verge of the roads were not 
precisely located on the centrelines but offset by few meters, and thus required a 
tolerance to match them to the road network; 
 
Lastly, some accidents were simply not located on any road link, and could therefore 
not be matched. 
==PAGE:59==
59 
 
 
Figure 16: Accident geo-location 
 
Based on these remarks, two choices were made. First of all, the greater coverage of the 
NAVTEQ dataset could have been a reason to only match accidents with this road network. 
However, this dataset contained far less information compared to the NTM, which could 
have led to a great loss of data. Therefore, it was decided to keep the two datasets for road 
matching. 
Secondly, thresholds had to be defined in order to accurately match accidents to the road 
network. A balance had to be found between too loose thresholds which could have led to 
an important risk of matching accidents to a wrong road link, and too narrow thresholds 
greatly reducing the number of identified accidents as shown in Figure 17.  
 
Figure 17: Road matching cumulative error distribution 
==PAGE:60==
60 
 
Indeed, thresholds tighter than 10 m could have made the share of matched accidents fall 
below 80 %, while cut-offs over 50 m could have led to the matching of almost 100 % of 
the crashes. A last consideration helped finding a good definition of these thresholds by 
looking at the road type of the link and its associated width. Indeed, the cross-sections of 
the different road types in Denmark supplied by the Danish Road Directorate (Vejdirektoratet 
2016b) revealed that a 3-lanes motorway had a width of 35 m, compared to only 5.5 m for 
a small one-way road as shown in Figure 18, indicating an important variation of the road 
width depending on its type. 
 
 
Figure 18: Danish road widths (motorway / one-way road) 
[Reproduced from (Vejdirektoratet 2016b)] 
 
 
All these considerations led to the definition of thresholds as presented in Table 8, with 
tighter values in urban areas due to a greater road density.

==PAGE:61==
61 
 
Finally, the scheme retained for road matching was as follows: 
 
The Near tool was run on the NAVTEQ and NTM level 3 networks with a bandwidth 
limit of 50 m; 
 
Based on the thresholds described in Table 8, accidents were matched on the NTM 
links; 
 
The matching procedure was applied on the NAVTEQ road network for the accidents 
that were still not identified. 
With this strategy, 8315 out of 9283 accidents were correctly matched to the road 
networks, hence representing an efficiency of 89.6 %. 
 
3.5.2.2 
NTM zones and municipalities matching 
Accidents had to be matched with the NTM zones as well as the municipalities. Indeed, all 
socio-economic variables were collected at the NTM zonal level, while the rural index and 
the car ownership were collected at the municipality level. 
Using again the Near geoprocessing tools implemented in ArcGIS, accidents were directly 
matched with the NTM zones which cover the whole Denmark, and to the municipalities. 
No particular issue was encountered when matching the accidents with the NTM zones. 
Indeed, the relatively good precision of the GPS coordinates of the accidents, as well as a 
comprehensive coverage of the country by the 907 zones allowed matching each one of the 
9283 accidents. 
 
 
3.5.3 
Road characteristics 
 
Based on the road network matching performed in the previous section, road characteristics 
at the location of the accidents were retrieved. Additionally, characteristics of the road 
network in the vicinity of the accidents were considered, by using buffers centred on the 
crashes. 
 
3.5.3.1 
Accident level 
By combining the sources of information from NAVTEQ and the NTM, the following 
elements regarding the road characteristics at the accident location were retrieved: 
 
Urban/rural road; 
 
Speed limit; 
 
Number of lanes per direction; 
==PAGE:62==
62 
 
 
Paved/unpaved road; 
 
Road category (motorway, national road, regional road, major/medium/small local 
road); 
 
One-way/two-way undivided road/two-way divided road; 
 
Accident in/outside intersection. 
 
3.5.3.2 
Aerial buffer level 
Road characteristics in the surroundings of the accidents could provide meaningful 
information which may have influenced the severity of accidents, such as the presence of 
traffic calming roads in the vicinity of crashes.  
As these attributes could be seen as land use characteristics, it was decided to use aerial 
buffers as suggested in section 3.4.3.2 with a radius of 500 m. Based on the findings of 
the literature review, the following variables were collected by running the Near tool in 
ArcGIS: 
 
Density of road network (km/km²), accounting for the extension of the road network 
around the accident which could be linked to the exposure of the pedestrian to the 
road traffic, as shown in Figure 19; 
 
Share of low speed road (≤30 km/h), indicating the presence of traffic calming areas 
around the accident; 
 
Share of high speed road (≥  70 km/h), showing the presence of fast traffic roads 
around the accident; 
 
Number of intersections, being used as a measure of exposure of the pedestrian to 
the road traffic. Indeed, the more intersections, the more the pedestrian was 
exposed to the road traffic by crossing streets; 
 
Number of pedestrian accidents, revealing the concentration of accidents in space 
(hotspots). 
 
==PAGE:63==
63 
 
 
Figure 19: Road density within 500 m of the accident 
 
 
3.5.4 
Distance to hospital 
 
Very few studies had considered the influence of the distance from an accident to the 
nearest hospital on its severity level, while (Prato et al. 2013) claimed that this factor had a 
significant effect on the level of severity of rural road accidents. Therefore, investigating the 
influence of this parameter on pedestrian crashes severity has been found relevant and 
constituted a novelty. 
Using the NAVTEQ road dataset, the network distance between each of the 9283 accidents 
and the nearest hospital among the 114 emergency facilities in Denmark has been 
computed. For that purpose, the Make Closest Facility Layer tool implemented in ArcGIS 
has been used: given the set of accidents as starting points and the set of hospitals as 
ending points, the tool computed the shortest route from every accident to the nearest 
hospital through the NAVTEQ network. Hence, all constraints such as one-way roads, 
forbidden roads for certain vehicle types were taken into account as shown in Figure 20. 
 
==PAGE:64==
64 
 
 
Figure 20: Route to hospital 
 
 
3.5.5 
Socio-economic characteristics 
 
The multiple sources of data available for this thesis allowed retrieving several 
characteristics of the population at the NTM zone level: 
 
Population density; 
 
Job density; 
 
Blend of population in terms of age; 
 
Blend of population in terms of gender; 
 
Blend of population in terms of socio-economic categories; 
 
Car ownership; 
 
Unemployment rate; 
 
GDP within the zones; 
 
Mean income. 
 
 
3.5.5.1 
Population density 
The population density has been directly computed from the NTM data. Indeed, each one of 
the 907 zones are attributed a number of inhabitants. In order to compare zones of different 
sizes, the population figures were normalised by dividing them by the area (km²) of the 
zones, as shown in Figure 21. Population density can indeed be seen as a measure  
==PAGE:65==
65 
 
 
Figure 21: Population density 
 
 
of the potential exposure of pedestrian to road traffic, as more populated areas are expected 
to be associated with more traffic. 
 
3.5.5.2 
Job density 
The job density could be seen a measure of the pedestrian exposure to road traffic for 
certain periods of the day: indeed, workers were expected to commute in the morning and 
the evening. As a consequence, a relation could exist between pedestrian accidents 
occurring at peak hours and workers commuting.  
The job density was retrieved thanks to the data from the Central Business register. Each 
geo-located Danish workplace was linked to one of the 907 NTM zones by using the Near 
tool in ArcGIS. Thereafter, the numbers of employees provided by the Central Business 
Register for every workplace were summed within the zones, and normalised by dividing by 
the size of the zone (km²), as shown in Figure 22. 
==PAGE:66==
66 
 
 
Figure 22: Job density 
 
 
3.5.5.3 
Blend of population in terms of age and gender 
The population characteristics in terms of age and gender could also provide meaningful 
insights. Indeed, males were often associated with riskier behaviours than women, therefore 
leading to an over-exposure to road accidents.  In a similar manner, young people and 
elderly were usually seen as exposed populations and more prone to be involved in road 
accidents, as demonstrated by several road safety studies. Therefore, these characteristics 
of the population seemed to be relevant as explanatory factors for pedestrian road 
accidents. 
The shares of young people below 18 years and elderly above 65 years were computed for 
each one of the 907 zones by using the prototypical population matrix developed for the 
NTM, and representing the Danish population as for 2010. On average, young people 
accounted for 23 % of the population, while elderly accounted for 15 %. In both categories, 
a standard deviation of 5% was observed within the zones, thus defining the thresholds 
shown in Figure 23. 
The shares of males in each of the NTM zone were computed in a similar way. 
 
==PAGE:67==
67 
 
 
 
Figure 23: Shares of young and elderly population 
 
 
3.5.5.4 
Socio-economic categories 
The prototypical population matrix proposed by the NTM allowed splitting the population in 
6 different categories, which followed Statistics Denmark rules for categorising the 
population: 
 
Fully employed; 
 
Part-time employed; 
 
Students, pupils; 
 
Retired; 
 
Unemployed, job seeking; 
 
Unemployed, not job seeking. 
These different categories provided an insight in the composition of the population, which 
could have influenced the characteristics of the accidents. Indeed, a high representation of 
pupils in the zones could have been linked to an increased risk of school accidents, while 
the presence of employees could have been associated with commuting accidents. 
 
3.5.5.5 
Car ownership 
Car ownership was collected thanks to data provided by Statistics Denmark at the 
municipality level. The variable considered was the share of households without a car in the 
municipality, as shown in Figure 24. 
==PAGE:68==
68 
 
 
Figure 24: Car ownership 
 
 
3.5.5.6 
Unemployment rate 
According to Eurostat, the official definition of unemployment rate was the share of people 
among the labour force who were (Eurostat 2016): 
 
Aged between 15 and 74 years; 
 
Without work during the reference week; 
 
Available to start work within the next two weeks; 
 
Actively having sought employment at some time during the last four weeks. 
With this definition, and given the socio-economic categories as defined in section 3.5.5.4, 
the unemployment rate was computed as follows:

3.5.5.7 
GDP and mean income 
 
 
Figure 26: Mean income 
==PAGE:70==
70 
 
Lastly, the wealth of the zone was measured in terms of GDP and mean income in the 
zones. Indeed, some studies showed that deprived areas were more exposed to pedestrian 
road accident, hence demonstrating the importance of these factors on the accidents. 
The GDP and mean income values were directly provided as inputs from the NTM. The 
mean income was computed as the mean annual gross income for households in the 
considered zone, as shown in Figure 26. 
 
3.5.6 
Rural index 
 
As mentioned in the presentation of data sources, the Danish Ministry of Food, Agriculture 
and Fisheries developed an indicator to measure the development of rural Danish areas, in 
order to provide measures to ensure equality all over Denmark. 
The usual definition of rural areas according to the OECD was a geographical area with a 
population density of less than 150 inhabitants per km². However, the Danish Ministry of 
Food, Agriculture and Fisheries, together with the Danish Institute of Agricultural Sciences, 
decided to establish a classification system showing the rural degree for each of the 98 
Danish municipalities in a more detailed way. In order to achieve these goals, the 
municipalities’ degrees of rurality were estimated based on 14 indicators covering socio-
economic, demographic and urbanisation factors (Danish Ministry of Food Agriculture and 
Fisheries 2011): 
 
Population per km²; 
 
Population in rural areas and towns with less than 1 000 inhabitants; 
 
Proportion of the municipality’s area in rural zones; 
 
Percentage employed in agricultural enterprises; 
 
Percentage of the population aged 17 – 24  years; 
 
Percentage of the population aged 25 – 44  years; 
 
Employment trends, 1994 – 2004; 
 
Population trends, 1994 – 2004; 
 
Average distance to a motorway; 
 
Jobs in proportion to employees (dependence on commuting); 
 
Percentage of workforce with basic schooling, 2005; 
 
Percentage of the workforce with medium cycle or tertiary education, 2005; 
 
Average distance to areas with a high surplus of jobs, 2004; 
 
Taxation base per capita, 2007. 
It thus gave a considerably more subtle indication of where the need for strengthening the 
development in the rural areas was the largest (Danish Ministry of Food Agriculture and 
Fisheries 2011). 
==PAGE:71==
71 
 
 
Figure 27: Municipality rurality index 
 
 
Based on an equal weighing scheme of the former indicators, the municipalities were 
divided into four different classes defining the “rural development scale” as shown in Figure 
27: 
 
Urban municipalities; 
 
Intermediate municipalities; 
 
Rural municipalities; 
 
Peripheral municipalities. 
 
3.5.7 
Presence of specific facilities 
 
A significant number of road safety studies investigated the role played by the presence of 
specific facilities near pedestrian accidents, such as schools or alcohol outlets, with 
significant results. Therefore, investigating the influence of these facilities on the accident 
patterns was relevant. 
 
3.5.7.1 
Schools 
Schools could be directly linked with accidents involving young people. Two variables were 
proposed: 
==PAGE:72==
72 
 
 
Figure 28: Presence of school within 500 m of the accidents 
 
 
 
A variable indicating whether the accident occurred in front of a school. For this 
purpose, aerial buffers of 50 m around schools were used; 
 
A variable indicating whether the accident may be linked with children commuting 
from/to school. As a consequence, the 500 m network-wise buffers were 
considered around schools, as shown in Figure 28. 
For this purpose, the Company Business Register was used to locate all primary schools in 
Denmark. 
 
3.5.7.2 
Retails 
The presence of retails near accidents could be an indicator of exposure. Indeed, shopping 
areas were likely to attract a large volume of pedestrians, hence increasing their exposure to 
the road traffic. Thus, the number of retails within 500 m network-wise of the accident was 
computed. 
The retails considered were extracted from the Company Business Register, and comprised 
the following types of shops: detail shops, bakeries, discount supermarkets, supermarkets, 
fisheries, butchers, fruit seller, kiosks, service stations and tobacco shops.  
 
==PAGE:73==
73 
 
3.5.7.3 
Leisure facilities 
Some studies revealed the influence of the presence alcohol outlets on the risk and severity 
of alcohol-related accidents nearby, especially during weekends. 
In order to account for this effect, the number of facilities within 500 m network-wise of 
the accident, which could be linked with alcohol consumption or weekend leisure time was 
computed: restaurants, cafés, bars, theatres, cinemas and night clubs, as shown in Figure 
29. In the same way as for the retail shops, data was retrieved thanks to the Company 
Business Register data. 
 
 
Figure 29: Presence of leisure facilities within 500 m of the accident 
 
3.5.7.4 
Local population and job density 
Even though the population and job densities were investigated at the NTM zone level, 
spatial dissimilarities could be encountered within the largest NTM zones. In order to 
account for this issue, population and job densities were also collected at a narrower scale, 
namely 500 m around the accident (aerial). For this purpose, the population grid provided by 
the European Environmental Agency as well as the Company Business Register data were 
employed. 
 
==PAGE:74==
74 
 
3.5.7.5 
Low volume rural roads 
Low volume rural roads were considered as suggested by (Prato et al. 2013). Indeed, these 
roads could hide specific types of pedestrian accidents, associated with high speed and 
pedestrian walking on the road. For this reason, a variable indicating whether the crash 
occurred on a low volume rural road was created, as shown in Figure 30. This road 
category was defined in the exact same way as for (Prato et al. 2013): 
 
The road was categorised as rural; 
 
It belonged to the NTM network and was assigned an AADT lower than 2 000 
vehicles/day; 
 
Or it did not belong to the NTM network but had the lowest functional class as 
defined in the NAVTEQ network. 
 
Figure 30: Accidents on low volume rural road 
 
3.5.8 
Road traffic volumes 
 
Road traffic volume was an important measure of the pedestrian’s risk exposure. As 
mentioned in the literature review, it has been widely studied in road safety, and therefore 
was identified as a factor to include when studying traffic accidents. 
As mentioned in the source description, the NTM assigned AADT values on all road links 
included in the model. Therefore, we would have been able to use the outputs of this model 
==PAGE:75==
75 
 
to estimate the pedestrian exposure to the road traffic by a simple road matching procedure. 
However, this proposal was in fact unpractical. Indeed, the NTM road network did not fully 
cover the whole road network of Denmark, and especially missed out small roads. 
Therefore, many accidents occurring on local roads did not have traffic assigned. For this 
reason, it was needed to move towards a more aggregated level to capture the traffic 
flows.  
Due to the structure of the NTM model, the most logical level of aggregation for traffic 
flows was the NTM zone. Indeed, the model was built to forecast traffic at this unit level, 
due to its use of Danish Travel Survey data collected at zonal level.  
By aggregating traffic at zonal level, the AADT unit (i.e. number of vehicle per day on a road 
link) was not meaningful anymore, as the traffic volume was considered for an area and not 
a road link. The following procedure was then defined to collect the traffic counts at NTM 
zonal level: 
 
Using the Near tool in ArcGIS, every NTM road link was attached to one of the 907 
NTM zones; 
 
For each road link, the traffic was estimated by multiplying its AADT to its length, 
hence leading to 𝑣𝑒ℎ𝑖𝑐𝑙𝑒∙𝑘𝑚/𝑑𝑎𝑦 estimates of traffic; 
 
For each zone, the traffic volume was computed by summing the 𝑣𝑒ℎ𝑖𝑐𝑙𝑒∙𝑘𝑚/𝑑𝑎𝑦 
for all links belonging to the zone; 
 
In order to account for the different sizes of the zones, as well as for the various 
road network densities, the traffic volume was normalised into traffic intensity by 
dividing the 𝑣𝑒ℎ𝑖𝑐𝑙𝑒∙𝑘𝑚/𝑑𝑎𝑦  by the area of the zone. Thus, the traffic intensity for 
each zone was expressed in 𝑣𝑒ℎ𝑖𝑐𝑙𝑒∙𝑘𝑚/𝑑𝑎𝑦/𝑘𝑚² as shown in Figure 31, which 
allowed having comparable measures of traffic between zones. 
 
Lastly, the outputs of the NTM considered separate AADT values for the whole road traffic, 
cars and trucks. Therefore, the three outputs were used to estimate total, car and truck 
traffic intensity for each NTM zone by following the procedure previously mentioned. 
 
==PAGE:76==
76 
 
 
Figure 31: Road traffic intensity 
 
 
3.5.9 
Walk traffic volumes 
 
The walk traffic volume constituted an important measure of the pedestrian’s exposure. 
However, it had been barely considered in previous road safety studies, due to the little data 
available around the world regarding these figures. 
In Denmark, the Danish National Travel Survey (TU) constitutes a milestone in the 
estimation of walk traffic volumes. Indeed, by conducting an extensive survey about the 
travel habits in Denmark for every mode of transportation, including walking, the TU allows 
estimating the travel patterns of Danes, both in terms of distance or localisation of walk 
trips. 
Thus, by using these inputs, a comparable measure to road traffic volumes was 
constructed: the walk traffic volume intensity, collected at the NTM zone level and 
expressed in 𝑤𝑎𝑙𝑘∙𝑘𝑚/𝑑𝑎𝑦/𝑘𝑚². 
The computation of the walk density for each zone followed several steps described in the 
following paragraphs. 
 
==PAGE:77==
77 
 
3.5.9.1 
Structure of the Travel Survey 
The Travel Survey, in its current form, comprised the results of one-day interviews of Danes 
for 9 years and 8 months (May 2006 – December 2015). Each interviewed Dane had been 
asked to precisely describe his/her trips during the day before the interview, from 3am to 
3am, in terms of length, mode of transport, starting and ending point. 
The considered dataset comprised all trips for all modes of transportation collected for the 9 
years and 8 months during the interviews, and was structured as follows: 
 
Interview session: unique identification of the interviewee; 
 
Journey: entire journey from home and return to home; 
 
Trip : travel from one stay/activity to the next one; 
 
Stage: usage of one mode of transport during the trip. 
 
The stage contained information about the length of use for every mode of transportation 
used during the trip (including walking), while the trip contained information related to the 
start and ending point (municipality, nearest train station or NTM zone). As a consequence, 
a trip was decomposed in several stages as shown in Figure 32. The first task consisted in 
extracting all trips involving a walk stage. In total, 106 639 stages of trips involved 
walking. 
 
 
 
Figure 32: Link between trip and stages in the Travel Survey 
 
3.5.9.2 
Session weight 
Each interviewee was chosen among the Danish population for its representativeness of the 
overall population. Therefore, a session weight taking into account socio-economic 
characteristics was attributed to each interviewee, in order to extrapolate the daily travel 
patterns of the whole Danish population from the interviews. 
The session weights were designed in a way that, by summing one year of the TU 
interviewees ‘trips multiplied by the session weights, the number of journeys for each NTM 
zone and each mode of transportation amounted to an average daily traffic in Denmark 
(AADT). 
Walk
Car
Walk
Train
Walk
Start NTM zone

The considered Travel Survey dataset did however not cover a year of interviews, but 9 
years and 8 months instead. In order to scale the trips up to the AADT, each walk 
stage 𝑤𝑎𝑙𝑘𝑇𝑈 was then multiplied by the following factor: 
𝑤𝑎𝑙𝑘𝐴𝐴𝐷𝑇= 𝑤𝑎𝑙𝑘𝑇𝑈∙𝑠𝑒𝑠𝑠𝑖𝑜𝑛 𝑤𝑒𝑖𝑔ℎ𝑡

Eq. 2: Travel Survey scale factor 
 
3.5.9.3 
Data linking 
All stages of trips that implied walking were linked with the trip information in order to 
retrieve the start and end zones of the trips.  
The position of the walk stage within the trip was also collected, in order to know whether 
the walk stage occurred at the start, the middle or the end of the trip. 
Lastly, each trip was linked to the interviewee characteristics, allowing retrieving the 
session weight associated with it. 
 
3.5.9.4 
Origin-destination cleaning 
An important share of the trips in the Travel Survey were missing information regarding 
their start and end destination. Simply discarding them would have introduced an important 
bias in the data: therefore, a scheme was developed to tackle this issue. 
Three options were available in the trip dataset to retrieve the start and end destination, 
namely the municipality, the NTM zone or the nearest train station. The following procedure 
was used to clean the dataset for the start point and input missing data as shown in Figure 
33: 
 
If the NTM zone for the start point was indicated, the record was kept; 
 
If no information for the start point was provided, the record was discarded; 
 
If the start municipality was indicated and was identical to the end municipality, the 
record was kept. It was then assumed that the start and end NTM zones were the 
same. 
==PAGE:79==
79 
 
 
Figure 33: Walk origin imputation 
 
The same process was applied for missing end points. After this cleaning phase, only few 
records have been deleted. 
 
3.5.9.5 
Allocation of the walk stages to the NTM zones 
At this stage, all trips were linked to a start and end NTM zone. The last task consisted in 
the allocation of the walking stages within the trips to the appropriate NTM zone. Indeed, 
only the start and end locations of the trips were available; no information about the 
location of the stages within the trips was provided as shown in Figure 32. In order to 
allocate the walk stages to the correct zone, a scheme detailed in Figure 34 was proposed, 
taking into account the following parameters: 
 
The number of stages in the trip; 
 
The origin/destination NTM pair; 
 
The position of the walking stage during the trip (start, end or middle of the trip).

𝑤𝑎𝑙𝑘∙𝑘𝑚/𝑑𝑎𝑦/𝑘𝑚² as shown in Figure 35.

Due to complexity of walk data handling, the final results were checked for accuracy. The 
overall daily km walked in Denmark according to the calculations was estimated at 3 696 
713 km, which corresponded to 0.67 km/day/person in the entire Denmark. This figure 
seemed reasonable and in line with the officials calculations from Transport DTU, which 
estimated an average 0.82 km/day/person in Denmark in 2013 (Transport DTU 2016a). 
 
3.5.10 
Public transport 
 
Several road safety studies investigated the role of the public transport on pedestrian 
accidents. Indeed, public transport could be considered as a measure of the pedestrian’s 
exposure, as public transport users were most likely to walk during their trip in order to 
reach their bus or train stop. Moreover, the presence of public transport stops could 
influence the patterns of accidents. For instance, the presence of bus stop could be 
associated with pedestrian crossing in front of/behind a bus, without visibility. For all these 
reasons, two variables were created accounting for: 
 
The presence of public transports in the vicinity of the accidents; 
 
The public transport traffic flows. 
For this purpose, the National Transport Model set of bus and train stops was used, as well 
as the public transport assigned on the majors stops in Denmark. 
 
3.5.10.1 
Presence of transport stops 
Three different measures of the presence of public transports around the accidents have 
been used in the present study.  
First of all, a variable was created to indicate whether the accident occurred at a bus stop 
or not. In this regard, the Near tool was used to detect accidents occurring within 15 m of a 
bus stop. This variable thus helped to detect specific patterns of accidents, such as 
pedestrian crossing the road in front or behind the bus, without visibility. 
The influence of the public transport stops could also extended further away. For instance, 
a crowd exiting a train station could create congestion in the neighbouring roads and leading 
to an important risk of accidents. Therefore, only considering really close accidents to the 
stops could have led to an uncomplete view of the influence of the public transports.  
Two more variables were then been created to capture the influence of the public 
transports. Using the 500 m network-based buffers around the accidents, one variable 
aimed at counting the number of bus stops (including bus and boat bus stops) surrounding 
the accidents as shown in Figure 36, while a second variable aims at counting the number 
of train stops (including metro, S-trains, local, regional and national trains). 
==PAGE:82==
82 
 
 
Figure 36: Number of bus stops within 500 m of the accident 
 
 
3.5.10.2 
Public transport flows 
 
 
Figure 37: Public transport traffic intensity 
==PAGE:83==
83 
 
In a similar way as for the road and walk traffic volumes, the public transport flows were 
aggregated at the NTM zone level in order to get comparable measures regarding the 
different transport modes flows. Thus, a density of daily boarding and alighting in the zone 
was computed, and expressed in 𝑏𝑜𝑎𝑟𝑑𝑖𝑛𝑔−𝑎𝑙𝑖𝑔ℎ𝑡𝑖𝑛𝑔/𝑑𝑎𝑦/𝑘𝑚² as shown in Figure 37. 
 
3.5.11 
Land use 
 
The land use characteristics could provide meaningful information related to the immediate 
environment of the accident, and proved to be relevant in several pedestrian safety studies. 
For this reason, the land use cover CORINE was used to characterize the land use pattern 
within 500 m of the accidents. 
The CORINE land use cover comprised 44 land use types, which was far too extensive for 
the purpose of the study. Therefore, the number of categories was reduced to only 8 
meaningful ones: 
 
Continuous urban fabric; 
 
Discontinuous urban fabric; 
 
Industrial and commercial areas; 
 
Roads and railways; 
 
Green urban areas, sport and leisure facilities; 
 
Open land; 
 
Forest; 
 
Water areas (wet lands, rivers, lakes, seaside, sea, ocean). 
 
Thus, 8 different variables storing the share of land use type in the proximity of the 
accidents were created as shown in Figure 38. In order to summarize these elements of 
information into one variable, a diversity measure proposed by (Zahabi et al. 2011) was 
used, the Herfindahl-Hirschman index. This index is usually employed in economics to 
measure the concentration of a market, when different players are at stake. However, its 
definition could be easily adapted to the case of land use diversity assessment. 
The index used the shares of the 8 classes of land use previously described to unveil the 
diversity of the surroundings around the accident, according to Eq. 3: 
𝐻𝐻= ∑(𝑆ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑒𝑖∙100)2
8
𝑖=1
 
Eq. 3: Herfindahl-Hirschman index 
 
Where 𝑖∈⟦1: 8⟧ is the index of the land use type; 
 
 𝑆ℎ𝑎𝑟𝑒𝑖∈[0; 1] is the share of land use type i in the buffer around the accident. 
==PAGE:84==
84 
 
 
Figure 38: Land use within 500 m of the accident 
 
 
This index could then be normalised between 0 and 1 as shown in Eq. 4: 
𝐻𝐻𝑛𝑜𝑟𝑚𝑎𝑙𝑖𝑠𝑒𝑑=

8
 
Eq. 4: Normalised Herfindahl-Hirschman index 
 
The value 1 of the normalised index indicated that only one type of land use was present 
around the accident; on the contrary, the value 0 revealed a perfect diversity of the land 
use, with each of the 8 land use types having a share of 12.5 %. Thus, the lower the 
HHnormalised index was, the more diverse the area was, as shown in Table 9.

3.6 Summary 
 
The data collection process involved several data sources and led to the computation of 
numerous variables related to crash, land use, socio-economic characteristics as well as 
exposure factors. All the variables collected during this phase as well as their level of 
aggregation and sources are summarised in appendix (page 243). 
Following this step, all variables had to be brought together to construct the datasets. 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:86==
86 
 
 
 
==PAGE:87==
87 
 
4. Case studies 
4.1 Datasets elaboration 
 
After the retrieval of variables, the last step was to prepare the datasets. The variables have 
been selected from the complete list of variables collected (available in appendix page 243), 
based on their relevance and reliability. Variables missing too often, as well as variables 
overlapping with other ones have been discarded. 
This data cleaning process has only uncovered few issues: 
 
Several crashes, as stated in section 3.5.1, involved several vehicles or pedestrians; 
 
A significant share of accidents were hit-and-runs (i.e. involving drivers leaving the 
crime scene), hence without any information related to the driver; 
 
The road matching procedure had only identified about 90 % of the records; 
 
In the crash dataset, the variable describing the accident scenario (action of the 
pedestrian and the vehicle before the crash) was missing in an important share of the 
records. 
 
It has been chosen to discard records involving several vehicles or pedestrians, as they 
could not be easily incorporated in the different models employed in the thesis. This 
decision was consistent with the literature (Mohamed et al. 2013) (Sasidharan et al. 2015). 
 
Hit-and-runs differed from other crashes by their inherent nature: as the driver escaped the 
scene, variables related to his/her characteristics were missing. For this reason, it has been 
chosen to separate hit-and-run and no hit-and-run accidents in two separate datasets. 
 
 Finally, the data cleaning process has led to the elimination of several records as described 
in Table 10:  
Total records

Finally, two datasets have been created: 
 
A no hit-and-run dataset, comprising 6539 records; 
 
A hit-and-run dataset, comprising 930 records.

The two datasets thus contained all complete records of accidents involving one pedestrian 
and one vehicle in Denmark, from January 2006 to December 2015. The following 
paragraphs aim at giving a first overview of the datasets characteristics.

The sample comprises 6539 complete observations of no hit-and-runs pedestrian-vehicle 
accidents, from 2006 to 2015 in Denmark. Only 5.1 % of the pedestrian crashes have led 
to a fatality, 32.1% to severe injuries, while 45.4% of the crashes led to no injuries.  
Female pedestrians are slightly over-represented in the sample, accounting for 53.5 % of 
the accidents, while representing 50.5% of the Danish population in 2010 (Danmarks 
Statistik 2016b). From the age distribution of the Danish population as for 2010, it can be 
derived that young pedestrians are under-represented, while older pedestrians above 70 
years old are over-represented (17.4 % of the accidents for 10.9 % of the population in 
Denmark) (Danmarks Statistik 2016b). Most drivers are males (73.1 %), usually with more 
than 20 years of driving experience (43.1 %) and driving a car (74.3 %). The most common 
action of the pedestrian is found to be crossing the street (63.9 %), while the driver is 
mostly found to drive straight ahead (70.3 %). Most accidents occur on straight road 
sections (57.3 %), during daylight (61.7 %) in urban areas (87.7 %) with a speed limit of 
50 km/h (74.1 %), and do not involve alcohol for the pedestrian (89.1 %) or the driver 
(96.8 %). The majority of crashes occur on weekdays (79.1 %) with a good weather (92.8 
%) and dry roads (66.3 %). Interestingly, the autumn season is over-represented in the 
dataset (32.0 %).  
Direct conclusions regarding the land use and socio-economic characteristics of the 
accidents are more difficult to draw by looking at the whole sample. It can however be 
noted that the largest share of accidents is found in the Copenhagen area (37.6 %), in 
urban municipalities (56.5 %) with a rather high population density between 1000 – 5000 
inhabitants/km² (40.2 %), and located within 1 – 5 km to the nearest hospital (57.9 %). 
The remaining variables show a more spread distribution.  
The distribution of the accidents as shown in Figure 39 reveals that the largest cities of 
Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense and Esbjerg) exhibit a significant 
concentration of crashes.  
Most of the severe and fatal accidents occur in rural areas of Denmark, while city centres 
concentrate the less severe ones. Indeed, a significant share of fatalities can be found in 
rural areas of Denmark. Interestingly, accidents in the Copenhagen region reveal the 
presence of severe accidents in the inner part of the city, while less severe accidents are 
found to be located in the surroundings of Copenhagen.

less elements of information compared to the no hit-and-run dataset, due to the inherent 
nature of these accidents. Indeed, hit-and-runs are accidents for which the driver illegally 
leaves the crime scene: thus, little information regarding the driver is available, and only 
relies on witnesses’ declarations to the police.

Table 12: Hit-and-run accidents, characteristics 
 
The sample comprises 930 complete observations of hit-and-runs pedestrian-vehicle 
accidents, from 2006 to 2015 in Denmark. Only 2.3 % of the pedestrian crashes have led 
to a fatality, 25.8% to severe injuries, while 50.9% of the crashes led to no injuries. As a 
fact, hit-and-run pedestrian crashes appear to yield less severe injuries compared to the non-
hit-and-runs sample.  
The gender of the pedestrian does not show any specific tendency, with an equal share of 
males and females in the sample. However, the age distribution of the Danish population in 
2010 shows an over-representation of teenagers (15 – 19 years old) and young adults 
pedestrians (20 -29 years old) (respectively 15.9 % and 21.7 % of the accidents for 6.3 % 
and 11.5 % of the population), and an under-representation of  pedestrians between 60 – 
69 years old (7.0 % of the accidents for 12.1 % of the population) (Danmarks Statistik 
2016b). Due to the inherent nature of hit-and-runs, little information is available for the 
driver: only the vehicle type (a car in 76.6 % of the cases) and the driver’s behaviour prior 
to the accident (going straight in 71.8 % of the cases) are available. No busses or heavy 
vehicles are involved in hit-and-runs, exhibiting a result that differs from the existing body of 
literature (Fujita et al. 2014). Pedestrians are found to mostly be crossing the road at the 
moment of the accident (56.9 %), while drivers are mostly found to be driving straight 
==PAGE:96==
96 
 
ahead (71.8 %). Most accidents occur on straight road sections (59.3 %), during daylight 
(57.7 %) in urban areas (90.9 %) and a speed limit of 50 km/h (79.4 %), and do not 
involve alcohol for the pedestrian (91.2 %). The environment conditions highlight that most 
accidents occur on weekdays (75.4 %), good weather conditions (86.2 %), dry roads (76.1 
%) and an over-representation of the autumn season (30.3 %) as for the no hit-and-run 
sample. 
Similar results as for the no hit-and-run sample are yielded regarding the land use and socio-
economic characteristics. It can be noted that the largest share of accidents is found in the 
Copenhagen area (36.6 %), in urban municipalities (57.6 %) with a rather high population 
density between 1000 – 5000 inhabitants/km² (39.1 %), and located within 1 – 5 km to 
the nearest hospital (62.9 %).  
The accidents spatial distribution as shown in Figure 40 reveals that the four largest cities 
of Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg and Odense) concentrate a significant share of 
hit-and-run cases. Moreover, it can be interestingly be noted that the Middle East of Jutland 
seem to concentrate hit-and-run accidents. 
The spatial distribution of the severity of these accidents is more difficult to assess 
compared to the no hit-and-run sample. The fatal accidents are mostly located in the 
centres of few cities (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Odense, Randers, Helsingør), while the 
remaining severity levels are found to be quite evenly spread over the country. 
 
==PAGE:97==
97 
 
 
Figure 40: Hit-and-run accidents, severity map 
==PAGE:98==
98 
 
4.4 Summary 
 
The case studies yield two different datasets of pedestrian accidents in Denmark for the 
time period 2006 – 2015, namely no hit-and-runs and hit-and-runs. 
Hit-and-runs represent a significant share of the total number of pedestrian accidents in 
Denmark: indeed, they represent 12.4 % of the total number of crashes considered in the 
study. 
The two datasets exhibit a rather similar structure in terms of involved age groups of 
pedestrians, accidents characteristics, environment and socio-economic factors. However, 
the spatial distribution of the two datasets reveals differences of patterns in terms of 
severity for the two datasets. 
The two samples presented in this section can thus be used in the following sections to 
further understand the crash patterns in Denmark as well as the factors influencing the 
severity level of these accidents. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:99==
99 
 
5. Latent class analysis 
 
The first analysis to be carried out in this study aims at unveiling the pedestrian crash 
patterns in Denmark. For this purpose, the Latent Class Analysis method is used. This 
section first describes the relevance of the method for accident analysis, and then presents 
its theoretical background. Thereafter, the analyses are presented along with the results. 
 
5.1 Relevance of the method  
 
This paragraph builds up on the literature review carried out in section 2.3. As a 
consequence, it acts as a reminder and a summary related to the interest of latent class 
analysis with regard to pedestrian accident analysis. 
Investigating patterns within pedestrian crashes is an important area of research. Indeed, 
the detection of crash patterns helps to address pedestrian safety issues, to conceive 
countermeasures, and to target exposed populations with the overall goal to improve 
pedestrian safety (Prato et al. 2011). Clustering methods are therefore suitable for this 
purpose, as they rely on heuristics trying to optimise the similarities within the clusters and 
the differences between these different clusters (Depaire et al. 2008), thus separating 
observations into homogenous and exclusive groups.  
Latent class analysis (LCA), also known as model-based clustering or finite-mixture 
clustering, belongs to these classification techniques aiming at unveiling hidden patterns 
among observations. The method hypothesises that there is an unobserved categorical 
variable dividing the sample into exclusive and comprehensive latent classes (Lanza & 
Rhoades 2013). In another terms, it divides crash observations in different clusters by 
grouping them based on their similarities in the explanatory variables. It represents an 
unsupervised learning approach, as the amount of clusters and their definitions are not 
known in advance ((Magidson & Vermunt 2002), (Depaire et al. 2008)).  
The method has first been presented in the 1970s (Goodman 1974), and has been applied 
to several domains (sociology, psychology, education) (Lanza et al. 2007). The recent 
development of the computation capabilities of computers has shed a new interest for the 
method, making it an efficient and fast way to analyse patterns within datasets (Magidson 
& Vermunt 2002). Compared to other classification techniques, latent class analysis 
provides some benefits by allowing overlaps across clusters (Kaplan & Prato 2013), and 
also takes advantage of the existence of different goodness-of-fit criteria that helps the 
decision regarding the convergence of the method ((Depaire et al. 2008), (Mohamed et al. 
2013)). Moreover, the method does not imply any preliminary structure in the data, thus 
==PAGE:100==
100 
 
giving it an important flexibility (Kaplan & Prato 2013). Lastly, different types of variables 
(for instance counts, continuous, categorical, nominal) can be included in LCA, without 
standardization process (Depaire et al. 2008) . 
First applied to road traffic accidents  ((Depaire et al. 2008), (De Oña et al. 2013), (Weiss et 
al. 2015)) and cyclists accidents (Kaplan & Prato 2013), the method has recently been 
successfully applied to pedestrian crashes ((Mohamed et al. 2013), (Sasidharan et al. 
2015)). As a consequence, LCA appears to be a suitable and timely technique to analyse 
pedestrian crash patterns. 
 
5.2 Theory 
5.2.1 
Model formulation 
5.2.1.1 
General formulation 
LCA aims at classifying observations into K latent classes 𝐶𝑘, 𝑘∈[: , 𝐾], implying uncertainty 
in the cluster membership, while the amount of clusters and their size are unknown (Kaplan 
& Prato 2013). 
Assuming a vector of I crash observations defined by a vector of M variables (  𝑦𝑖=
 𝑦1, ⋯, 𝑦𝑀) and letting 𝑌𝑖 (𝑌𝑖= 𝑌𝑖1, ⋯, 𝑌𝑖𝑀) be the vector of parameters of crash i for the M 
variables, the latent class probability is written as shown in Eq. 5 (Depaire et al. 2008):  
𝑝(𝑌𝑖|𝜃) = ∑
𝑃(𝐶𝑘)𝑝(𝑌𝑖|𝐶𝑘, 𝜃𝑘)
𝐾
𝑘=1
       
Eq. 5: LCA model general specification        
 
Where 𝑘 (𝑘= 1, ⋯, 𝐾) indicates a latent class among the K classes considered in the model, 
𝑃(𝐶𝑘) is the probability of class 𝐶𝑘 in the sample, 𝑝(𝑌𝑖|𝐶𝑘, 𝜃𝑘) corresponds to the conditional 
multivariate probability that a crash in class 𝐶𝑘 would be described by 𝑌𝑖 and 𝜃𝑘 is a vector 
of parameters to be computed (Kaplan & Prato 2013). In a simpler way, the model aims at 
maximising the 𝑝(𝑌𝑖|𝜃) value, by grouping the observations in K clusters based on their 
similarities among the M variables, and captured by the 𝜃𝑘 vector. 
The model does not imply any assumptions regarding the variable types (i.e. continuous or 
discrete), their distributions, and the correlations between observations and variables 
(Kaplan & Prato 2013). 
 
==PAGE:101==
101 
 
5.2.1.2 
Restrictive assumptions 
The previous section has described the general formulation of the LCA method. However, 
an LCA formulation only allowing categorical variables has been adopted in the scope of this 
thesis, in order to derive an estimable LCA model with reduced complexity. 
Following the work done by peer researchers ((Kaplan & Prato 2013); (Sasidharan et al. 
2015); (Weiss et al. 2015)), simplifying assumptions are elaborated which comply with the 
problem formulation proposed by (Lanza et al. 2007): 
 
Every variable m is transformed in an ordinal variable with 𝑅𝑚 outcomes ( 𝑟𝑚=
 1, ⋯, 𝑅𝑚). With this assumption, only categorical variables are to be included in the 
model; 
 
The observations are assumed to be uncorrelated. In the case of pedestrian 
accidents, this assumption is fairly respected, as accidents can be assumed to be 
independent (Kaplan & Prato 2013); 
 
The categorical parameters are presumed to be independent within a latent class. 
This implies that the within-class covariance matrix is diagonal, thus keeping a 
simple model structure ((Kaplan & Prato 2013), (Weiss et al. 2015)); 
 
Categorical variables are endogenous indicators of the clusters. This implies that no 
covariates are used to determine the class attachment. 
 
5.2.1.3 
Categorical LCA formulation 
Based on these assumptions, the categorical LCA can be written as proposed by (Lanza et 
al. 2007) and shown in Eq. 6: 
𝑝(𝑌𝑖|𝜃) = ∑𝜋𝑘∏∏𝜃𝑚𝑟𝑚|𝑘
𝛿(𝑦𝑖𝑚=𝑟𝑚)
𝑅𝑚
𝑟𝑚=1
𝑀
𝑚=1
𝐾
𝑘=1
 
Eq. 6: LCA model categorical specification 
 
Where k is the cluster that observation i is a member of, δ is an indicator function taking the 
value 1 if 𝑦𝑖𝑚 equals 𝑟𝑚 and 0 otherwise, and 𝜋𝑘 and 𝜃𝑚𝑟𝑚|𝑘 are parameters to be computed. 
The parameters 𝜋𝑘 indicate cluster affiliation probabilities and 𝜃𝑚𝑟𝑚|𝑘 are indicators response 
probabilities conditional on the cluster membership (Kaplan & Prato 2013). 
 
5.2.2 
Estimation 
The model is estimated based on the maximum likelihood approach. With the formulation 
provided in Eq. 6, the problem of finding the clusters reduces to the estimation of the 𝜃𝑘 
vector parameter, which is to be estimated by the means of expectation-maximisation 
algorithm (Depaire et al. 2008).  
==PAGE:102==
102 
 
The expectation-maximisation algorithm is a two-step iterative process working as follows. 
Given the Y vector of accident observations, the 𝐶𝑘 latent clusters, the 𝜃𝑘  vector of 
parameters and the likelihood function 𝐿(𝜃𝑘, 𝑌, 𝐶𝑘) = 𝑝(𝑌, 𝐶𝑘|𝜃𝑘) , the marginal likelihood 
estimate (MLE) of the unknown parameters 𝜃𝑘 is determined by the marginal likelihood 
shown in Eq. 7 (Theodoridis 2015): 
𝐿(𝜃𝑘 , 𝑌) = 𝑝(𝑌|𝜃𝑘 ) = ∑𝑝(𝑌, 𝐶𝑘|𝜃𝑘)
𝐾
𝑘=1
 
Eq. 7: Marginal likelihood 
 
The expectation-maximisation algorithm then alternates between two steps (Theodoridis 
2015): 
 
An expectation step (E), which calculates the expected value of the log likelihood 
function, with respect to the conditional distribution of 𝐶𝑘 given Y under the current 
estimate of the parameters 𝜃𝑘
(𝑡) shown in Eq. 8: 
 
𝑄(𝜃𝑘|𝜃𝑘
(𝑡)) = 𝐸𝐶𝑘|𝑌,𝜃𝑘
(𝑡)[log 𝐿(𝜃𝑘, 𝑌, 𝐶𝑘)] 
Eq. 8: Expectation step 
 
 
A maximisation step (M), which finds the parameters that maximise Q as shown in 
Eq. 9: 
𝜃𝑘
(𝑡+1) = 𝑎𝑟𝑔𝑚𝑎𝑥𝜃𝑘 𝑄(𝜃𝑘|𝜃𝑘
(𝑡)) 
Eq. 9: Maximisation step 
 
The model iterates on these (E) and (M) steps until convergence.  
 
5.2.3 
Goodness-of-fit  
5.2.3.1 
Choice of criterion 
The best number of clusters K explaining the relations within the considered dataset is not 
known in advance in the LCA. As a consequence, it is needed to run different specifications 
by changing the number of clusters in order to determine the best model fit.  
The determination of the number of clusters is driven by the minimization of the error when 
attaching individual crashes to a cluster (Sasidharan et al. 2015). Several goodness-of-fit 
measures are proposed for estimation ((Lanza et al. 2007), (Depaire et al. 2008)): 
 
AIC: Akaike Information Criterion; 
 
BIC: Bayesian Information Criterion; 
==PAGE:103==
103 
 
 
CAIC: Consistent Akaike Information Criterion; 
 
Entropy. 
AIC, BIC and CAIC measures are based on the value of the likelihood function, and have in 
common that the lower the value for these criteria, the better the model is (Sasidharan et al. 
2015). On the contrary, a higher entropy is related to a better fit (Sasidharan et al. 2015), 
and ranges from 0 to 1. It is commonly accepted that an entropy higher than 0.9 provides a 
good fit (De Oña et al. 2013).  
The BIC is chosen to assess the goodness-of-fit among the different criteria previously 
mentioned. Indeed, (Nylund et al. 2007) demonstrates the superiority of BIC over the other 
information criteria, both in terms of accuracy and consistency. 
 
5.2.3.2 
Best solution assessment 
As mentioned previously, the lower the value of the BIC criterion, the better fit of the 
model. However, it is unclear whether an increase in the number of clusters would always 
reach a minimum value in terms of BIC, especially for large samples (Sasidharan et al. 
2015). Moreover, the number of clusters should also be based on the ability to distinguish 
and interpret clusters of non-negligible sizes (Lanza et al. 2007) (Kaplan & Prato 2013). 
Indeed, adding clusters may lead to a difficult interpretation of the results for the 
researcher, with an important load of information that could be detrimental for the analysis. 
For these different reasons, the LCA analysis is run from 2 to 20 clusters. The BIC criterion 
is observed, with two different cases for the determination of the number of clusters: 
 
Clear minimum: in case the BIC criterion provides a clear minimum value in the range 
2 – 20 clusters, the number of clusters that minimises the BIC criterion is kept as the 
best fit; 
 
Decreasing trend: in case the BIC criterion shows a decreasing trend, the number of 
clusters from which the solution does not improve by more than 0.5 % onwards is 
kept as the best fit. This method is consistent with the existing practice in the 
literature (Sasidharan et al. 2015). 
 
5.2.4 
Characterisation of clusters 
After the determination of the right number of clusters to be considered, the consecutive 
step consists to identify and name them. Following the existing body of literature ((Depaire 
et al. 2008), (Kaplan & Prato 2013), (Weiss et al. 2015)), the clusters are identified based 
on the prevalence and/or the over-representation of some crash characteristics within the 
clusters. The threshold applied to consider a feature as prevalent is a share higher than 50 
% in the cluster. The over(under)-representation of a characteristic is based on the analysis 
==PAGE:104==
104 
 
of the variation of the distribution of a variable within the clusters, tested against a Chi-
squared test with p=0.05, by comparing the characteristic distribution in the cluster with 
the expected distribution in the whole dataset as proposed by (Weiss et al. 2015). 
 
5.2.5 
Implementation 
The estimation of the model is based on the estimation-maximisation procedure described in 
section 5.2.2. Its actual implementation relies on the SAS procedure developed by (Lanza et 
al. 2007), at the Methodology Center of Pennstate University. An example of the procedure 
is provided in appendix (page 246). Following the work of (Depaire et al. 2008) and (Weiss 
et al. 2015), 50 sets of random starting values are used, in order to increase the likelihood 
of convergence to the global maximum. This is due to the fact that the likelihood function is 
not necessarily concave, which may lead the EM algorithm to converge to a local maximum. 
 
5.3 LCA model elaboration 
 
According to the assumptions developed in section 5.2.1.2, the datasets of pedestrian 
accidents are prepared with only categorical variables. As a consequence, continuous 
variables such as population density, mean income or road traffic volumes are transformed 
into categorical ones.  
A selection of the variables to include in the LCA is carried out. Indeed, numerous variables 
are included in the datasets presented in section 4, leading to an extremely important 
amount of data to process in the LCA. Therefore, several model specifications are run, in 
order to find the best version of the LCA analysis. All variables regarding the accident 
characteristics are included, while land use and socio-economic factors are carefully 
selected. Indeed, a balance has to be found between the prevalence of the crash 
characteristics within the clusters and the inclusion of land use and socio-economic factors. 
The LCA analysis is separately performed on the two datasets described in section 4: 
 
Non-hit-and-runs: 6539 observations (one vehicle-one pedestrian); 
 
Hit-and-runs: 930 observations (one vehicle-one pedestrian). 
 
The following sections aim at presenting the results yielded by the LCA analyses. 
 
==PAGE:105==
105 
 
5.4 No hit-and-run analysis 
5.4.1 
Number of clusters 
 
As previously mentioned, different models are estimated (2 to 20 clusters) using variables 
described in Table 11 at the exception of the severity level sustained by the pedestrian. 
Indeed, it is an outcome of the crash and relates to the cluster characteristics; thus, it 
cannot be used as an independent variable (Kaplan & Prato 2013). The BIC criterion shows 
a decreasing trend while the number of clusters is growing, as shown in Figure 41. 
Complying with the assumptions mentioned in section 5.2.3.2, the most appropriate 
solution is found to be 8 clusters, as the fit of the model does not improve by more than 
0.5 % onwards. Moreover, the entropy criterion for this solution is 0.95, indicating 
excellent class separation and homogeneity. 
 
 
Figure 41: No hit-and-run accidents, LCA convergence 
 
5.4.2 
Characterisation of clusters 
 
The distribution of the variables within the 8 clusters are summarised in Table 13. Several 
variable categories are prevalent across all clusters: few crashes involve pedestrian or driver 
intoxication, while the most frequent gender for the driver is male. Regarding the 
environment, most crashes occur on two-way undivided roads with one lane per direction, 
on weekdays with good weather.

==PAGE:110==
110 
 
Features identifying the clusters are based on the prevalence and over(under)-representation 
of the variable categories shown in Table 13. The sizes of the clusters are found to be 
unevenly distributed, clusters C4 and C5 respectively accounting for 5.4 % and 6.7 % of 
the observations, while cluster C1 accounts for 19.9 % of the sample.  
Due to the large number of variables included in the model, the most important features 
characterizing the clusters are highlighted in Figure 42. Seven clusters are linked with urban 
accidents (C1 – C7) and a speed limit of 50 km/h, while one cluster is associated with rural 
accidents (C8) with a higher speed limit.  
Among the seven clusters in urban areas, five are related to accidents occurring of road 
sections (C1 – C5). Clusters C1 – C3 are linked with sober drivers, while C4 and C5 involve 
drunk drivers as well as a majority of female pedestrians. Clusters C1 and C2 relate to 
pedestrians being hit by cars going straight ahead while crossing, while C3 is associated 
with pedestrians hit by reversing cars. Cluster C1 mostly involves elderly pedestrians in 
highly dense city centres in Denmark, while C2 both implicates young and elderly 
pedestrians in less dense urban areas, with an over-representation of older drivers (50 – 59 
years old). Cluster C3 includes young and elderly pedestrians, with an over-representation 
of pedestrians being hit by reversing cars in residential, low buildings areas with low road 
traffic. Cluster C4 relates to female pedestrians being hit when crossing by drunk cyclists 
aged between 20 – 29 years old, mainly on major two-way-divided local roads in extremely 
densely populated areas. Cluster C5 contains accidents involving female pedestrians and 
intoxicated moped riders aged less than 18 years old. These accidents mostly occur with 
mopeds jumping off the road, hitting female pedestrians walking on the pavement in narrow 
streets. 
Clusters C6 and C7 are related to accidents occurring in urban intersections, involving 
vehicles hitting pedestrians crossing major local roads. Cluster C6 contains accidents 
involving drivers going straight aged between 30 – 39 years old, hitting intoxicated 
pedestrian aged 20 – 29 years old in daylight, with an over-representation of involvement of 
busses. Cluster C7 relates to drivers turning in an intersection (mainly left), hitting sober 
female pedestrian on illuminated road, with an over-representation of bad weather 
conditions and wet road during autumn/winter. 
Finally, cluster C8 is clearly distinct from the rest of the crash patterns, and contains rural 
accidents on road sections, involving sober drivers and drunk male pedestrians, on express, 
national or regional roads with a speed limit higher or equal to 80 km/h. Pedestrians are 
usually found standing on or walking along the road, in poor visibility conditions on dark 
roads, during weekends, with an over-representation of involvement of heavy vehicles. 
This classification can then be summarised in Table 14. 
==PAGE:111==

Table 14: No hit-and-run accidents, cluster definition 
 
 
Therefore, distinct accident patterns are unveiled by the classification which can be further 
investigated. It can interestingly be noted that almost all unveiled crash patterns comply 
with the existing body of knowledge regarding pedestrian accidents. Cluster C1 regarding  
elderly being hit in dense urban areas has already been observed by (Prato et al. 2012) in 
the metropolitan area of Tel Aviv, Israel. Cluster C2 related to young pedestrians being hit in 
low dense residential areas has also been pointed out by (Prato et al. 2012), with accidents 
involving children in urban road sections of small villages in Israel. Cluster C3 regarding 
careless reversing involving old pedestrians has been identified in New-York, USA 
(Mohamed et al. 2013) and  Switzerland (Sasidharan et al. 2015). Clusters C4 and C5 
related to bikes and mopeds have been highlighted in urban areas of Israel (Prato et al. 
2012). Cluster C7, related to accidents on urban intersections and illuminated roads has 
already been uncovered by (Mohamed et al. 2013). Lastly, cluster C8 related to weekend 
accidents with pedestrian intoxication on rural roads has been identified in Switzerland 
(Sasidharan et al. 2015). 
These different findings thus prove the relevance of the LCA method with respect to the 
identification of pedestrian crash patterns; moreover, it confirms the major crash types that 
should be targeted by policy makers.

5.4.3 
Distribution of accidents’ severity 
 
The distribution of the pedestrians ‘injury severity levels within each cluster is considered as 
a dependent variable deriving from the cluster features, as proposed by (Kaplan & Prato 
2013) and summarised in Table 15, in which each cluster has been attributed a colour 
depending on its severity level (light blue being the lowest, dark grey the highest). The 
clusters are significantly different in terms of pedestrians’ severity (Pearson’s Chi-squared 
test=410.3 with 21 degrees of freedom) at the 0.001 level. Interestingly, cluster C8 
sustains the highest fatality rate (18.2 %), while C4 and C5 have the lowest rates (0.9 % 
and 1.4 %), compared to the average 5.1 % in the whole sample. These figures can be 
easily explained, as C4 is associated with accidents involving bicycles and mopeds 
(associated with low speed and vehicle weight), while C8 relates to crashes at high speed 
on rural roads, hence increasing the risk of severe accidents. In turn, C8 contains 36.9 % of 
total fatalities in the sample, while accounting for 10.4 % of the observations. The 
remaining clusters are quite evenly distributed in terms of severity at the exception of C3, 
involving reversing cars, which shows a higher than average fatality rate (6.5 %).

5.4.4 
Spatial distribution of clusters 
 
The 8 clusters are mapped as shown in Figure 43. A clear spatial pattern can be 
investigated: clusters C1, C4 and C6 are mostly located in the largest cities of Denmark 
(Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense), indicating that these cities share the same issues 
related to pedestrian accidents. Cluster C1 is mostly present in the suburbs of these cities, 
while C4 and C6 are present in the inner parts of the cities. Cluster C2 is mostly present in 
the smaller cities of Denmark, such as Køge, Viborg or Fredericia. Cluster C3 is evenly 
spread over low populated areas of Denmark. Cluster C7 is present in the suburbs of major 
and medium cities of Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense, Esbjerg, Fredericia, 
Viborg, etc.). Lastly, cluster C8, related to rural accidents and most severe cluster, is evenly 
spread over Denmark. This indicates that safety problems related to rural accidents are not 
spatially clustered, but rather represent an issue at the country-level. 
==PAGE:114==
114 
 
 
Figure 43: No hit-and-run accidents, cluster map 
==PAGE:115==

5.5 Hit-and-run analysis 
5.5.1 
Number of clusters 
 
Different models are estimated (2 to 20 clusters), using the variables described in Table 12 
at the exception of the severity level sustained by the pedestrian, for the same reasons as 
developed for the non-hit-and-run sample (section 5.4.1). The BIC criterion reveals a clear 
convex shape when plotted against the number of clusters, as shown in Figure 44. 
Complying with the assumptions mentioned in section 5.2.3.2, the most appropriate 
solution is 5 clusters as the lowest BIC value is attained at that point; moreover, the 
entropy criterion for this solution is 0.95, indicating great class separation. 
 
 
Figure 44: Hit-and-run accidents, LCA convergence 
 
5.5.2 
Characterisation of clusters 
 
The distribution of the variables within the 5 clusters are summarised in Table 16. Several 
variable categories are prevalent across all clusters: few crashes involve pedestrian 
intoxication, while the majority of pedestrians are Danes. Regarding the environment and 
crash characteristics, most crashes occur on two-way undivided roads with one lane per 
direction and involve cars. Lastly, most hit-and-runs occur on weekdays at daylight, with 
good visibility and weather.
