Table 16: Hit-and-run accidents, LCA results 
 
 
Based on the classification assumptions mentioned in section 5.2.4 and similarly as for the 
non-hit-and-run sample, features identifying the clusters are based on the prevalence and 
over(under)-representation of the variable categories shown in Table 16. The LCA yields 
four large clusters containing each about 20 % of the whole sample (C1 – C4), and one 
smaller cluster only including 7.3 % of the observations (C5). 
As fewer elements of information are available in the hit-and-run sample due to the absence 
of the driver’s characteristics, elements regarding the environment are found to be more 
prevalent to understand the patterns of these accidents. The characteristics of the clusters 
are summarised in Figure 45. Clusters C1 – C4 are related to accidents occurring in urban 
areas. Two clusters are related to accidents on road sections (C1 – C2), while the other 
two are related to accidents occurring in intersections (C3 – C4). Cluster C1 relates to 
accidents with an over-representation of bikes going straight ahead, hitting intoxicated 
pedestrian aged 20 – 29 years old crossing major illuminated local roads on weekend 
nights. Cluster C2 involves a higher than average share of cars jumping off the road, hitting 
young pedestrian aged 10 – 19 years old on minor streets in small cities of Denmark. 
Cluster C3 relates to cars going straight ahead in intersections, on two-way divided roads 
with a speed limit between 51 – 79 km/h, hitting a pedestrian crossing by good weather at 
daylight. Cluster C4 involves turning car drivers, hitting female pedestrians in 
autumn/winter, with an over-representation of bad weather and wet roads. 
Lastly, cluster C5 is related to rural accidents, involving male sober pedestrians, aged 50 -
59 years old, walking along or standing on unlit express/national/regional roads with an 
over-representation of weekend nights during spring/autumn. Lastly, this cluster reveals the 
over-representation of involvement of mopeds. 
 
This classification can then be summarised in Table 17, helping to better identify the 
clusters. 
==PAGE:120==
120 
 
C1 
Urban, road section,  driver going straight ahead, bike,   
20 – 29 yo intoxicated pedestrian crossing,  weekend, illuminated road, dry road 
C2 
Urban, road section,  driver jumping off the road, car,   
teenage sober pedestrian  walking on the pavement/along the road,  weekday, daylight, dry road 
C3 
Urban, intersection,  driver going straight ahead, car,     
sober pedestrian crossing,  weekday, daylight, dry road 
C4 
Urban, intersection,  driver turning, car,  
sober female pedestrian  crossing,  weekday, daylight, wet road 
C5 
Rural, road section,  driver going straight ahead, car,  
sober male pedestrian  walking along/standing on the road,  weekend, unlit road, dry road 
Table 17: Hit-and-run accidents, cluster definition 
 
As for the no-hit-and-run analysis, the LCA yields clusters with clear and distinct patterns. 
Moreover, it can be noted that some hit-and-run clusters overlap with the no hit-and-run 
ones. Indeed, cluster C4 and C5 are really close in their definition from the no hit-and-run 
clusters C7 and C8. However, the remaining clusters prove that some patterns are specific 
to hit-and-runs, thus revealing these crashes call for different policies to reduce their 
occurrence compared to no hit-and-runs. 
Due to their close definitions of no hit-and-run crash types, clusters C4 and C5 can be 
linked to patterns observed in New York, USA regarding accidents at intersections in bad 
weather conditions (Mohamed et al. 2013) and Israel, regarding rural accidents at night 
(Prato et al. 2012).

5.5.3 
Distribution of accidents’ severity 
 
A similar approach, as for the non-hit-and-run sample, is adopted regarding the distribution 
of the pedestrians ‘injury severity levels. Table 18 summarises the distribution of 
pedestrians ‘injury severity levels across the clusters. As for the no hit-a-run sample, each 
cluster is attributed a colour depending on its severity level (share of severe injury + 
fatality). The clusters are not found to be significantly different in terms of pedestrians’ 
severity (Pearson’s Chi-squared test=19.3 with 12 degrees of freedom) at the 0.001 level. 
Interestingly, the overall severity of hit-and-runs is found to be lower compared to the non-
hit-and-run sample. Cluster C5, related to rural accidents, is highlighted as greatly increasing 
the risk of severe fatalities (38.2 % against 25.8 % in the whole sample), while surprisingly 
not leading to any fatalities.

5.5.4 
Spatial distribution of clusters 
 
The 5 clusters are mapped as shown in Figure 46. In a similar way as for the non-hit-and-
run sample, some spatial pattern can be investigated. Clusters C1 and C3 are found to be 
present in the larger cities of Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg and Odense), with C1 
being present in the inner parts of the cities, while C3 is mostly present in the suburbs. C2 
is found to be present in low densely populated areas of Denmark, and quite evenly spread 
over Denmark. Cluster C4 is found to be present in smaller cities of Denmark, such as Køge, 
Herning or Randers. Lastly, cluster C5, the most severe cluster and related to rural 
accidents, is evenly spread over Denmark, indicating a global issue over the country for this 
type of accident. 
==PAGE:123==
123 
 
 
Figure 46: Hit-and-run accidents, cluster map 
==PAGE:124==
124 
 
5.6 Summary 
 
The two analyses carried out have helped to unveil interesting patterns. The non-hit-and-run 
dataset is found to be more informative, due to the greater size of the sample and the 
availability of data.  
Interestingly, many unveiled clusters comply with the previous findings in the literature, 
thus confirming the different pedestrian crash patterns that should be targeted by policy 
makers. 
It can be noted that hit-and-runs and non-hit-and-runs yield different crash patterns. Only 
two clusters are found to be partially overlapping between the two datasets (rural accidents 
on national road and accidents in intersections with drivers turning) while the remaining 
clusters reveal significantly different crash patterns between the two samples. Thus, hit-
and-runs are proved to have specificities that are not shared with other accident types and 
call for different measures to specifically target this issue.  
The severity level within clusters is found to be significantly different in the no hit-and-run 
sample, thus confirming that specific crash types may be more severe than other ones and 
should be prioritised regarding pedestrian safety policies. 
Lastly, the spatial distribution of the clusters reveals a clear spatial segregation between 
crash patterns. In turn, these results can be used to specifically focus pedestrian safety 
policies in precise areas of Denmark, thus greatly improving the efficiency of these 
measures. 
 
 
==PAGE:125==
125 
 
6. Severity analysis  
 
The latent class analysis allowed investigating pedestrian accidents ‘patterns, and revealed 
interesting and unforeseen multi-faceted aspects of these crashes. However, road safety 
researchers are also interested in the development of effective countermeasures to reduce 
the severity of these accidents, requiring a thorough understanding of the characteristics 
that mitigate the degree of injury sustained by pedestrians (Mannering & Bhat 2014). Thus, 
in accordance with the traditional framework of road safety analyses, severity models of 
pedestrian accidents are developed in order to supplement the results obtained from the 
LCA models. In a first attempt, a multinomial logit model is built in order to unveil the 
significant variables affecting the severity level of the accidents. Subsequently, 
heterogeneity among the crash observation is investigated through the inclusion of mixed 
effects in the model. Lastly, spatial correlation within the dataset is investigated. 
 
6.1 Relevance of the method 
 
As described in the literature review, especially in section 2.2.3.1, severity modelling in 
road safety research has been thoroughly investigated. Numerous types of models have 
been used, as summarised by (Mannering & Bhat 2014), such as logit/probit models, 
multinomial logit, ordered models and their refinements (nested models, random parameters 
to account for heterogeneity, spatial and temporal correlation). Moreover, a trend in road 
safety research reveals the shift of interest from simple binary logit/probit models towards 
more sophisticated ones for the recent years.  
Therefore, the study of the severity of pedestrian accidents is in line with the road safety 
researchers’ interests. Moreover, the use of advanced modelling techniques is consistent 
with the latest trends in severity modelling and targets widely recognised issues. 
A great debate in severity modelling deals with the consideration of the severity level by the 
victim as a nominal or an ordered outcome (Mannering & Bhat 2014). On the one hand, 
injury severity could be considered as an ordered outcome, as the different severity levels of 
accidents can be easily ranked (no injury < light injury < severe injury < fatality).  On the 
other hand, several limitations arise from ordered response models as reported by (Castro et 
al. 2013), such as inconsistency problems due to varying under-reporting rates across injury 
severity levels, for which unordered models are more robust.  
Given the previously mentioned considerations, three non-ordered severity models are 
developed due to their wide use in severity modelling (Mannering & Bhat 2014). The 
multinomial logit model is chosen for its simplicity and constitutes a base model which can 
==PAGE:126==
126 
 
be used to compare more advanced techniques with. Thus, it allows unveiling the 
significant variables influencing the severity level of pedestrian accidents in Denmark. 
Consequently, the mixed multinomial logit model is chosen to investigate the heterogeneity 
within the observations, due to the recognition of its benefits in the literature (Mannering & 
Bhat 2014). Moreover, the mixed multinomial logit can be directly derived from a 
multinomial logit model, thus facilitating its development.  
Lastly, a spatial lag binary logit model is used to specifically study the influence of spatial 
correlation among observation, a recognised issue in severity modelling (Mannering & Bhat 
2014). The choice of such a model has however led to some difficulties. A literature review 
regarding these models has indeed uncovered three spatial techniques meeting the needs of 
the present thesis, namely spatial lag/spatial error generalized ordered probit model (Castro 
et al. 2013), spatial lag multinomial logit model (Brasington et al. 2016) and spatial lag 
binary logit model (Klier & McMillen 2008). However, these spatial models are rather new 
and complex in their formulation: thus, only the last model was available in usual statistical 
software package and has therefore been chosen. 
 
6.2 Theory 
 
This section aims at describing the theory behind the different severity models that are 
employed in this thesis. 
6.2.1 
Models formulation 
 
Three different models are employed: a multinomial logit, mixed effect multinomial logit and 
spatial binomial logit models. 
6.2.1.1 
Multinomial logit model 
Being an extension of the binary logit model, the multinomial logit model (MNL) is a 
regression method that allows for multiple discrete outcomes variable (dependent variable). 
Based on the values of the explanatory variables, the MNL model thus classifies the 
observations in order to reproduce the observed outcomes of the dependent variable.  
Choice set (dependent variable) 
In the present case, the outcome considered is the level of severity sustained by the 
pedestrian in the accident and can take four values: 
 
No injury; 
 
Light injury; 
 
Severe injury; 
==PAGE:127==
127 
 
 
Fatality. 
Thus, for each observation of the dataset, the MNL aims at reproducing the severity level 
sustained by the pedestrian, based on the explanatory factors included in the model. 
The multinomial logit model requires some restrictions on the set of outcome alternatives 𝑆, 
all being fulfilled by the severity level sustained by the pedestrian: 
 
The alternatives have to be mutually exclusive; 
 
The choice set must be exhaustive; 
 
The alternatives are in finite number. 
Utility functions (explanatory variables) 
The explanatory variables included in the MNL model are employed to predict, for each 
observation, which outcome in the dependent variable is chosen. Therefore, for each crash 
observation 𝑖  facing a choice between S severity levels, S utility functions 𝑈𝑠𝑖 are 
constructed, which model the “preferences” of each crash for the different severity levels, 
based on the accident characteristics captured by the explanatory variables (Koppelman & 
Bhat 2006).  
These utility functions can however not provide a full explanation of the choice among 
severity levels. Indeed, the explanatory variables included in the model are of the road 
safety researcher’s choice, who most likely does not possess perfect information regarding 
the influencing factors on the severity level of accidents. Moreover, accidents are a unique 
combination of numerous influencing factors for which the “choice” of a severity level may 
not be consistent and transitive among observations. 
For these reasons, the utility functions are decomposed into a deterministic part, capturing 
the utility observed by the road safety researcher, and a stochastic part accounting for the 
unknown portion of the utility. As a result, and given K explanatory variables chosen by the 
road safety researcher, the utility function of severity level 𝑠∈𝑆 for accident 𝑖∈𝐼 can be 
written as follows (Koppelman & Bhat 2006): 
𝑈𝑠𝑖=
𝑉𝑠𝑖
⏟
𝐷𝑒𝑡𝑒𝑟𝑚𝑖𝑛𝑖𝑠𝑡𝑖𝑐
+ 
𝜀𝑠𝑖
⏟
𝑆𝑡𝑜𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑠𝑡𝑖𝑐
= 𝑄𝑠+ ∑𝛽𝑠𝑘∙𝑋𝑖𝑘
𝑘𝜖𝐾
+ 𝜀𝑠𝑖 
Eq. 10: MNL utility function 
 
Where 𝑉𝑠𝑖 represents the deterministic part of the utility function; 
 
𝜀𝑠𝑖 is the error term accounting for the stochastic part of the utility function; 
 
𝑄𝑠 is an alternative-specific constant; 
==PAGE:128==
128 
 
 
𝛽𝑠𝑘 is the parameter capturing the influence of variable k on level of severity s; 
 
𝑋𝑖𝑘 is the value taken by variable k for the crash observation i. 
The deterministic part of the utility function 𝑉𝑠𝑖 corresponds to the observable part of the 
utility function 𝑈𝑠𝑖. Two types of variables can be identified, as shown in Eq. 10: 
 
Alternatives specific constants 𝑄𝑠 are defined in order to reproduce the observed 
shares of the different alternatives. Moreover, it allows the error terms 𝜀𝑠𝑖 to have a 
mean equal to 0. 
 
Variables varying across accidents 𝑋𝑖𝑘, which are the explanatory variables. The 
choice of the variables to include is up to the researcher’s choice, mostly driven by 
previous studies in the field, as well as trials and errors processes. 
 
As shown in Eq. 10, the utility functions are specified as linear-in-parameters, while 
explanatory variables can be of various forms (continuous, categorical) and adapted with 
mathematical transformations (log, scaling, etc.). The  𝛽𝑠𝑘 parameters capture the influence 
of variable k on severity level s and therefore are the elements of information road 
researchers are interested in. These parameters can be defined as varying across the 
severity levels (for a variable k, s parameters 𝛽𝑠𝑘 are defined, one for each severity level), or 
be fixed across the severity levels, hence degenerating in 𝛽𝑘. 
The stochastic part of the utility, 𝜀𝑠𝑖, represents the error between the true utility 𝑈𝑠𝑖 and the 
deterministic part 𝑉𝑠𝑖 of the utility that the researcher can capture. As stated before, it 
accounts for the fact that the road safety researcher is not able to fully and accurately 
model the influencing factors on the severity levels, hence leaving unobserved effects. In 
the case of an MNL model, the error term follow three assumptions (Koppelman & Bhat 
2006): 
 
The errors are identically and independently distributed across alternatives; 
 
The errors are identically and independently distributed across observations; 
 
The errors are Gumbel distributed, as shown in Eq. 11. 
𝑓(𝜀) = 𝜇∙(𝑒𝑥𝑝(−𝜇∙(𝜀−𝜂)) ∙𝑒𝑥𝑝(−𝑒𝑥𝑝((−𝜇∙(𝜀−𝜂))) 
Eq. 11: MNL model error distribution 
 
Where 𝜇 is the scale parameter, determining the variance of the distribution; 
 
𝜂 is the mode parameter. 
The choice of such a distribution is driven by two important properties. The Gumbel 
distribution is indeed almost similar as assuming that the errors terms are independent and 
==PAGE:129==
129 
 
normally distributed. Moreover, the difference between two errors follows a logistic 
distribution, which allows the MNL to be formulated in a simple way. 
Decision rule (random utility theory) 
The decision rule for each crash to fall under a specific severity level relies on the random 
utility theory. This theory hypothesizes that each crash observation can be seen as a 
rational utility maximiser. Thus, a crash observation 𝑖 would choose the severity level 𝑠∈𝑆  
if and only if: 
𝑈𝑠𝑖≥𝑈𝑙𝑖 ∀ 𝑙≠𝑠 ∈𝑆 
Eq. 12: Utility maximisation 
 
As uncertainty is observed in the definition of the utility functions, the deterministic decision 
rule as shown in Eq. 12 is impractical. Instead, the MNL model describes the choice in term 
of probabilities of each observation to fall under the different severity levels as shown in Eq. 
13 (Koppelman & Bhat 2006): 
𝑃𝑠𝑖= 𝑃𝑟𝑜𝑏 {𝑈𝑠𝑖≥𝑈𝑙𝑖 , ∀ 𝑙≠𝑠 ∈𝑆} 
Eq. 13: Random utility maximisation 
Model formulation 
Given all the assumptions presented, and following the random utility theory as shown in 
Eq. 13, the probability of the severity level s for accident i in the MNL model is written 
(Koppelman & Bhat 2006): 
𝑃𝑠𝑖=
exp (𝑄𝑠+ ∑
𝛽𝑠𝑘∙𝑋𝑖𝑘
𝑘𝜖𝐾
)
∑(exp (𝑄𝑙+ ∑
𝛽𝑙𝑘∙𝑋𝑖𝑘)
𝑘𝜖𝐾
)
𝑙
 
Eq. 14: MNL choice probability 
 
As a summary, the construction of an MNL model for the severity of accidents primarily 
aims at reproducing the outcomes of the accidents by modelling the influence of the 
explanatory variables on the severity level. Hence, the 𝛽𝑠𝑘 parameters represent the focus of 
the researcher, as they determine the influences and their magnitudes on each level of 
severity of accidents. 
 
6.2.1.2 
Mixed effects multinomial logit 
The mixed effect multinomial logit model (MXL) builds up on the standard multinomial logit 
model by relaxing the assumptions related to the 𝛽𝑠𝑘 parameters capturing the influence of 
variable k on severity level s. In a large share of studies related to injury severity modelling, 
only fixed 𝛽𝑠𝑘 parameters are indeed included, implying that explanatory variables have the 
same impact across accident observations. However, unobserved heterogeneity is likely to 
==PAGE:130==
130 
 
exist among accidents: one could for instance think about the characteristics of the 
pedestrian that may vary from individual to individual and not captured (differences in risk-
taking behaviour, psychological factors), or detailed characteristics of the vehicle type not 
accounted for in the model and influencing the severity level (shape of the vehicle, presence 
of bumpers and other passive pedestrian safety elements). Therefore, not accounting for 
these heterogeneities may lead to biased estimation of parameters. 
In order to solve this issue, the mixed multinomial logit model (MXL) replaces the fixed 𝛽𝑠𝑘 
by distributed parameters 𝛽𝑠𝑘
̃ , following density distributions 𝛽𝑠𝑘
̃ ≈𝑓(𝛽𝑠𝑘
̃ |𝛺𝑠𝑘) where 𝛺𝑠𝑘 are 
the universes of the 𝛽𝑠𝑘
̃ . Thus, the level of influence of a variable on the severity level of 
accidents can be individualized among observations. Several distributions can be used to fit 
the 𝛽𝑠𝑘
̃   parameters. However, it is common to use the Normal distribution to model them, 
hence reducing the determination of 𝑓(𝛽𝑠𝑘
̃ |𝛺𝑠𝑘) to the calculation of mean and variance. 
The utility functions are defined in the exact same way as for the MNL. Based on a 
realisation 𝜷̂ of the distributed parameters 𝛽𝑠𝑘,
̃  𝑠∈𝑆, 𝑘∈𝐾, the probability of having severity 
level s for accident 𝑖∈𝐼  , conditional on 𝜷̂ can be written as: 
𝐿𝑠𝑖(𝜷̂) =
𝑄𝑠+ 𝑒𝑥𝑝(∑

6.2.1.3 
Spatial binary logit model 
Crashes in close proximity in space are likely to share unobserved effects. (Levine et al. 
1995) hence reveal that accidents are likely to be spatially correlated, and should therefore 
not be considered as pure independent observations. Ignoring such correlations might imply 
a loss of efficiency and precision in the estimation of parameters of severity models 
(Savolainen et al. 2011). 
The spatial lag binary logit model developed by (Klier & McMillen 2008) builds up on the 
classical binary logit model by accounting for correlation among observations. Due to the 
==PAGE:131==
131 
 
restriction to a binary choice (in our case, no injury/light injury versus severe injury/fatality), 
only one utility function has to be written, one outcome being taken as reference. For a set 
of n observation, the utility function in the spatial lag logit model for observation 𝑖∈[1: 𝑛] is 
written as shown in Eq. 17 (Klier & McMillen 2008): 
𝑈𝑖
∗= 𝜌∙ 
∑

∗

+ 𝑄+ ∑𝛽𝑘∙𝑋𝑖𝑘
𝑘𝜖𝐾
+ 𝜀𝑖

𝑋𝑖𝑘 is the value taken by variable k for observation I; 
 
𝜀𝑖 is the error term, specified in the same way as for the MNL model (see 6.2.1.1). 
The specification of the utility function 𝑈∗ as shown in Eq. 17 implies that the severity level 
of accident i does not only rely on the explanatory variables, but also on the weighted 
outcomes of the neighbouring accidents through the term 𝜌∙ ∑

The matrix W is of size n x n, and represents a “weight” matrix among observations. 
Following the example of (Castro et al. 2013), several specifications of W matrices are 
defined, based on an inverse representation of the spatial closeness between observations, 
including the inverse of the distance between crash observations and its higher orders, as 
well as the inverse of the exponential, logarithm and square root of these distances.

 
The diagonal of W is filled with 0 (no autocorrelation), hence ∀ 𝑖∈[1; 𝑛], 𝑊𝑖𝑖= 0; 


As a summary, the estimation of the spatial lag binary logit model can be interpreted in the

level of influence of variable k on the severity outcome. The 𝜌 parameter is the only added 
parameter to be estimated compared to the other models specifications, and captures the

logit model, one can refer to (Klier & McMillen 2008). 
 
6.2.2 
Estimation 
The present section aims at describing the estimation procedures that are to be used for the 
multinomial logit, mixed multinomial logit and spatial lag binary logit models. 
6.2.2.1 
Multinomial logit model 
The multinomial logit model is to be estimated by a maximum likelihood procedure 
(Koppelman & Bhat 2006). In the first place, the likelihood function of the model is defined 
as:

6.2.2.2 
Mixed multinomial logit model 
As explained in the previous sections, the mixed multinomial logit model constitutes an 
extension of the MNL model, by introducing distributed 𝛽𝑠𝑘 parameters. The theoretical 
formulation of the model as shown in Eq. 16 cannot be estimated directly due to the several 
integrals involved. In order to overcome this issue, the MXL model is estimated thanks to 
simulation: 
 
A draw d is executed to extract values of  𝛽𝑠𝑘
̂  from their density distributions; 
 
Conditional on these values, the model is estimated in the same way as the standard 
MNL model, thus leading to the estimation of the probabilities 𝐿𝑠𝑖(𝛽𝑠𝑘
̂ 𝑑) as described 
in Eq. 15; 


probabilities averaged:

̂𝛿𝑠𝑖
𝑠∈𝑆
𝑖∈𝐼
 
Eq. 25: Simulated likelihood function 
 
Subsequently, the estimation of the maximum of the log of the simulated likelihood is 
executed in the exact same way as for the traditional multinomial logit. 
 
6.2.2.3 
Spatial lag binary logit model 
The estimation of the spatial lag logit model is significantly different compared to the 
previous models. Indeed, the inclusion of correlation among observations greatly increases 
the complexity of the model, thus leading to the impossibility to estimate it with the 
likelihood maximisation method. 
(Klier & McMillen 2008) develop a linearized generalized method of moments (linearized 
GMM) method to estimate the model. The GMM method is chosen because of its two main 
advantages over maximum likelihood estimation, namely the fact that it is not based on the 
hypothesis of normally distributed errors, and that it does not require the computation of 
determinants and inversion of n x n matrices. The linearization part of the estimation is 
compulsory in order to handle large samples as in our case, as the GMM procedure would 
be too computational demanding for important datasets (Klier & McMillen 2008).  
According to the formulation of (Klier & McMillen 2008), the model is linearized around the 
starting point for which 𝜌= 0, thus reducing to a traditional binary logit model. For this 
point estimate, β is estimated consistently with a standard logit model. Moreover, the 
matrix (𝑰−𝜌∙𝑾)−1 = 𝑰: no matrix inversion is then required. The utility function can be 
linearized around the initial estimate 𝜞𝟎= (𝜷𝟎, 𝜌0)′  by writing: 𝑢𝑖≈𝑢𝑖
0 −𝑮(𝜞−𝜞𝟎) where G 
is the gradient vector. Defining 𝑣𝑖= 𝑢𝑖
0 + 𝑮𝜞𝟎−𝑮𝜞 and Z a set of instruments (n x L), the 
objective function for the GMM estimator is written as (Klier & McMillen 2008: 
𝒗′𝒁(𝒁′𝒁)𝒁′𝒗 
Eq. 26: GMM estimator objective function 
 
The instruments Z correspond to a linear combination of the explanatory variables X and the 
weight matrix W, defined in a way that 𝑴= (𝒁′𝒁)−1 is definite positive. In this thesis, the 
instruments are employed in the same way as proposed by (Klier & McMillen 2008), and 
comprises a constant,  𝑿 and 𝑾𝑿: 𝒁= (𝑿, 𝑾𝑿). 
 
==PAGE:135==
135 
 
The implementation of the method previously presented is composed of two steps (Klier & 
McMillen 2008): 
 
First, the model is estimated by a standard logit model by setting 𝜌= 0, giving an 
estimation of the parameters 𝜷𝟎
̂. The utility function 𝑢0 is estimated, as well as the 
gradient terms for each β parameter and observation i, 𝑮𝜷𝒊= 𝑷𝒊
̂ ∙(1 −𝑷𝒊
̂) ∙𝑿𝒊 as well 
as the gradient for the ρ parameter 𝑮𝝆𝒊= 𝑷𝒊
̂ ∙(1 −𝑷𝒊
̂) ∙𝑯𝒊𝜷̂, where 𝑯= 𝑾𝑿. 
 
The gradients vectors 𝑮𝜷 and 𝑮𝝆 are then regressed on the set of instruments Z. The 
predicted values are 𝑮𝜷
̂  and 𝑮𝝆
̂ . Then, 𝑢0 + 𝑮′𝜷𝜷𝟎
̂  is regressed on 𝑮𝜷
̂  and 𝑮𝝆
̂ . The 
coefficients are the calculated values of β and ρ. 
In this estimation process, no large matrices have to be inverted, and the mechanism only 
requires the estimation of a standard logit model followed by a two-step least squares 
regressions. 
This linearized model estimation comes with some limitations. Given that the true structure 
of the model is described by Eq. 19, the linearization indeed provides accurate estimates as 
long as ρ is small. For a complete presentation of the model, one can refer to (Klier & 
McMillen 2008). 
 
6.2.3 
Goodness-of-fit 
 
After the models are estimated, measures are required to assess their quality. The following 
paragraphs present tools that are used to assess the overall fit of the models, as well as the 
level of significance of the variables included in the model specifications. 
6.2.3.1 
Model fit 
 
MNL and MXL models 
The overall fit of the multinomial models can be estimated through the log-likelihood value 
obtained in the model at convergence, 𝑙∗(𝜷). Indeed, as indicated in sections 6.2.2.1 and 
6.2.2.2, the MNL and MXL models are estimated by maximising the log-likelihood function. 
In turn, the higher the log-likelihood value, the better the model is. 
The model fit can also be expressed though the r² coefficient, also called coefficient of 
determination. It indicates the proportion of the variance in the dependent variable that can 
be explained by the independent variables. It is computed by using the values of the 
likelihood function at the start of the estimation 𝑙∗(0) and at convergence 𝑙∗(𝜷), and is 
expressed as: 
 
==PAGE:136==
136 
 
𝑟² = 1 −𝑙∗(𝜷)
𝑙∗(0) 
Eq. 27: Rho-square index 
 
Its value lies between 0 and 1, 1 indicating a full explanation of the variance of the 
dependent variable by the model (thus, the higher, the better). However, this value also 
depends on the number of parameters estimated in the model, due to the definition of the 
likelihood function. Therefore, the more parameters included in the model, the higher the r² 
value, even though the model poorly fits the data. As a consequence, the adjusted rho-
squared index 𝑟𝑎𝑑𝑗²  has been introduced, and gives a correction for the number of 
parameters k estimated in the model: 
 
𝑟𝑎𝑑𝑗² = 1 −𝑙∗(𝜷) −𝑘
𝑙∗(0)
 
Eq. 28: Adjusted rho-squared index 
 
Spatial lag binary logit model 
In the exact same ways as for the MNL and MXL models, the spatial lag binary logit model 
fit can be estimated through the log-likelihood value and the adjusted r² value. 
However, (Klier & McMillen 2008) propose a supplementary tool to estimate the fit of the 
spatial logit model, as well as the potential endogeneity of the Z instruments by using the 
Hansen J-test.  Indeed, the instrumental variables have to satisfy two requirements to be 
valid (Baum et al. 2003): 
 
They must be correlated with the included endogenous variables; 
 
They must be orthogonal to the error terms. 
The first condition can be verified during the first stage of the regression, when the 
endogenous variables are regressed on the instruments; more information about this 
assessment can be found in (Baum et al. 2003). The second condition of independence of 
the instruments Z from the error terms must however be verified by conducting an over-
identification test.  
Given L instruments in the matrix Z, L moments can be expressed as (Baum et al. 2003): 
∀𝑖∈[1: 𝑛],
𝒈𝒊(𝜷̂) = 𝒁𝒊′𝒗𝒊̂ = 𝒁𝒊′(𝒚𝒊−𝑿𝒊𝜷̂) 
Eq. 29: Instrumental variables moments 
 
Where 𝒈𝒊(𝜷̂)  is a (L x 1) column vector corresponding to a moment estimate at the 
 
evaluated parameters 𝜷̂; 
==PAGE:137==
137 
 
 
𝒁𝒊 is the (L x 1) column vector of the instruments values for observation I; 
 
𝒗𝒊̂  is the residual value from the estimated model for observation i. 
The exogeneity of the instruments (i.e. the independence of the instruments from the error 
terms) implies that there are L moments conditions, or orthogonality conditions, satisfied at 
the true value of β (Baum et al. 2003): 
𝐸{𝒈𝒊(𝛽)} = 0 
Eq. 30: Instrumental variables orthogonality conditions 
 
A global estimate of g is defined as (Baum et al. 2003): 
𝒈̅(𝜷̂) = 1

Eq. 31: Instrumental variables aggregated moments 
 
Therefore, the estimation of the model will aim at minimising the value of 𝑔̅. However, with 
L instruments and only K explanatory variables (L > K), the model is over-identified, and no 
guaranty of the independence of the instruments and the errors terms can be provided. 
Therefore, one can test the moment conditions as proposed in Eq. 30 to verify the 
orthogonality of the instruments from the error terms. 
The Hansen J-test examines the joint hypotheses of correct model specification and the 
orthogonality conditions. Under the null hypothesis that the over-identifying restrictions are 
valid, the J-test is Chi-squared distributed: 𝐽~𝜒𝑑

Eq. 32: Hansen J-test

Given the definition of the J-test, a value of 0 indicates a perfect fit of the model. Thus, the 
smaller the J-value, the better the test is. 
 
6.2.3.2 
Significance of parameters 
When including variables in the model, it is unsure whether these factors have a significant 
influence on the dependent variable. For this reason, two tests can be carried out to assess 
the level of significance of variables, namely the t-test and the p-value. 
T-test 
T-test can be used to evaluate the significance of a single parameter β against the value 0. 
The test is based on the null hypothesis that the parameter is equal to 0 (thus having no 
influence in the model): 
𝐻0: 𝛽= 0 
Eq. 35: T-test null hypothesis 
 
Given the estimated value of the parameter from the model, 𝛽∗, and its standard deviation 
𝑆𝛽, one can define the t-value, which can be approximated by a Normal distribution with 
mean 0 and standard deviation of 1 due to the central limit theorem (as the sample used to 
estimate the parameters is large): 
𝑡= 𝛽∗
𝑆𝛽
≈𝑁(0,1) 
Eq. 36: T-test value 
 
Lastly, the null hypothesis 𝐻0 is rejected at α significance level if 𝑡≥𝑡𝛼 or 𝑡≤− 𝑡𝛼, where 𝑡𝛼 
is defined such as : 𝑃[− 𝑡𝛼< 𝑋< 𝑡𝛼] = 1 −𝛼, 𝑋~𝑁(0,1). Thus, the T-test allows deciding 
whether the 𝛽∗ parameters are equal to 0 or significantly different from 0, with a predefined 
level of risk α. Usually, α is defined at 10, 5 or 1 % level. 
P-value 
The p-value is another measurement of the significance of the β parameters, directly linked 
to the t-test. It defines a null hypothesis that the parameter has a value of 0: 
==PAGE:139==
139 
 
𝐻0: 𝛽= 0 
Eq. 37: P-value null hypothesis 
 
The p-value then indicates the probability of wrongly rejecting the null-hypothesis; hence 
the probability of claiming that β is different from 0 whereas the null hypothesis is in fact 
true. It can directly be computed from the t-value, and is defined as: 
𝑝= 𝑃[𝑋< 𝑡 ∪𝑋> 𝑡], 𝑋~𝑁(0,1)  
Eq. 38: P-value 
 
Thus, the lower the p-value is, the better: in the present thesis, we consider a variable 
significant when its p-value is lower than 10 %. 
 
6.2.3.3 
Comparison between models 
It was mentioned in section 6.2.1.1 that the β parameters could be fixed across levels of 
severity for a given variable. However, the decision of defining s 𝛽𝑠𝑘 parameters for a given 
variable k or only one parameter 𝛽𝑘 for all severity levels must be based on a rationale. The 
likelihood ratio test thus allows this kind of decision, and it defined as follows: 
 
The model with the s 𝛽𝑠𝑘 parameters is estimated, thus leading to a log-likelihood 
estimate 𝑙∗(𝜷); 
 
The restricted model with the fixed 𝛽𝑘 parameter across alternatives is estimated, 
leading to a log-likelihood estimate 𝑙∗(𝜷𝒓𝒆𝒔); 
 
The likelihood ratio is then computed as indicated in Eq. 39. Given that there are S 
levels of severity, the full model has S - 1 more degrees of freedom than the 
restricted one; thus, the likelihood ratio test is Chi-squared distributed with S - 1 
degrees of freedom: 
𝐿𝑅= −2 ∙(𝑙∗(𝜷𝒓𝒆𝒔) −𝑙∗(𝜷)) ~ 𝜒𝑠−1
2
 
Eq. 39: Likelihood ratio 
 
 
Defining a level of risk α, the restricted model is accepted if 𝐿𝑅≤𝜒𝑠−1,   ∝
2
, where 
𝜒𝑠−1,   ∝
2
 is defined such that 𝑃[𝑋< 𝜒𝑠−1,   ∝
2
] = 1 −𝛼, 𝑋~𝜒𝑠−1
2
. 
To summarise, the likelihood ratio allows testing whether the full model is significantly 
different from the restricted one. If not, the restricted model is accepted, thus leading to a 
degenerated 𝛽𝑘 parameter across severity levels for variable k. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:140==

6.2.4 
Elasticities 
 
The outcome of the different models leads to the computation of the 𝛽𝑠𝑘 parameters. 
However, their interpretation may be difficult, as the signs of the parameters do not always 
determine the direction of the effect on the severity outcome because of the non-linear 
nature of the models (Prato et al. 2013). 
In order to overcome this issue, the model elasticities are computed. Indeed, elasticities aim 
at quantifying the changes in the probability distribution among the severity outcomes in 
response to changes in the values of the explanatory variables, as described in Eq. 40. For 
instance, an elasticity of 40% for the fatal outcome when shifting from sober to intoxicated 
pedestrian indicates that the probability for a pedestrian to be killed would be increased by 
40% compared to a sober one. Hence, elasticities are much easier to understand, and give 
information related to the magnitude of the effects of the different variables on the severity 
outcomes. 
𝜂= %  𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑛𝑔𝑒 𝑖𝑛 𝑠𝑒𝑣𝑒𝑟𝑖𝑡𝑦 𝑜𝑢𝑡𝑐𝑜𝑚𝑒 𝑝𝑟𝑜𝑏𝑎𝑏𝑖𝑙𝑖𝑡𝑦
% 𝑐ℎ𝑎𝑛𝑔𝑒 𝑖𝑛 𝑒𝑥𝑝𝑙𝑎𝑛𝑎𝑡𝑜𝑟𝑦 𝑣𝑎𝑟𝑖𝑎𝑏𝑙𝑒
 
Eq. 40: Elasticity definition

written as:

6.2.4.2 
Categorical variable elasticity 
The case of categorical variables needs to be treated separately. Indeed, categorical 
variables are by definition discrete: therefore, they are not differentiable and the previously 
mentioned elasticity definition does not hold. In order to overcome this issue, direct pseudo-
elasticities are calculated as the percentage change in severity probability outcome when a 
variable 𝑋𝑖𝑘 is shifted from the reference category  R (𝑋𝑖𝑘𝑅) to another category C (𝑋𝑖𝑘𝐶)  
(Prato et al. 2013): 
𝜂𝑋𝑖𝑘𝐶
𝑃𝑠𝑖
= 𝑃𝑠𝑖[𝑋𝑖𝑘𝐶] −𝑃𝑠𝑖[𝑋𝑖𝑘𝑅]
𝑃𝑠𝑖[𝑋𝑖𝑘𝑅]
 
Eq. 43: Categorical variable pseudo-elasticity definition 
 
In a similar manner as for the continuous variables, the direct pseudo-elasticities are 
computed for each severity level s and each crash observation i, and then averaged over the 
whole sample. 
 
6.2.5 
Implementation 
 
Different software packages are employed in order to implement the previously presented 
models. 
 
6.2.5.1 
MNL and MXL models 
Both the multinomial logit and the mixed multinomial logit models are implemented by the 
means of the software BIOGEME, an open source freeware designed for the maximum 
likelihood estimation of parametric models developed at the EPFL (Ecole Polytechnique de 
Lausanne) by Michel Bierlaire (Bierlaire 2003). An example of BIOGEME code is provided in 
appendix (page 247). 
 
6.2.5.2 
Spatial lag binary logit model 
The spatial lag binary logit model is implemented thanks to the R package MCSpatial, 
developed by Daniel McMillen and available online (McMillen 2013). The package comprises 
an implementation of the estimation method developed by (Klier & McMillen 2008). An 
example of the R code is provided in appendix (page 248). 
 
 
 
==PAGE:142==
142 
 
6.3 Models elaboration 
6.3.1 
Considered dataset 
 
The datasets presented in section 4 constitute the data input for the three severity models. 
However, the small sample size observed in the hit-and-run data represents a major issue, 
preventing the estimation of the severity models. 
As a consequence, only the no hit-and-run dataset is considered in the severity models. 
 
6.3.2 
Dependent variable 
 
Unlike the LCA model, which focused on the frequency of the accidents, the dependent 
variable in this section is the severity level of the accident. Four outcomes are possible, as 
stated in section 6.2.2.1, which are no injury, light injury, severe injury and fatality. 
Because of the structure of the logit models, one severity outcome is to be taken as a 
reference: indeed, one is interested in factors explaining the shift from one severity level to 
another. For that reason, the “no injury” level is arbitrarily taken as a reference: its utility 
function is set to 0 and no parameters are estimated for this alternative. The other three 
alternatives (light injury, severe injury and fatality) are then compared to the no injury 
outcome.  
In the case of the spatial binary logit model, the dependent variable has to be recoded so 
that only two alternatives exist. Therefore, the outcomes for this model are defined as 
follows: 
 
No/light injury (taken as reference); 
 
Severe injury/fatality. 
 
6.3.3 
Explanatory variables 
 
The variables included in the dataset presented in section 4.2 are used as inputs. However, 
several adaptations have to be carried out in order to comply with the specifications of the 
logit models. 
 
6.3.3.1 
Continuous variables 
Due to the structure of the LCA model, all continuous variables had to be transformed into 
categorical ones. However, this constraint does not hold for logit models, for which 
continuous variables are allowed. As the shift from continuous to categorical variables may 
==PAGE:143==
143 
 
lead to a loss of information by reducing the set of values that the variable can take, it 
seems beneficial to move back to continuous variables when feasible.  
When possible, the continuous and categorical versions of the variables are tested in the 
model. Moreover, scaling and log transformation of the continuous variables are tested. 
 
6.3.3.2 
Categorical variables 
The categorical variables used in the LCA analysis cannot be directly used in the logit 
models. Indeed, the estimation of the 𝛽𝑠𝑘 parameters requires that each category within a 
given variable is considered as an independent variable. Therefore, each categorical variable 
comprising C categories is transformed in C independent binary variables. 
Moreover, in a similar way as for the dependent variable, one category among the C 
outcomes of a given variable is to be taken as a reference, to which the other categories are 
compared. Indeed, one is interested in the effects of shifting from one category to another, 
therefore leaving C - 1 degrees of freedom. The choice of the reference category may be a 
difficult task in itself. Even though this choice should not alter the results of the models, it 
may still influence the ease of results interpretations. Three strategies may be adopted for 
its definition: 
 
Choosing the category for which the number of observations is the largest; 
 
Choosing the category representing a “normal” situation: for instance, it would make 
sense to consider a sober pedestrian as a normal situation thus taking it as the 
reference, to which the effect of intoxication are compared; 
 
In the case of a variable representing a numerical value, the mean or the extreme end 
categories can be taken as a reference. For instance, for income distribution, one can 
take the median income category as a reference, hence comparing the effects of 
high and low incomes to median ones. For speed, one could take the lowest speed 
category as a reference, thus comparing the effect of increased speeds to the lowest 
one. 
Lastly, the sample size within each category may be really small, hence making it difficult 
for the model to estimate the parameter for this given category. Therefore, categories may 
have to be grouped in order to produce larger samples, thus leading to more consistent 
estimates of parameters. 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:144==
144 
 
6.3.4 
Model specifications 
 
Like the LCA analysis, the severity models would suffer from an excess of variables 
included in the estimation. Indeed, too many parameters could lead to the overfitting of the 
model, thus preventing its estimation. 
As a consequence, a selection among the variables has to be provided. Two approaches can 
be adopted: 
 
Establishing a first model with a reduced set of vital variables (gender of pedestrian, 
speed, type of vehicle for instance), followed by a step-wise inclusion of other 
significant variables; 
 
Establishing a first model including all variables, followed by a step-wise elimination 
of insignificant variables. 
The first approach is followed in the present thesis. Starting from a voluntarily reduced 
model, variables are progressively added and tested for significance. The process of 
including a new variable can be described as follows and summarised in Figure 47: 
 
The categorical form of the variable is first considered; 
 
A reference category is chosen; 
 
The significance of the variable is tested by looking at the p-values of the 𝛽𝑠𝑘 
parameters  as described in section 6.2.3.2; 
 
If all the categories are not significant, several tries are conducted to change the 
reference category, or group categories; 
 
If some categories are still not significant, the degenerated 𝛽𝑘 parameter, equalised 
across severity level, is tested. The significance of this formulation is tested thanks 
to the likelihood ratio test as described in section 6.2.3.3; 
 
If the variable also has a continuous version, it is also tested in the model as well as 
its logarithm form; 
 
Based on all the previous simulations and the results of the significance tests (p-
value and likelihood ratio test), the variable is either kept or discarded from the final 
model. 
==PAGE:145==
145 
 
 
Figure 47: Variable selection process 
 
6.3.5 
Distributed parameters 
 
In the mixed multinomial logit model, the choice of parameters to be distributed is driven by 
the following consideration. For variables that are found partially significant in the 
multinomial logit model, the non-significant categories are tested as distributed parameters. 
The improvement of the p-value for the parameter, as well as the p-value of the standard 
deviations are used to choose to include or not the distributed parameter. 
 
6.3.6 
Weight matrices 
 
In the spatial logit model, several specifications of the weight matrix as indicated in section   
6.2.1.3 are tested. The models are compared to a simple logit model, and the choice of the 
best weight matrix is driven by: 
 
The value of the ρ parameter; 
 
Its significance, shown by the p-value; 
 
The improvement of model fit in terms of log-likelihood.

6.4 Multinomial logit model 
6.4.1 
Elaboration 
 
As stated in the previous section, several trials have been carried out before achieving a 
good specification of the model.  
Due to the small sample size of the hit-and-run sample, it has been impossible to estimate 
an MNL model for this dataset. Thus, only the no hit-and-run sample has been considered. 
A significant number of degenerated 𝛽𝑘 parameters are included in the final model, after 
being tested for relevance thanks to the likelihood ratio test. Moreover, four continuous 
variables are included, instead of their categorical versions.  
 
6.4.2 
Results 
 
Table 19 summarises the estimated parameters for the MNL model, while Table 20 shows 
the model elasticities. Thereafter, the different results are analysed based on the observed 
elasticities.

Table 20: Multinomial logit elasticities 
 
==PAGE:151==
151 
 
6.4.2.1 
Pedestrian characteristics 
Different conclusions can be drawn from the pedestrian characteristics. First of all, the 
pedestrian gender is not found significant, hence failing to validate the conclusion of over-
representation of males in severe and fatal pedestrian accidents, drawn in many studies and 
reviewed by (Zegeer & Bushell 2012).  
The age distribution of pedestrian victims reveals interesting results. Indeed, age effect is 
found to be significant for all age categories, and almost every level of severity. Young 
children below 14 years old to face lower risk of light, severe injuries and fatalities 
compared to the reference category of adults between 30 and 59 years old (elasticities of -
7.5, -7.2 and -5.8 %). An important decrease in the likelihood of severe and fatal injuries is 
observed for teenagers between 15 and 19 years old (-18.1 % and -53.9 %), thus making 
this age category the safest and providing contradictory conclusions compared to previous 
studies ((Abdel-Aty et al. 2007), (Koopmans et al. 2015), (Niebuhr et al. 2016)). Indeed, 
children would be expected to constitute an exposed population, due to their fragile physical 
characteristics. As suggested in the literature, elderly citizens above 60 years old face a 
major increased risk of suffering from severe injuries and fatalities, probably due to their 
physical and cognitive weaknesses ((Zajac & Ivan 2003), (J. K. Kim et al. 2008), (Niebuhr 
et al. 2016)). Moreover, the risk of fatality is clearly increased between the 60 – 69 years 
age category (elasticity of 104.1 %) and elderly above 70 years old (215.2 %).  
As expected, pedestrian alcohol consumption is correlated with an increase of the accident 
severity level with a limited effect (elasticities of 7.9 % and 5.7 % for the severe and fatal 
outcomes), confirming the results of previous studies ((Öström & Eriksson 2001), (Lee & 
Abdel-Aty 2005), (Clifton et al. 2009)). Indeed, alcohol alters the pedestrian’s cognitive 
abilities, thus increasing the risk of dangerous behaviour leading to severe accidents. 
The pedestrian behaviour is more difficult to interpret, as many categories are not 
significant. The reference category of road crossing is found to be more dangerous 
compared to walking along the road (elasticity of -33.6 % for fatality). However, 
pedestrians crossing from a hidden place (for instance hidden by a vehicle) face a higher risk 
of severe injuries (25.0 %), which can be explained by the incapacity of the driver to predict 
the dangerous situation and try to avoid the pedestrian.  (Ulfarsson et al. 2010) analyse this 
factor in their studies, by deriving two models for pedestrian and driver’s fault. In case of 
pedestrian’s fault, crossing has indeed been identified as the most dangerous situation, 
followed by pedestrian crossing from a hidden place, thus confirming the results from the 
present model. 
Lastly, the pedestrian nationality is found significant. Surprisingly, foreigners are less likely 
to suffer from light and severe injuries compared to Danes (-9.8 % and -15.1 %), while no 
significant difference is concluded regarding fatalities. These results may suggest that 
==PAGE:152==
152 
 
pedestrians are more prudent when walking in a less familiar environment, such as a foreign 
country. 
6.4.2.2 
Vehicle characteristics 
The vehicle type is found to have a great influence on the severity level sustained by the 
pedestrians. As expected, light vehicles such as bikes and mopeds are associated with a 
decrease in risk of severe injuries (-15.9 % and -28.2 %) and fatalities (-79.3 % and -88.8 
%) compared to cars. Interestingly, the observed decreases for mopeds are greater than for 
bikes. In the contrary, vans show an increased risk in light (7.9 %), severe (7.2 %) and fatal 
categories (5.5 %) compared to cars, as previously observed by (Ballesteros et al. 2004). 
Trucks are associated with a major increase in the risk of fatalities (173.2 %), as observed 
by (J. K. Kim et al. 2008), which can be explained by the important weight of these 
vehicles as well as the absence of passive pedestrian safety elements in their design 
(vertical engine hood for instance). Lastly, the influence of busses is not found to be 
significantly different from cars.  
 
6.4.2.3 
Driver characteristics 
Several drivers’ characteristics are observed in the model. First of all, male drivers 
significantly increase the likelihood of severe (7.5 %) and fatal accidents (32.6 %) 
compared to female, confirming results observed by (J. K. Kim et al. 2008). Thus, male 
drivers may be associated with a more dangerous behaviour, or to a less attention to the 
road traffic. 
The age category of drivers also reveals interesting results. Compared to the reference 
category of middle-aged adults (25 – 60 years old), young drivers below 25 years old 
appear to incur a slightly higher risk of light (4.4 %), severe (4.1 %) and fatal accidents (2.9 
%). In contrast, drivers above 60 years old are found to slightly decrease the risk of severe 
injury (-1.8 %) and significantly lower the risk of fatality (-42.7 %), suggesting that older 
drivers are more prudent than other age categories, in line with the observation of (J. K. 
Kim et al. 2008). This may be explained by the lower cognitive abilities of elderly drivers 
compared to younger ones. Indeed, old drivers may feel unsafe when driving, as they may 
have difficulties to process all the elements of information surrounding them (traffic lights, 
directions, other vehicles, etc.). In turn, they may drive more slowly, thus reducing the risk 
of having an accident. 
Driver’s intoxication is, as expected, an aggravating factor. Surprisingly, the factor is not 
found significant for severe injury; however, it induces a major increased risk of fatality 
(113.3 %) as indicated by (J. K. Kim et al. 2008). This can easily be explained by the 
longer driver’s reaction when intoxicated which alters his/her judgment. 
==PAGE:153==
153 
 
The driver’s action before the crash has also been investigated. Compared to the reference 
category of driving straight ahead, left, right turn as well as reversing reduce the risk of 
severe injuries (respectively – 38.3, - 20.1 and -6.7 %) and fatalities (-35.5, -56.9 and -5.0 
%). Turning left is thus highlighted as the safest situation regarding the risk of fatality, 
while turning right is found to be the safest in terms of severe injury. Lastly, reversing is 
only identified to have a rather limited effect, with elasticities lower than 10 %.The results 
are similar to those found by (J. K. Kim et al. 2008). The fact that turning would be safer 
than driving straight ahead is quite understandable. Indeed, this manoeuver implies more 
attention to the road traffic for the driver; moreover, it implies a lower speed compared to 
going straight ahead.  
 
6.4.2.4 
Road characteristics 
Diverse aspects of road characteristics are investigated. In the first place, the road type 
does not show clear results, with categories that are not significant for all severity levels. 
Major local roads are found to increase the risk of light injuries (7.6 %) and more 
importantly of fatalities (52.4 %) compared to minor roads. Regional roads do not yield any 
significant results, while motorway/express/national roads increase the likelihood of severe 
(5.0 %) and fatal accidents (24.4 %), due to their higher speed limits. Thus, major local 
roads are found to be the most dangerous road category.  
Road location does not yield information regarding the effect of intersection compared to 
road sections. However, curves greatly increase the likelihood of severe 16.4 %) and fatal 
accidents (60.5 %), in line with findings of (Ulfarsson et al. 2010). Indeed, curves may be 
associated with difficulties for the driver to detect the presence of a pedestrian. 
Lastly, the speed limit is found to be greatly significantly. Compared to a speed limit up to 
50 km/h, the two categories 50 – 80 and above 80 km/h indeed remarkably increase the 
likelihood of severe (13.2 % and 20.9 %) and fatal accidents (104.2 % and 176.7 %) as 
previously studied by (Kröyer 2014). This is easily understandable, as a higher speed implies 
a higher energy released during the crash, thus dramatically increasing its severity. 
 
6.4.2.5 
Environmental characteristics 
Roadside environment provides significant results. The reference category of absence of 
buildings in the vicinity of the accident is found to be the riskiest environment. Indeed, 
residential, low buildings decrease the probability of severe (-5.3 %) and fatal accidents (-
3.9 %), as well as industrial areas (-9.3 and – 37.5 %). Lastly, shopping streets are found 
to be the safest areas, with a -7.2 % decrease for severe injuries and -48.4 % for fatalities. 
These results are intelligible, as the roadside environment may be directly linked to the 
speed limit of the considered road (e.g. no building being associated with rural roads; 
shopping streets associated with low speed roads, etc.) Lastly, the results comply with the 
==PAGE:154==
154 
 
observations of (Ukkusuri et al. 2012) which note an increased risk of severe accidents in 
open land, while residential areas are associated with a decrease.  
Time characteristics are also analysed. The weekend indicator variable is not significant, on 
the contrary of the time period. Night time is found to be the most dangerous for all 
accident outcomes (elasticities of 12.3, 15.7 and 56.5 % for light, severe and fatal 
outcomes) compared to the reference time of 09:00 – 15:00, as observed by (Eluru et al. 
2008) and (Ulfarsson et al. 2010). Contrariwise, the 09:00 – 15:00 time period is the 
safest one. Morning peak is not significant, while afternoon peak slightly increases the 
likelihood of severe accidents (7.9 %). Lastly, evening time greatly raise the risk of fatality 
(54.9 %). These results are comprehensible, as night time may be associated with a lower 
visibility for the driver, thus leading to an increased risk of not seeing a pedestrian on the 
road. 
Directly linked to time characteristics, dark road are found to greatly increase the likelihood 
of a fatality (104.0 %) compared to daylight or illuminated roads. This is particularly 
understandable, as pedestrians are most likely not visible on dark roads: thus, the driver 
may not be able to attempt a manoeuver to avoid the pedestrian, hitting him/her at full 
speed.  
Road surface conditions are not found significant. 
A geographical study of the accidents reveals meaningful results. Compared to the region of 
Copenhagen, Zealand, Fyn and South Jutland are found to reduce the likelihood of light and 
severe injuries in the same magnitude. However, Central Jutland slightly increases the risk 
of fatality compared to Copenhagen (8.5 %). Last, and most importantly, the region of 
North Jutland faces a major increased risk of light (49.5 %), severe (5.5 %) and fatal 
accidents (42.4 %), and is by far shown as the most dangerous region of Denmark.  Thus, 
North Jutland should be specifically targeted regarding pedestrian safety policies. 
The study of the rural index confirms the conclusions drawn regarding the regional 
distribution of risks. Indeed, remote areas are found to increase the likelihood of light (11.1 
%), severe (10.0 %) and fatal accidents (7.2 %) compared to urban municipalities. Rural 
areas also increase the likelihood of light (29.4 %) and severe accidents (4.6 %). Thus, 
these municipalities should be specifically targeted regarding pedestrian safety. 
The land use diversity showed by the Herfindahl-Hirschman index reveals intelligible results, 
associated with negative elasticities for the three severity outcomes. It indicates that the 
more diverse the area, the higher the severity of the accident. This conclusion is 
contradictory with previous studies ((Zahabi et al. 2011), (Clifton et al. 2009)), but can 
however be defended. Indeed, a uniform environment would suggest less information to 
analyse for both the driver and the pedestrian, thus leading to an easier identification of 
==PAGE:155==
155 
 
potential risks. In the contrary, a really diverse area, with different landscapes and building 
shapes may distract the driver and/or the pedestrian from the danger of the road, and 
therefore may increase the likelihood and severity of accidents. 
As suggested by previous studies such as (LaScala et al. 2000), the unemployment rate in 
the area around the accident is associated with higher severity levels of accidents, with 
positive elasticities of 22.2, 16.7 and 24.8 % respectively for light, severe and fatal 
accidents. This may indicate that deprived populations are less educated regarding road 
safety, thus increasing the likelihood of having an accident due to a dangerous behaviour. 
Moreover, poor citizens may be more likely to use cheaper means of transport such as 
walking or public transport instead of driving a car, thus increasing their exposure. 
If the road traffic volume is not found significant, and therefore not included in the model, 
one can however observe that the walk traffic volume has an important impact on the 
severity level. As suggested by (Elvik 2016), one can indeed observe a safety-in-number 
effect: the higher the walk density, the lower the severity level of the accident, through the 
negative elasticities yielded for the three different severity levels. This would indicate that 
the more visible the pedestrians are due to their traffic volume, the more prudent the drivers 
would be. 
Lastly, the distance from an accident to the nearest hospital is significant for fatalities (2.2 
%). The non-relevance of this variable for lower severity levels can be easily understood, as 
a light accident does not imply a hospitalisation. In the contrary, the survival chance of a 
pedestrian in a more severe crash may depend on the possibility to quickly reach emergency 
services. The observed sign of the elasticity related to the distance to the hospital is 
meaningful: indeed, it indicates that the larger the distance to the hospital, the higher the 
risk of fatality, in line with previous conclusions drawn by road safety researchers (Mueller 
et al. 1988). 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:156==
156 
 
6.5 Mixed multinomial logit model 
6.5.1 
Elaboration 
 
 
The mixed multinomial logit model is built as an extension of the fixed effect MNL model 
developed in the previous section. Therefore, the results from the previous model are taken 
as a starting point. The elaboration of the MXL model is as follows: 
 
No new variables are added; 
 
Non-significant parameters in the MNL are changed to distributed parameters and 
tested for significance (p-value); 
 
The alternative-specific constants are also tested as distributed parameters. 
Due to memory limitations of the software used, only 500 draws have been used to 
estimate the model, while 1000 draws is often stated as a minimum acceptable value. 
 
6.5.2 
Results 
 
 
The results of the MXL model are shown in Table 21, while the elasticities are shown in 
Table 22. The model includes 8 distributed parameters, and significantly improves the 
goodness-of-fit of the model compared to the MNL: indeed, a likelihood ratio test conducted 
between the two models indicates a value of 31.258, which is found significant at the 
99.9% level for Chi-squared distribution with 8 degrees of freedom (𝜒8,   𝛼=99.9%
2
= 26.1).

The conclusions drawn for the mixed logit model do not differ from the MNL model as 
described in section 6.4.2:  indeed, the included variables are the same, while the signs and 
significance of the different parameters are confirmed.  Only the magnitudes of the 
elasticities are corrected, with little observed variation compared to the MNL model. One 
interesting result can however be uncovered by the MXL model: the weekend variable 
becomes almost significant (p-value=0.17), and seems to indicate a higher severity level of 
accidents during weekends compared to weekdays. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:162==
162 
 
6.6 Spatial lag binary logit model 
6.6.1 
Elaboration 
 
The estimation of the spatial lag model implies to start over the elaboration process of the 
model. Indeed, the injury outcomes have to be split between no injury/light injury and severe 
injury/fatality: only one utility function has to be estimated. Therefore, the variables that are 
likely to be significant may be different compared to the MNL and MXL models. 
A similar procedure as for the MNL model is developed. A simple binary logit model is 
estimated by progressively adding variables and testing them for significance. When the 
final model is ready, the spatial effect is added by deriving the spatial lag model including a 
weight matrix as presented in section 6.2.1.3. Among the different weight matrices tested 
as indicated in Table 23, the best fit is found with the simple inverse of the distance 
between observations: indeed, the highest log-likelihood is achieved with this specification, 
as well as the lowest p-value for the ρ parameter.

Table 25: Spatial logit model elasticities 
 
It can be seen that the introduction of a spatial term as shown in Table 24 does alter the 
estimation of the parameters and is found greatly significant (p-value=0.03): therefore, the 
relevance of taking into account spatial correlation is proven. 
The J-test indicates a value of 0.134, relatively close to 0, thus proving the quality of the 
regression instruments employed, as well as the model fit. 
 
6.6.2.1 
Pedestrian characteristics 
In a similar way as for the MNL and MXL models, the pedestrian gender is not found 
significant, both in the logit and spatial model. It thus confirms the non-relevance of this 
factor on the severity level sustained by the pedestrian in the considered dataset.  
The age distribution reveals similar results as for the previous models: compared to the 
reference category of 30 – 59 years old, young pedestrians face a lower probability of 
having a severe/fatal accident, with a decrease of probability by -35.2 % for the 20 – 29 
years old age category. In the contrary, older pedestrians face a much higher risk of 
severe/fatal accidents, with almost a doubled risk for pedestrians aged over 70 compared to 
the reference category (97.1 %). The spatial correlation here shows its relevance, with a 
much higher magnitude of the elasticities compared to the simple logit model, hence 
pointing out the high risk associated with elderly pedestrians. 
The pedestrian intoxication shows its association with an increased probability of 
severe/fatal injury by 37.4 % compared to a sober pedestrian. 
As for the MNL and the MXL, not all the categories for the pedestrian behaviour are 
significant. Compared to a crossing pedestrian, a pedestrian crossing from a hidden place 
increases the probability of having a severe/fatal accident by 20.9 %, while standing on the 
road would lead to a decreased probability by 20.2 %. 
==PAGE:169==
169 
 
Lastly, as for the previous models, foreign pedestrians are found to face a lower probability 
of suffering from a severe/fatal accident compared to Danish pedestrians. 
 
6.6.2.2 
Vehicle characteristics 
The vehicle characteristics are found to follow the same patterns as for the MNL and MXL 
models. Bikes and mopeds decrease the risk of having a severe/fatal accident (-38.9 % and 
-53.0 %), and confirms the fact that mopeds are associated with less severe accidents than 
bikes. Vans and busses are not found significant; however, heavy vehicles yield a 38.3 % 
increase of severe/fatal accidents. 
 
6.6.2.3 
Driver characteristics 
As for the MNL and the MXL models, the driver gender is found significant. Indeed, male 
drivers increase the likelihood of a severe/fatal accident by 19.1 % compared to female 
drivers. 
The age distribution also shows similar results compared to the previous models: if the 
driver age category 0 – 25 years old is still not found significant, elderly drivers lower the 
risk of severe/fatal accidents by -13.8% compared to the reference category of 25 – 60 
years old. 
Surprisingly, driver’s intoxication is not found significant in the spatial logit model, which 
differs from the conclusions of the MNL and MXL models conclusions. 
The driver’s behaviour is found significant for all categories. Compared to going straight, 
turning right is found to be the safest action, with a decreased risk by -52.9%. Reversing 
only reduces the likelihood of severe/fatal accident by -14.6%, thus being found to be 
riskier than turning left or right. This conclusion is somewhat counter-intuitive, as reversing 
would be expected to happen at very low speed, thus reducing the risk of severe accident 
compared to turning. However, it is consistent with the conclusions of the MNL and MXL 
models. 
 
6.6.2.4 
Road characteristics 
The road type does not yield any significant results. Indeed, road type has already been 
quite difficult to interpret in the MNL and MXL model with the four outcome categories. 
Moving to a binary outcome probably increased the uncertainty relative to the effect of the 
road type on the severity level of the accidents, thus leading to non-significant parameters 
estimates. 
==PAGE:170==
170 
 
The road location is found to be partially significant. Compared to a straight road section, an 
accident occurring in a curve increases the risk of a severe/fatal outcome by 39.5%; the 
influence of an intersection compared to a road section is not found significant. 
As for the MNL and MXL models, the speed limit is found to have a great influence on the 
severity level of the accidents. It can then be noted that the higher the speed, the higher 
the probability of having a severe/fatal accident: for instance, a crash on a road with a 
speed limit higher or equal to 80 km/h yields an increased risk of severe/fatal outcome by 
71.6% compared to a crash at a speed lower than 50 km/h. 
Finally, the roadside environment is found significant for all categories. Shopping streets are 
found to be the safest areas (-26.4 %) compared to the absence of roadside buildings, 
found to the riskiest roadside type. Residential areas with tall buildings yield a -12.4 % 
decrease in the risk of severe/fatal accidents, while residential, low building exhibit a           
-20.7  % decrease compared to the absence of roadside buildings. 
 
6.6.2.5 
Environment characteristics 
Seasonal effects are observed, indicating that the autumn period is likely to decrease the 
risk of severe/fatal accident by -16.8% compared to the spring/summer period. Weekends 
are not found to yield a different risk compared to weekdays. 
The time period is found significant. Compared to the reference category of 09:00 – 15:00, 
night time from 21:00 – 06:00 is found to yield a higher risk of severe/fatal accident, with 
an increased risk of 23.3%. 
Light conditions have an impact on the severity level. Compared to daylight, darkness is 
found to be the riskiest lighting condition, with an increased likelihood of severe/fatal 
accidents by 36.4% compared to daylight. Illuminated roads are found to constitute an 
intermediate situation, with an increased risk of 23.8% compared to daylight. 
The visibility conditions yield more information compared to the MNL and MXL models. 
Indeed, bad visibility decreases the likelihood of severe/fatal accidents by -13.5%, 
suggesting that drivers are more prudent in bad visibility conditions. In the contrary, wet 
roads are found to increase the risk by 9.7%, which could be supported by the degraded 
braking and manoeuvring conditions in case of a wet road. 
Likewise the MNL and MXL models, the regions of occurrence of the accidents are found to 
have an effect on their severity levels. North Jutland is found to be the most dangerous 
area in Denmark, with an increased risk of severe/fatal accident by 21.8% compared to 
Copenhagen, followed by Central Jutland with an elasticity factor of 13.3%. In the 
contrary, Zealand is the safest area of Denmark, with an elasticity of -12.4%. 
==PAGE:171==
171 
 
The rural index of the municipality is not significant, at the exception of the intermediate 
municipalities, which are found to decrease the risk of a severe/fatal accident by -17.8% 
compared to urban municipalities. 
The share of low speed roads within 500 m around the accident is significant and included 
in the spatial logit model, which was not the case in the previous models. It is concluded 
that a higher share of low speed roads would lead to a decreased risk of severe/fatal 
accidents, thus proving the effectiveness of low speed roads regarding pedestrian safety. 
However, the elasticity of -2.6% indicates that the magnitude of this effect is minor. 
Unemployment rate exhibits a much more important elasticity: indeed, its elasticity is found 
to be of 11.4%, thus indicating that deprived areas are facing a higher risk of severe injuries 
compared to other places in Denmark. 
As for the MNL and the MXL models, the walk density is significant, and confirms the 
safety-in-numbers effect, with an elasticity of -2.9%. 
Lastly, the distance to the hospital is not found to be significant in the spatial logit model, in 
the contrary of the previous models. This may be explained by the need to group the severe 
and fatal outcomes together, which may have hidden the significance of this variable. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:172==
172 
 
6.7 Summary 
 
Three severity models have been developed in this section. First of all, the multinomial logit 
model constituted the basis model, being used to test the significant variables as well as 
providing a point of reference. The mixed multinomial logit model aimed at filling the 
methodological gaps of the MNL model, by allowing parameters to be distributed among 
observations. It proved its relevance by significantly improving the model fit. Lastly, the 
spatial logit model aimed at alleviating the issue of spatial correlation among observations, 
and also highlighted the relevance of taking into account this issue. 
The most important results revealed that elderly pedestrians were the most exposed to 
severe accidents, while intoxication (for the pedestrian or the driver) was associated with a 
higher risk of severe accidents. The most dangerous actions prior to the accidents were 
found to be crossing from a hidden place for pedestrians and driving straight ahead for 
drivers. Heavier vehicles were linked with more severe accidents. The road characteristics 
revealed major local roads as being the most dangerous road category, with an increased 
risk of severe accidents in case of a high speed limit and the presence of curves. The light 
conditions pointed out night time and unlit roads as being the most dangerous 
circumstances. Geographical considerations revealed rural and remote municipalities as 
being more exposed to severe accidents compared to the rest of Denmark, with an 
important over-exposure of North Jutland. In a similar way, areas located far from a hospital 
were found to be greatly exposed to fatal accidents. Lastly, exposure measures showed 
that deprived areas of Denmark were facing a greater risk of severe accidents, while areas 
with a high pedestrian activity were found to be safer compared to the rest of the country. 
 
 
==PAGE:173==
173 
 
7. Discussion 
 
 
The LCA analysis as well as the severity models unveiled interesting results regarding 
pedestrian safety in Denmark. In order to get a broad view of these accidents, the two 
modelling techniques are combined. Thus, each cluster is carefully examined and specific 
policy implications proposed. Comparing the results of three severity models would however 
be confusing. Therefore, the MXL model is chosen as the primary source of information in 
this section, as it provides a better model fit compared to the MNL model and keeps the 
four severity levels outcomes in contrast with the spatial lag binary logit model.  
 
7.1 No hit-and-run clusters 
7.1.1 
Cluster C1 
 
No hit-and-run cluster C1 
Urban,  road section, sober driver going straight ahead, car,  dense city centre, 
sober elderly pedestrian, crossing, weekday, daylight, dry road 
19.8 % of the whole sample 
No injury 
Light injury 
Severe injury

46.6 % 
16.5 % 
33.0 % 
3.9 % 
Table 26: No hit-and-run cluster C1 overview 
==PAGE:174==
174 
 
7.1.1.1 
Cluster composition and severity level 
Cluster C1 is the largest cluster unveiled in the no hit-and-run sample, comprising 19.8 % of 
the observations. It involves sober elderly pedestrians (> 70 years) crossing a road section 
in urban dense residential areas, hit by sober drivers going straight ahead in cars. The 
cluster reveals an average severity level which is in line with the values found in the whole 
sample. Indeed, 3.9 % of the accidents in this cluster lead to a fatality, 33.0 % to severe 
injury, 16.5 % to a light injury and 46.6 % to no injury. Table 27 summarises the different 
elements characterising the cluster as  well as factors influencing its severity level. 
The presence of elderly pedestrians over 70 years old is particularly worrying, as all the 
severity models indicated that this population is particularly exposed to severe road 
accidents. Indeed, the MXL model indicates an elasticity of 214.2 % for fatality, and 29.9 
% for severe injuries compared to no injury state and the 30 – 59 years category, which 
may be explained by the physical and cognitive weaknesses of elderly pedestrians. The 
pedestrians’ soberness can be seen as a mitigating factor compared to intoxication. Lastly, 
the pedestrians’ action of crossing is obviously found as one of the most dangerous action 
compared to walking on the pavement or the road, as the pedestrians are directly in contact 
with the road traffic.  
The pedestrians are hit by cars, which is the most common vehicle type in the whole 
dataset and not found to influence the severity level of the accident. The presence of male 
drivers is associated with an increased likelihood of severe accidents, with an 8.2 % 
increased risk of severe accidents compared to female driver and a 31.1 % increase for fatal 
accidents. The soberness of the drivers is found as a mitigating factor compared to 
intoxication. The drivers’ action to drive straight ahead is the most common action in the 
sample and is the most dangerous action compared to reversing or turning. Lastly, the 
drivers are found not to have any duty to give way to the pedestrians. 
Most accidents occur on medium and minor local roads, which are found to mitigate the 
severity of the accidents and are the safest road types according the severity model. Most 
of these roads are two-way undivided with one lane per direction, and situated in urban 
residential areas, that are found to yield a medium severity level compared to other roadside 
building environments.  Lastly, accidents belonging to cluster C1 occur on straight road 
sections yielding a medium severity level compared to other road layout, and a speed limit 
of 50 km/h which is found the safest speed category compared to higher ones.  
Accidents belonging to cluster C1 mostly occur on weekdays, with daylight, a good 
visibility, good weather and dry roads that provide the best conditions to mitigate the 
severity of these accidents.
