Most of these accidents are located within 5 km of a hospital, thus being in the average of 
the whole sample. Moreover, these accidents are mostly located in urban municipalities 
according to the rurality index, yielding a medium severity level according to the MXL 
model. 
The population density in the vicinity of the accidents is quite high (1000 – 5000 
inhabitants/km²) and characterised by a rather high unemployment rate, which increases the 
severity level of the accidents according to the MXL model (elasticities of 19.3 % and 29.5 
% for the severe and fatal outcomes). 
 Lastly, exposure factors indicate a rather high road (10000 – 25000 veh.km/km²/day) and 
walk traffic volumes (1000 – 10000 walk.km/km²/day), which mitigates the severity level 
of accidents due to the safety-in-number effect (elasticities of -4.1 % and -6.2 % for the 
severe and fatal outcomes).  
 
7.1.1.2 
Spatial distribution 
The spatial distribution of these accidents indicates that they mostly occur in the large and 
medium cities of Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense, Esbjerg, Roskilde, 
Viborg, etc.). Interestingly, these accidents do not occur in the inner part of Copenhagen, 
but only in the suburbs as suggested by the population density observed in the cluster. 
 
 
7.1.1.3 
Proposals 
As a summary and reminder, cluster C1 contains accidents involving sober elderly 
pedestrians (> 70 years) crossing a road section in urban dense residential areas, hit by  
sober drivers going straight ahead in cars. 
Elderly pedestrians are known to be particularly exposed to road accidents. Indeed, their 
capacity to analyse the road traffic may be reduced compared to younger pedestrians, as 
suggested by (Gorrie et al. 2008). Moreover, the high road and walk traffic volume may 
affect the judgment of these elderly pedestrians, as they could be disoriented by the high 
activity on the roads. This population is also over-exposed due to its low speed when 
crossing, as well as its poor strategy to avoid the road traffic as implied by (Oxley et al. 
1997). Lastly, elderly pedestrians account for a large share of pedestrians: in 2008, 
(Bernhoft & Carstensen 2008) revealed that 85 % of the elderly Danes are walking in their 
hometown as a weekly basis. In turn, these pedestrians may create a dangerous situation, 
by crossing unexpectedly the road at low speed and represent a major issue revealed by the 
size of the cluster (19.8 % of the total accidents). 
Several suggestions can be provided to tackle the issue unveiled in cluster C1: 
==PAGE:177==
177 
 
 
Elderly people tend to cross in a dangerous way: (Bernhoft & Carstensen 2008) 
indicates that only 30 % of elderly Danes walk up to a crossing when seeing them. 
Education should be provided to change the behaviour of elderly pedestrians, in order 
to encourage them to use pedestrian crossings. This would be particularly relevant as 
most of accidents in cluster C1 occur in deprived areas of Denmark (high 
unemployment rate), which are proven to greatly increase the exposure to road 
accidents and may be partially explained by a lack of education; 
 
Elderly people may also cross the road outside pedestrian crossing in order not to 
make a detour which could be physically demanding for them (Bernhoft & 
Carstensen 2008). Therefore, more crosswalks could be implemented to limit the 
walking distance of elderly to the nearest crossing; 
 
Elderly pedestrians are also found to be greatly sensitive to the road surface quality 
(Bernhoft & Carstensen 2008). Thus, providing elevated crossings could improve the 
safety in several ways: by lowering the speed of the vehicles, but also by providing a 
smooth surface from sidewalk to sidewalk, which is found as an important safety 
factor for old pedestrians (Bernhoft & Carstensen 2008). Moreover, it would reduce 
the risk of elderly pedestrians falling onto the road due to road surface irregularities, 
thus lowering the risk of accident; 
 
The crossing time of elderly pedestrians should also be investigated. If a large share 
of accidents in cluster C1 is reported on minor local streets, the larger roads could be 
equipped with islands at mid-way. This would allow elderly pedestrians to rest and 
not hurry when crossing the road. Moreover, it would help them to better judge the 
traffic situation: indeed, elderly would only have to check one direction of road 
traffic at a time, hence reducing the crossing complexity for them; 
 
Most of accidents in cluster C1 occur in residential areas. Thus, lowering the speed 
in these zones may be a policy to investigate. Indeed, traffic calming roads with a 
speed of 30 km/h would both reduce the risk of accidents by allowing drivers and 
pedestrians to better assess the road traffic situation and anticipate danger, as well 
as greatly reducing the severity levels caused to elderly pedestrians, as suggested by 
(Niebuhr et al. 2016). 
 
Lastly, the spatial distribution of the accidents belonging to cluster C1 clearly targets 
specific cities of Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense, Esbjerg, Roskilde, 
Viborg, etc.) where to focus attention on this specific issue. 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:178==
178

7.1.2.1 
Cluster composition and severity level 
Cluster C2 is somewhat similar to C1 in its crash composition. However, the land use and 
socio-economic factors clearly differ, and call for different conclusions. 
Cluster C2 involves sober young (< 15 years) and elderly (> 70 years)  pedestrians 
crossing a road section in low dense residential areas, hit by sober drivers going straight 
ahead in cars. The cluster reveals an average severity level which is in line with the values 
found in the whole sample. Indeed, 3.5 % of the accidents in this cluster lead to a fatality, 
33.7 % to severe injury, 19.8 % to a light injury and 43.1 % to no injury. Table 29 
summarises the different elements characterising the cluster as  well as factors influencing 
its severity level. 
==PAGE:179==
179 
 
The presence of elderly pedestrians over 70 years old is worrying in a similar way is for 
cluster C1 and constitutes an aggravating factor for the severity level of the accidents. In 
the contrary, the presence of children below 15 years old tends to lower the severity level, 
with elasticities of -7.7 % and – 6.0 % for the severe and fatal outcomes compared to the 
reference age of 30 – 59 years. The pedestrians’ soberness represents a mitigating factor 
compared to intoxication. Lastly, the pedestrians’ action of crossing is found to the most 
dangerous action as for cluster C1. 
The pedestrians are hit by cars, the most common vehicle type in the whole dataset. The 
presence of male drivers increases the likelihood of severe accidents, with elasticities of 8.2 
% for severe accidents and 31.1 % for fatal ones compared to female. The cluster also 
reveals an over-representation of drivers over 50 years, which is found to mitigate the 
severity level of the accidents. Indeed, drivers over 60 years yield -2.7 % and -43.5 % 
decreases regarding severe and fatal outcomes compared to the reference age category of 
25 – 60 years. The soberness of the drivers acts as a mitigating factor compared to 
intoxication. The driver’s action to drive straight ahead is the most common action in the 
sample and leads to the same conclusions as drawn for cluster C1. Lastly, the drivers are 
found not to have any duty to give way to the pedestrians. 
Most accidents occur on medium and minor local roads, which are the safest road types 
according the severity model. Most of these roads are two-way undivided with one lane per 
direction, and situated in urban residential areas, yielding a medium severity level compared 
to other roadside building environments.  Lastly, cluster C2 contains accidents occurring on 
straight road sections yielding a medium severity level compared to other road layout, and a 
speed limit of 50 km/h which is found the safest speed category compared to higher ones.  
Cluster C2 reveals accidents that mostly occur on weekdays, with daylight, a good 
visibility, good weather and dry roads, providing the best conditions to lower the severity 
level of these crashes.  
Most of these accidents are located within 5 km of a hospital, thus being in the average of 
the whole sample. Moreover, these accidents are over-represented in rural and remote 
municipalities according to the rurality index, which lead to an increased risk of severe 
accidents (4.7 % and 8.6 % respectively). Besides, South and Mid Jutland regions are over-
represented in the cluster, mitigating the risk of severe (-16.4 %) and fatal accidents (-17.2 
%) in South Jutland compared to Copenhagen region. Lastly, a low HH index is reported in 
the cluster, thus indicating a great land use diversity increasing the likelihood of severe 
accidents (elasticities of -16.7 % and -25.5 % for the severe and fatal outcomes).

The population density in the proximity of the crashes is medium (100 – 1000 
inhabitants/km²) and characterised by a rather low unemployment rate, which mitigates the 
severity level of the accidents (elasticities of 19.3 % and 29.5 % for the severe and fatal 
outcomes). 
 Lastly, exposure factors indicate a rather low road (5000 – 10000 veh.km/km²/day) and 
walk traffic volumes (100 – 1000 walk.km/km²/day), which increases the severity level of 
accidents due to the absence of safety-in-number effect in the contrary of cluster C1.  
 
7.1.2.2 
Spatial distribution 
The spatial distribution of these accidents indicates that most of them occur outside the 
major cities of Denmark (few accidents in Copenhagen, Aarhus or Odense) and are primarily 
located in residential areas in smal cities/towns of Denmark. 
 
 
7.1.2.3 
Proposals 
As a summary and reminder, cluster C2 comprises accidents involving sober young (< 15 
years) and elderly (> 70 years)  pedestrians crossing a road section in low dense residential 
areas, hit by sober drivers going straight ahead in cars. 
As for cluster C1, elderly pedestrians are known to be particularly exposed to road 
accidents. The presence of young pedestrians below 15 years old in the same cluster 
suggests that this age category may share characteristics regarding their exposure to the 
road traffic. Indeed, both age groups may suffer from cognitive issues: if elderly pedestrians 
may wrongly assess the safety of crossing due to some diseases or declining cognitive 
abilities, young pedestrians may have not integrated the risk of crossing a road due to their 
age. Therefore, both young and elderly pedestrians may adopt a risky behaviour when 
crossing the road. Physical impairment of elderly may also be a source of problems, as 
indicated in cluster C1. Moreover, children may also suffer from physical limitations: due to 
their small height, they may not be clearly visible for the road users, nor they would be able 
to see the road traffic before crossing. Several countermeasures can thus be proposed to 
alleviate the burden of cluster C2: 
 
Education for children should be enforced, in order to make them aware of the 
danger of the road. This could be done through the Danish organisation Rådet for 
Sikker Trafik (Council for Safe Traffic) which provides road safety education in 
Denmark, with a special focus on children (Rådet for Sikker Trafik 2016b). Education 
schemes may also be extended towards the elderly pedestrians, as this population is 
known to adopt a dangerous behaviour when crossing (Bernhoft & Carstensen 
2008); 
==PAGE:182==
182 
 
 
Most of accidents in cluster C2 occur in residential areas with low vehicle volume 
roads, along with a rather low walk volume. Thus, this could create a false 
impression of safety due the absence of traffic. Signs reminding the potential 
presence of pedestrians to road drivers could be placed along these roads. Similarly, 
pedestrian signs warning to watch the traffic could also be implemented, for instance 
with paintings on the sidewalks in front of the crossings. Lastly, these signs may be 
equipped with flashing lamps in order to catch the drivers’ attention, as they are 
likely to be distracted due to the high land use diversity (HH < 0.5); 
 
Visual obstacles next to crossings (barriers, traffic signs, parked cars, etc.) should be 
limited. This would help children to be seen, and help them to better assess the 
danger of crossing; 
 
The visibility of pedestrians and their ability to watch the traffic before crossing 
could also be improved with a curb extension. This would allow the pedestrians to 
better view the traffic and be better viewed by road users before crossing, but also 
to reduce the speed of road users; 
 
Elevated crossings would, as for cluster C1, lower the speed of the road users and 
provide a smoother road surface for the pedestrians; 
 
As for cluster C1, and due to the occurrence of the accidents in residential areas, 
speed limit could be lowered to 30 km/h, as suggested in the literature (Preston 
1995). Moreover, low speed zones have already proven their effectiveness in small 
residential areas of Denmark (Mertner & Jørgensen 1998); 
 
Lastly, the driver’s distraction due to the great land use diversity could be tackled by 
implementing an Autonomous Emergency Braking system (AEB) in cars. This system 
is indeed part of the vehicle safety assessment in Europe performed by the Euro 
NCAP since 2015 (Schram et al. 2015), and aims at supplementing the driver’s 
action in case of an imminent risk of hitting an obstacle by automatically braking the 
vehicle.  
Finally, the spatial distribution of the accidents in cluster C2 is somewhat diffuse: many 
residential areas spread all over Denmark seem to require attention regarding this specific 
issue. 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:183==
183

Table 30: No hit-and-run cluster C3 overview 
 
7.1.3.1 
Cluster composition and severity level 
Cluster C3 involves sober young (< 15 years) and elderly (> 70 years) pedestrians hit in 
low dense residential areas by sober reversing drivers. The cluster reveals a higher than 
average severity level, with 6.5 % of fatalities compared to 5.1 % in the whole sample. 
Table 31 summarises the different elements characterising the cluster as  well as factors 
influencing its severity level. 
The presence of elderly pedestrians over 70 years old is worrying as for clusters C1 and C2 
and constitutes an aggravating factor regarding the severity of accidents. Children below 15 
years old tend in the contrary to lower the severity level, as previously indicated in cluster 
C2. The pedestrians’ soberness mitigates the severity level of accidents compared to 
==PAGE:184==
184 
 
intoxication. Lastly, the pedestrians’ action of being hit by a reversing car mitigates the 
severity level of the accident.  
The pedestrians are hit by cars, the largest vehicle type in the whole sample. Male drivers 
increase the risk of severe accidents as for the previous crash patterns C1 and C2. The 
soberness of the drivers reduces the severity level compared to intoxication. The drivers‘ 
action of reversing is found to mitigate the risk by -7.5 % and -5.5 % for the severe and 
fatal outcomes compared to driving straight ahead, which can be explained by the low 
speed implied by this manoeuver. Lastly, the drivers do not have any duty to give way to 
the pedestrians. 
Accidents from cluster C3 occur on medium and minor local roads, the safest road types 
according the severity model. Most of these roads are two-way undivided with one lane per 
direction, and located in urban residential, low building areas, leading to a medium severity 
level compared to other environments.  In addition, cluster C3 comprises accidents 
occurring on straight road sections implying a medium severity level compared to other road 
layout, as well as a speed limit of 50 km/h being part of the safest speed category 
compared to higher ones. Lastly, the residential zones are characterised by the absence of 
low speed roads in the vicinity of the accidents, thus increasing the severity level of the 
accidents according to the spatial logit model (elasticity of -2.6 %). 
Cluster C3 contains accidents that mostly occur on weekdays, with daylight, a good 
visibility, good weather and dry roads, providing safe conditions that lower the severity level 
of accidents.  
Most of these crashes are located more than 10 km of a hospital, thus increasing the risk of 
fatality (elasticity of 2.3 %). The accidents are over-represented in rural and remote 
municipalities, which are linked to an increased risk of severe injury accidents (4.7 % and 
8.6 % respectively). Zealand, Fyn and South Jutland regions are over-represented, thus 
lowering the risk of severe accidents (respectively -23.1 %, -15.5 % and -16.4 %) 
compared to Copenhagen.  
The population density in the surroundings of the crashes is low (<100 inhabitants/km²) 
and characterised by a medium unemployment rate. 
 Lastly, exposure factors exhibit a very low road (<5000 veh.km/km²/day) and walk traffic 
volumes (<100 walk.km/km²/day), which greatly increases the severity level of accidents 
due to a false sensation of security.

7.1.3.2 
Spatial distribution 
Mapping cluster C3 crashes reveals that these accidents are evenly spread all over Denmark 
in residential areas of small towns. No accident is reported in the major cities of the 
country. 
 
 
7.1.3.3 
Proposals 
Summarising, cluster C3 involves sober young (< 15 years) and elderly (> 70 years) 
pedestrians hit in low dense residential areas by sober reversing drivers. It also represents 
the second most severe cluster unveiled in the analysis, with a high fatality rate of 6.5 %. 
Several countermeasures can be proposed to alleviate this issue: 
 
Education targeting drivers could also be enforced, in order to make them aware of 
the risk of reversing. This could for instance be done via advertising campaigns 
implemented by the Council for Safe Traffic (Rådet for Sikker Trafik 2016b); 
 
The low vehicle and walk volume certainly creates a false impression of safety. As a 
consequence, road signs could be implemented to remind drivers the potential 
presence of pedestrians; 
 
Vehicles could be equipped in a similar way as for busses and trucks with audible 
warning devices when reversing. A second option could be to generalise the 
equipment of cars of reversing sensors, in order to help the driver during the 
manoeuver. This could be in line with the European regulations which tend to include 
more and more safety equipment on board of vehicles (seat belts, ABS, air-bags, 
passive safety equipment…) and suggested by the Danish Road Safety Commission  
(Danish Road Safety Commission 2013); 
 
The distance to the nearest hospital may also greatly contribute to the severity level 
of accidents observed in cluster C3, which could probably be reduced with faster 
emergency services. For instance, automatic emergency call devices installed in cars 
could provide a faster reaction time by immediately triggering a call in case of 
accident. The inclusion of such a system is timely and relevant, as it will be 
mandatory in every new car model in the European Union from the 31st of March 
2018 (The European Parliament 2016). 
Lastly, the spatial distribution of the accidents within cluster C3 does not allow to 
distinguish specific locations in Denmark where to focus attention, as these accidents are 
quite evenly spread all over the country. 
 
 
==PAGE:187==
187 
 
7.1.4 
Cluster C4

Urban,  road section, intoxicated driver going straight ahead, bike, 
sober female pedestrian crossing,  weekday, daylight, dry road 
5.4 % of the whole sample

27.3 % 
0.9 % 
Table 32: No hit-and-run cluster C4 overview 
 
7.1.4.1 
Cluster composition and severity level 
Cluster C4 involves sober female pedestrians hit by 20 – 29 years intoxicated bike riders on 
urban road sections while crossing (from a hidden place). The cluster reveals the lowest 
severity level, with only 0.9 % of fatalities compared to 5.1 % in the whole sample. Table 
33 summarises the different elements characterising the cluster as  well as factors 
influencing its severity level. 
The cluster reveals a majority of female pedestrian involved. The pedestrians’ soberness 
mitigates the severity of the crashes compared to intoxication. However, the pedestrians’ 
action crossing from a hidden place increases the risk of severe injuries compared to other 
actions (24.8 %). 
==PAGE:188==
188 
 
The pedestrians are hit by bikes, which greatly reduces the risk of severe (-15.0 %) and 
fatal outcomes (-79.6 %) and can be explained by the low speed and weight of bikes 
compared to cars. As in the previous clusters, male riders increase the risk of severe. An 
over-representation of the age category 20 – 29 years can be found among these bike 
riders. The intoxication also constitutes an aggravating factor compared to sober ones, with 
an elasticity of 113.9 % for the fatal outcome. The riders’ action of going straight ahead is 
found to yield the highest risk level compared to other actions. Lastly, an over-
representation of riders crossing a red traffic light can be uncovered in the cluster. 
Cluster C4 is composed of accidents occurring on major local roads which are the most 
dangerous roads compared to minor local roads (49.5 % elasticity for the fatal outcome). A 
higher than average of these roads are two-way divided with two or more lanes per 
direction, and located in urban residential, tall building areas implying a medium severity 
level compared to other roadside environments. Cluster C4 also comprises accidents 
occurring on straight road sections as well as cycle tracks leading to a medium severity 
level compared to other road layout. The speed limit of 50 km/h yields the lowest severity 
level compared to higher speed categories. Lastly, a high share of low speed roads can be 
found in the vicinity of these accidents, thus lowering the severity level (-2.6 %) according 
to the spatial logit model. 
Cluster C4 relates to accidents that mostly occur on weekdays, with daylight, a good 
visibility, good weather and dry roads, providing safe conditions that lower the severity level 
of accidents. Moreover, an over-representation of the time period 09:00 – 18:00 can be 
observed in the cluster. 
Most of the crashes are located less than 1 km of a hospital, thus mitigating the risk of 
fatality (elasticity of 2.3 %). The accidents are mostly located in urban municipalities, which 
medium severity level. Copenhagen is also the major region found in the cluster, yielding an 
average severity level compared to other regions.   
The population density in the vicinity of the crashes is extremely high (>5000 
inhabitants/km²) and characterised by a high unemployment rate which increases the risk of 
severe (19.3 %) and fatal outcomes (29.5 %). 
 Lastly, exposure factors reveal a high road (25000 – 50000 veh.km/km²/day) and walk 
traffic volumes (> 10000 walk.km/km²/day), which decreases the risk of severe (-4.1 %) 
and fatal outcomes (-6.2 %) due to the safety-in-number effect.

7.1.4.2 
Spatial distribution 
The spatial pattern of cluster C4 reveals that accidents occur in large cities of Denmark 
(Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense), as well as few smaller cities (Kolding, Silkeborg, 
Slagelse, etc.). 
 
 
7.1.4.3 
Proposals 
In brief, cluster C4 involves sober female pedestrians hit by 20 – 29 years intoxicated bike 
riders on urban road sections while crossing (from a hidden place). Several countermeasures 
can be proposed to tackle this crash pattern: 
 
Education targeting young bike riders (20 – 29 years old) could be provided, by 
focusing on the risks associated with alcohol consumption and safety rules on the 
roads. This education scheme would be especially relevant as most accidents occur 
in deprived areas of Denmark (high unemployment), which are known to increase the 
risk of severe accidents. 
 
Signs warning pedestrians of the potential presence of bikes could be implemented. 
Indeed, pedestrians may focus their attention on bigger vehicle and not bikes when 
crossing; 
 
A significant share of accidents involves bikes crossing a red light on cycle tracks. 
More visible traffic lights as well as advanced stop lines for bike lanes should be 
implemented, in order to make bikes stop and reduce the risk of crashes; 
 
Accidents in cluster C4 also involve pedestrians crossing from a hidden place, thus 
not using the designed crossing places. Guardrails could be implemented in order to 
direct pedestrians to preferred crossing locations, as reported by (Fischer et al. 
2010); 
 
Alcohol controls focusing on bike riders in the major cities of Denmark could be 
implemented by the police. Dissuasive fines may also contribute to this goal of 
reducing the alcohol consumption for bike riders as suggested by the Danish Road 
Safety Commission (Danish Road Safety Commission 2013). 
 
Lastly, the spatial distribution of cluster C4 clearly indicates that these measures should 
focus on large cities of Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense) as well as cities 
such as Kolding, Silkeborg or Slagelse. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:191==
191

7.1.5.1 
Cluster composition and severity factors 
Cluster C5 involves sober female pedestrians hit by young (< 20 years) intoxicated moped 
riders on urban road sections while walking on the pavement. The cluster reveals the 
second lowest severity level, with only 1.4 % of fatalities compared to 5.1 % in the whole 
sample. Table 35 summarises the different elements characterising the cluster as  well as 
factors influencing its severity level. 
The cluster shows a majority of female pedestrian. The pedestrians’ soberness lower the 
severity of the crashes compared to intoxication. The pedestrians are mostly reported as 
walking on the pavement. 
==PAGE:192==
192 
 
The pedestrians are mainly hit by mopeds, which significantly lowers the risk of severe (-
32.6 %) and fatal outcomes (-89.4 %) and may be explained by a lower speed and weight 
compared to cars. As usual, male riders increase the risk of severe. An over-representation 
of the age category 0 – 19 years can be found among the moped riders. The intoxication of 
riders also constitutes an aggravating factor compared to sober ones, with an elasticity of 
113.9 % for the fatal outcome. The riders are mostly reported as jumping off the road. 
Lastly, no duties to give way are reported in the cluster. 
The majority of accidents in cluster C5 take place on medium and minor local roads which 
are the safest road categories. These roads are in majority two-way undivided with one lane 
per direction, and located in urban residential areas implying a medium severity level 
compared to other roadside environments. Cluster C5 also contains accidents occurring on 
straight road sections as well as cycle tracks leading to an average severity level compared 
to other road layout. A higher than average share of roads with a speed limit < 50 km/h is 
reported in the cluster, thus lowering the severity of the accidents.  
Cluster C5 comprises accidents that mostly occur on weekdays, with daylight, a good 
visibility, good weather and dry roads, thus leading to optimal conditions that mitigate the 
severity of the accidents. Moreover, an over-representation of the spring / summer period 
can be observed in the cluster. 
Most of the crashes are located within 5 km of a hospital, being in the average of the whole 
sample. The accidents are over-represented in intermediate and rural municipalities, which 
increase the severity level of accidents (respectively 4.7 % and 8.6 % for the severe 
outcome compared to Copenhagen).  
The population density surrounding the crashes is high (100 – 5000 inhabitants/km²) and 
characterised by a medium unemployment rate. 
 Lastly, exposure factors reveal a medium road (10000 – 25000 veh.km/km²/day) and walk 
traffic volumes (100 – 1000 walk.km/km²/day). 
 
7.1.5.2 
Spatial distribution 
Accidents belonging to cluster C5 are located out of the centres of the major cities in 
Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense and Esbjerg), but also in smaller cities 
(Fredericia, Næstved, Viborg, etc.).

==PAGE:194==
194 
 
7.1.5.3 
Proposals 
In short, cluster C5 involves sober female pedestrians hit by young (< 20 years) intoxicated 
moped riders on urban road sections while walking on the pavement. Several 
countermeasures can be proposed to tackle this issue: 
 
Riding a small moped in Denmark only requires a driving license for people between 
16 – 18 years (AM category); from 18 years, no license is required to drive such 
vehicles (Rigspolitiet 2016). Moreover, the license test is known to be relatively easy 
to take. As a consequence, stronger and mandatory driving license regardless of the 
age of the rider should be enforced regarding mopeds. A stronger focus on the risk 
associated with alcohol should be provided during the theoretical phase of the 
license, while the practical phase should insist on the ability of the rider to control 
his/her vehicle (i.e. limiting the risk of jumping off the road); 
 
More alcohol controls targeting moped riders in large and medium cities of Denmark 
should be implemented by the police, with a special focus on youngest drivers. 
Moreover, fines as well as licenses with penalty points should provide a mean of 
better ensuring the respect of the rules for these road users; 
 
Mopeds are allowed to drive on cycle lanes in Denmark. If the purpose of this 
measure is to protect moped riders from heavier vehicles, this can in contrast expose 
pedestrians walking on the sidewalks to higher risks. Indeed, cycle lanes are often 
found at the same level as the sidewalks; thus, no physical separation protects the 
pedestrians from the traffic on the cycle lane. In turn, kerbs or other physical 
elements such as barriers could be installed to physically separate cycles lanes and 
sidewalks, which would limit the risk of a moped to jump off the cycle lane as 
recommended by (Gitelman et al. 2012). 
Lastly, the spatial distribution of cluster C5 indicates that a specific attention should be 
given to this crash type in the suburbs of the major cities of Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, 
Aalborg, Odense and Esbjerg) as well as smaller cities such as Fredericia, Næstved or 
Viborg. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:195==
195

Table 36: No hit-and-run cluster C6 overview 
 
7.1.6.1 
Cluster composition and severity factors 
Cluster C6 involves young (20 – 29 years) intoxicated pedestrians crossing an urban 
intersection, hit by sober car or bus drivers going straight ahead. The cluster shows an 
average severity level which is in line with the values found in the whole dataset. Indeed, 
3.8 % of the accidents in this cluster lead to a fatality, 33.3 % to severe injury, 17.1% to a 
light injury and 45.8 % to no injury. Table 37 summarises the different elements 
characterising the cluster as  well as factors influencing its severity level. 
The cluster reveals the over-representation of pedestrians aged 20 – 29 years, which 
significantly reduces the severity level of the accidents (-22.8 % and -27.6% for the severe 
and fatal outcomes compared to the reference age group of 30 – 59 years). The 
==PAGE:196==
196 
 
pedestrians’ intoxication in the contrary increase the risk of severe accidents, with a 9.6 % 
increase for severe accidents and 6.8 % for the fatal ones compared to sober pedestrians. 
The pedestrians’ action of crossing is found as one of the most dangerous action compared 
to other actions. Lastly, the presence of foreign pedestrians tends to lower the risk of 
having severe accident (-16.5 %). 
The pedestrians are hit by cars or busses. The presence of male drivers, as in all the 
previous clusters, is associated with an increased likelihood of severe and fatal accidents. A 
higher than average share of drivers is aged 30 – 39 years. The soberness of the drivers 
constitutes a mitigating factor compared to intoxication. The drivers’ action of driving 
straight ahead is the most common action in the sample. Lastly, the cluster reveals an over-
representation of drivers crossing the intersection with a green traffic light. 
Most accidents occur on major local roads, which are found to be the most dangerous 
roads, with an elasticity of 49.5 % for the fatal outcome compared to minor local roads. A 
significant share of these roads are one-way roads with two or more lanes per direction, and 
situated in urban residential, tall buildings or shopping streets areas, that are found to be the 
safest roadside environment (shopping streets exhibit -7.6 % for the severe and -47.4 % for 
the fatal outcome compared to an absence of roadside building).  Lastly, accidents 
belonging to cluster C6 occur on intersections with a speed limit of 50 km/h.  
Accidents belonging to cluster C6 mostly occur on weekdays, with daylight, a good 
visibility, good weather and dry roads which are good conditions to lower the severity level 
of the accidents. 
Most of these crashes are located within 5 km of a hospital, thus being in the average of 
the whole sample. Moreover, these accidents are mostly located in urban municipalities 
according to the rurality index, yielding a medium severity level according to the MXL 
model. Lastly, a majority of the accidents are located in the Copenhagen region, thus 
leading to an average severity level compared to other regions of Denmark. 
The population density in the vicinity of the accidents is very high (> 5000 inhabitants/km²) 
and characterised by a rather high unemployment rate, which increases the severity level of 
the accidents (elasticities of 19.3 % and 29.5 % for the severe and fatal outcomes). 
 Lastly, exposure factors indicate an extreme high road (> 50000 veh.km/km²/day) and 
walk traffic volumes (> 10000 walk.km/km²/day) lowering the severity of the accidents 
due to the safety-in-number effect (elasticities of -4.1 % and -6.2 % for the severe and fatal 
outcomes).

==PAGE:198==

7.1.6.2 
Spatial distribution 
The spatial distribution of cluster C6 reveals that the accidents are only located in the inner 
parts of the four largest cities of Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg and Odense). 
 
 
7.1.6.3 
Proposals 
Concisely, cluster C6 involves young (20 – 29 years) intoxicated pedestrians crossing an 
urban intersection, hit by sober car or bus drivers going straight ahead. Moreover, these 
pedestrian can be supposed to cross illegally the intersection, as a significant part of the 
cluster is related to driver crossing the intersection with a green light when going straight 
ahead. Several countermeasures can be proposed to tackle this issue: 
 
Education for bus drivers could be provided in order to make them aware of the risk 
of pedestrian crossing illegally at intersections, in a similar way as proposed by 
(Mohamed et al. 2013) in New York for truck drivers; 
 
Enforcing alcohol tests as well as education for pedestrians, targeting 20 – 29 years 
age category, in the four largest cities of Denmark. In turn, this would reduce the 
risk of having intoxicated pedestrians in the streets having a dangerous behaviour; 
 
A significant share of accidents belonging to cluster C6 occurs on signalized 
intersections. Several improvements in the intersection design could be provided, 
such as installing guardrails to direct pedestrians to preferred crossing locations, as 
proposed by (Davies 1999).  
 
Intoxicated pedestrians are not likely to wait for the green light to cross, as 
suggested by the high share of driver found hitting a pedestrian despite having a 
green light. Automatic detection of pedestrian could be implemented, in order to 
automatically trigger the traffic light and reduce the waiting time for pedestrians. 
Time countdowns before green light could also be implemented as well as audible 
warning devices indicating the red/green state of the light, in order to incite the 
pedestrian to wait for the green light. Last, in-pavement light emitting red/green 
markers on sides of the crosswalk could incite the pedestrians to only cross when 
permitted, as suggested by (Gitelman et al. 2012). 
 
Drivers may also be incited to slow down at intersections, by reducing the speed 
limit. As a significant share of accidents belonging to cluster C6 involves roads with 
two or more lanes, it could also be investigated the reduction of number of lanes. 
 
As for cluster C6, Autonomous Emergency Braking system (AEB) could be 
implemented in vehicles to reduce the risk of drivers hitting a pedestrian in case of 
sudden and unexpected dangerous situation (e.g. pedestrian crossing illegally). 
 
Last, the spatial distribution of cluster C6 indicates that efforts regarding this specific issue 
should be focused in the city centres of Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg and Odense. 
==PAGE:199==
199

Table 38: No hit-and-run cluster C7 overview 
 
7.1.7.1 
Cluster composition and severity factors 
Cluster C7 involves sober female pedestrians crossing an urban intersection by bad weather 
conditions, hit by sober drivers turning left in cars. The cluster reveals an average severity 
level which is in line with the values of the whole sample. Indeed, 3.4 % of the accidents in 
this cluster lead to a fatality, 25.7 % to severe injury, 18.2 % to a light injury and 52.7 % 
to no injury. Table 39 summarises the different elements characterising the cluster as  well 
as factors influencing its severity level. 
A majority of pedestrians involved in cluster C7 are females. The pedestrians’ soberness 
represents a mitigating factor compared to intoxication. Pedestrians are primarily found 
crossing, which is found to be the most dangerous action as for the previous clusters. 
==PAGE:200==
200 
 
The pedestrians are hit by cars, in line with the characteristics of the whole dataset. The 
presence of male drivers increases the likelihood of severe accidents as stated in all 
previous clusters. The soberness of the drivers acts as a mitigating factor compared to 
intoxication. The drivers’ action of turning left is found to significantly reduce the risk of 
severe (-20.3 %) and fatal accidents (-57.3 %) compared to driving straight ahead, which 
can be explained by the lower speed implied by this manoeuver. Lastly, the drivers are 
found to turn with a green traffic light. 
Most accidents occur on major local roads, which are the most dangerous roads compared 
to minor local ones (elasticity of 49.5 % for the fatal outcome). Most of these roads are 
two-way divided with two lanes or more per direction, and situated in urban residential 
areas, yielding a medium severity level compared to other roadside building environments.  
Lastly, cluster C7 contains accidents occurring on intersections with an over-representation 
of a speed limit between 51 – 79 km/h which is found to be an aggravating factor 
compared to lower speeds (elasticities of 13.5 % and 105.5 % for the severe and fatal 
outcomes). 
Cluster C7 reveals accidents that mostly occur on weekdays, with illuminated roads, which 
significantly increases the risk of a severe and fatal accident (elasticity of 23.8 % compared 
to daylight according to the spatial logit model), which may be explained by the lower 
visibility of the pedestrian. The cluster also indicates a bad visibility and bad weather. Roads 
are also reported to be wet, thus increasing the risk of severe/fatal accident by 9.7 % 
according to the spatial logit model, which can be easily explained by the degraded braking 
conditions. Lastly, a significant share of the accidents is reported to occur in the autumn / 
winter period which is found to reduce the risk of a severe/fatal accident according to the 
spatial logit model (-16.8 %), along with an over-representation of the time period 06:00 – 
09:00. 
Most of these accidents are located within 5 km of a hospital, thus being in the average of 
the whole sample. Moreover, these accidents are mostly located in urban municipalities 
according to the rurality index, yielding an average severity level.  
The population density in the proximity of the crashes is relatively high (1000 – 5000 
inhabitants/km²) and characterised by a medium unemployment rate. 
 Lastly, exposure factors indicate a medium road (10000 – 25000 veh.km/km²/day) and 
walk traffic volumes (1000 - 10000 walk.km/km²/day).

7.1.7.2 
Spatial distribution 
According to the spatial distribution of the accidents, cluster C7 is found in the major cities 
of Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Odense, Aalborg and Esbjerg) as well as few smaller 
cities (Viborg, Horsens, Slagelse, Næstved, etc.).  
 
7.1.7.3 
Proposals 
Shortly, cluster C7 involves sober female pedestrians crossing an urban intersection by bad 
weather conditions, hit by sober drivers turning left in cars. Several countermeasures can be 
proposed to reduce the number of these accidents: 
 
Education for drivers could be provided, in order to warn them to watch pedestrians 
when turning left. This could for instance be done through advertising campaigns as 
suggested by (Fischer et al. 2010); 
 
Flashing lamps could also be implemented to remind the presence of pedestrian 
crosswalk to drivers when turning; 
 
Accidents in cluster C7 occur on illuminated roads. Thus, the visibility of pedestrians 
could be improved with a better lighting system over the pedestrian crossing, as 
proposed by  (Zegeer et al. 2005); 
 
The bad weather conditions associated with the illumination also limit the visibility of 
pedestrians due to the high reflectivity of the road. In turn, the visibility of 
pedestrians could be improved by implementing new road surfaces with a lower 
reflectivity, as proposed by (Kaplan & Prato 2013); 
 
The autumn/winter period along with the bad weather conditions may significantly 
alter the braking conditions, due to the presence of leaves, snow or wet surfaces as 
reported in the cluster. Thus, efforts could be provided with respect to the road 
cleaning as well as the implementation of roads surfaces with a better grip on these 
intersections in order to improve the braking conditions; 
 
Lastly, most accidents are reported to occur on signalised intersections. Pedestrians 
are primarily found crossing the road legally, as suggested by the green state of 
traffic light for the driver when turning left. These traffic lights could be improved, by 
providing an earlier green light for pedestrians than for turning vehicles, or even 
providing an exclusive green light for pedestrians, as implied by (Gitelman et al. 
2012). 
 
Lastly, the spatial distribution of cluster C7 indicates that efforts regarding this specific 
crash type should be focused on the major cities of Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, 
Odense, Aalborg and Esbjerg) as well as few smaller cities such as Viborg, Horsens, 
Slagelse or Næstved.  
 
==PAGE:203==
203 
 
7.1.8 
Cluster C8 
 
No hit-and-run cluster C8 
Rural, road section, sober driver going straight ahead, car,

Table 40: No hit-and-run cluster C8 overview 
 
7.1.8.1 
Cluster composition and severity factors 
Cluster C8 involves intoxicated male pedestrians hit by sober car or truck drivers on 
weekend nights, while walking along or standing on rural main road sections. The cluster is 
by far the most severe one, with a fatality rate of 18.2 % compared to 5.1 % in the whole 
sample. In turn, it contains 36.9 % of all the fatalities recorded during the ten years, while 
accounting for only 10.4 % of the observations. Table 41 summarises the different 
elements characterising the cluster as  well as factors influencing its severity level. 
A majority of pedestrians involved in cluster C8 are males. The pedestrians’ intoxication 
represents an aggravating factor associated with a 9.6 % increased risk of severe accidents 
and 6.8 % for fatal ones compared to sober pedestrians. Lastly, the pedestrians’ action of 
==PAGE:204==
204 
 
walking along or standing on the road is found to increase the risk of severe accidents 
compared to crossing (elasticity of 24.8 % for standing on the road). 
The pedestrians are hit by cars or trucks, the latter dramatically increasing the severity level 
of the accidents due to their important weight and less pedestrian-friendly design (179.9 % 
increased risk of fatality compared to cars). The presence of male drivers increases the 
likelihood of severe accidents as stated in all previous clusters. The soberness of the drivers 
mitigates the severity level compared to intoxication. The drivers’ action of going straight 
ahead is found to be the most dangerous action, as stated in previous clusters. Lastly, the 
drivers are found to have no duty to give way to the pedestrian. 
Most accidents occur on express, national or regional roads, which increase the severity 
level of accidents compared to minor local ones (elasticity of 21.1 % national roads for the 
fatal outcome). Most of these roads are two-way undivided with one lane per direction, and 
situated in rural areas without roadside buildings, yielding the highest severity levels 
compared to other roadside environments.  Cluster C8 comprises accidents occurring on 
road sections with an over-representation of curves, which greatly increase the severity 
level (elasticities of 14.6 % and 73.8 % for the severe and fatal outcomes compared to 
straight road sections), which can be explained by the lower visibility of pedestrians. The 
speed limit ≥ 80 km/h dramatically aggravate the severity of the accidents, with elasticities 
of 26.3 % for the severe outcome and 182.1 % for the fatal crashes compared to a speed 
limit up to 50 km/h, which can be explained by the higher energy released during the crash. 
Cluster C8 contains accidents that mostly occur on weekends with dark roads, which 
significantly increases the risk of a severe and fatal accident (elasticity of 108.7 % 
compared to daylight for fatal accidents), which is explained by the lower visibility of the 
pedestrian. The cluster also indicates a bad visibility, despite a good weather and dry roads. 
Lastly, an over-representation of the time period 21:00 – 06:00 is shown in the cluster. 
Most of these accidents are located more than 10 km of a hospital, thus greatly increasing 
the risk of a fatal accident (elasticity of 2.3 %). Moreover, these accidents are mostly 
located in rural and rural municipalities according to the rurality index, thus increasing the 
risk of severe accidents compared to urban ones(elasticities of respectively 4.7 % and 8.6 
%). Lastly, over-representations of North Jutland and Zealand are reported, increasing the 
severity level of the accidents (elasticity of 40.5 % for the fatal outcome in North Jutland 
compared to Copenhagen). 
The population density in the proximity of the crashes is extremely low (< 100 
inhabitants/km²) and characterised by a medium unemployment rate. 
Lastly, exposure factors indicate a very low road (< 5000 veh.km/km²/day) and walk traffic 
volumes (<100 walk.km/km²/day), increasing the severity of accidents due to the absence

==PAGE:206==
206 
 
of safety-in-number effect (elasticities of -4.1 % and -6.2 % for the severe and fatal 
outcomes). Moreover, it can be noted the absence of bus stops in proximity of the 
accidents. 
 
7.1.8.2 
Spatial distribution 
The spatial distribution of cluster C8 reveals that these accidents are evenly spread over the 
rural areas of Denmark. Moreover, it can be noted that the accidents are located along the 
major roads of Denmark. 
 
7.1.8.3 
Proposals 
In short, cluster C8 involves intoxicated male pedestrians hit by sober car or truck drivers on 
weekend nights, while walking along or standing on rural main road sections. Several 
countermeasures can be proposed to reduce the number of these accidents: 
 
Education targeting pedestrians could be provided, in order to warn them about the 
danger of walking on main roads at night, especially when intoxicated. For instance, 
pedestrians could be instructed to walk on the left hand side of the road in order to 
see the oncoming traffic, as suggested by the Danish Council for Safe Traffic (Rådet 
for Sikker Trafik 2016c); 
 
The false impression of safety due to the low traffic could be tackled by the 
implementation of flashing signs to warn drivers about the potential presence of 
pedestrians; 
 
The presence of drunk pedestrians on weekend nights, together with the absence of 
bus stops in proximity, suggest that these pedestrians are going out and have no 
means of transport to come back home, hence forcing them to walk. In turn, 
establishing bus services during weekend nights and connecting the different leisure 
places (restaurants, bars, night clubs) to the rural towns may greatly reduce the 
number of pedestrians on the roads; 
 
More police patrols could be enforced during weekend nights on the major roads of 
Denmark, in order to detect and help potential endangered pedestrians; 
 
The visibility of pedestrians on these roads is very low due to the absence of 
lighting. In turn, the wearing of a reflective jacket for pedestrians at night should be 
compulsory, as suggested by (Stutts 2004). This new regulation could be 
accompanied with safety campaigns including the free distribution of these jackets, 
as it has already been the case in Copenhagen and Frederiksberg in the recent years 
(Fischer et al. 2010); 
 
The over-representation of curves implying a low visibility could be lighted, in order 
to make pedestrians more visible; 
==PAGE:207==
207 
 
 
Accidents belonging to cluster C8 occur on main roads (express road, national and 
regional roads) that are certainly not designed to accommodate pedestrians (absence 
of sidewalks), thus forcing them to directly walk on the road. Improvements could be 
provided by establishing or widening shoulders which could move pedestrians away 
from the road traffic; 
 
The speed limit could be reduced in specific locations where pedestrians are often 
reported to walk. This could also be done by physically reducing the width of the 
road lanes, which would both reduce the speed of the vehicles as well as providing 
more space for pedestrians; 
 
The over-representation of heavy vehicles in cluster C8 could also be tackled. 
Regulations could be for instance enforced, in order to forbid or restrict truck traffic 
during weekend nights on roads where pedestrian traffic is reported, as suggested by 
(Mohamed et al. 2013); 
 
Lastly, measures to compensate the important distance to the nearest hospital 
reported in the cluster should be investigated. Faster access to emergency services 
could be enforced by providing a better geographical repartition of emergency 
departments in rural and remote areas of Denmark. Moreover, and as proposed for 
cluster C3, vehicles could be equipped with automatic emergency call devices, which 
will be compulsory in Europe in new car models from 2018 (The European Parliament 
2016). Indeed, these devices could help to save time by automatically sending the 
geo-location of the crash to emergency services. This is particularly relevant in the 
case of night rural crashes as in the present cluster, as drivers are likely to not be 
able to precisely state their exact location when asked by the emergency services, 
thus delaying their intervention. 
 
The spatial distribution of cluster C8 does not allow defining precise areas where to focus 
attention on this specific crash type. Instead, measures should be taken nationwide. 
 
Lastly, due to its higher than average severity level, cluster C8 should be prioritized with 
respect to other crash patterns. 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:208==
208 
 
7.2 Hit-and-run clusters 
The following paragraphs aim at analysing the hit-and-run clusters. However, in the contrary 
of the no hit-and-run sample, no severity models have been proposed due to the small 
sample size of the considered accidents. Thus, only the cluster composition is analysed in 
this section. 
 
7.2.1 
Cluster C1 
 
Hit-and-run cluster C1 
Urban, road section,  driver going straight ahead, bike, 
20 – 29 yo intoxicated pedestrian crossing,  weekend, illuminated road, dry road 
25.3 % of the whole sample 
No injury

Severe injury

52.1 % 
18.6 % 
26.7 % 
2.5 % 
Table 42: Hit-and-run cluster C1 overview 
 
7.2.1.1 
Cluster composition 
Cluster C1 involves 20 – 29 years intoxicated pedestrians hit when crossing urban road 
section by cars or bikes, with an over-representation of weekend nights. The cluster is in 
line with the average severity level observed in the whole sample, with 2.5 % of fatalities, 
==PAGE:209==
209 
 
26.7 % of severe injuries, 18.6 % of light injuries and 52.1 % of no injuries. Table 43 
summarises the different elements characterising the cluster as  well as factors influencing 
its severity level. 
A significant share of pedestrians involved in cluster C1 is aged 20 – 29 years. An over-
representation of intoxication is also reported in the cluster. Lastly, the main pedestrian’s 
action is found to be crossing. 
The pedestrians are hit by cars or bikes, which are primarily found to drive straight ahead. 
Most accidents occur on major local roads, with an over-representation of one-way roads 
with one lane per direction, and situated in urban residential, tall buildings or shopping 
streets areas. Moreover, the majority of accidents belonging to cluster C1 occur on road 
sections with a speed limit of 50 km/h. 
The cluster reveals an over-representation of accidents occurring on weekends with 
illuminated roads, with an over-representation of the time period 21:00 – 06:00. Lastly, 
good visibility, good weather and dry roads are reported in the cluster. 
Most of these accidents are located within 5 km of a hospital, in urban municipalities 
according to the rurality index, with a majority of crashes taking place in the Copenhagen 
area.  
The population density in the proximity of the crashes is very high (> 5000 
inhabitants/km²) and characterised by a high unemployment rate. 
Lastly, exposure factors indicate a very high road (> 25000 veh.km/km²/day) and walk 
traffic volumes (> 10000 walk.km/km²/day). The accidents are found in close proximity to 
restaurants, bars and night clubs (> 10) as well as bus stops (> 5).  
 
7.2.1.2 
Spatial distribution 
The spatial distribution of cluster C1 reveals that these accidents are only located in five 
cities centres in Denmark (Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense and Kolding). 
 
7.2.1.3 
Proposals 
As a summary, cluster C1 involves 20 – 29 years intoxicated pedestrians hit when crossing 
urban road section by cars or bikes, with an over-representation of weekend nights. Several 
countermeasures can be proposed to tackle this crash type:

 
Education targeting car drivers and cyclists could be proposed, in order to make them 
watch more carefully pedestrian traffic in city centres, as suggested by (Fischer et al. 
2010); 
 
Flashing signs could also be implemented in order to remind the presence of 
pedestrians to other road users; 
 
The issue of drivers leaving the accident scene (hit-and-runs) could be alleviated by 
installing CCTV in the centres of Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense and Kolding 
as proposed by (Fischer et al. 2010). Alternatively, more police patrols on weekend 
nights in these cities could reduce the risk of drivers leaving the crash scene; 
 
As a significant share of accidents occurs on illuminated roads, better lighting could 
be provided to reduce the risk of accidents, as recommended by  (Zegeer et al. 
2005); 
 
A large part of these accidents occur in one-way roads with one lane per direction. It 
can thus be deduced that these streets are rather small, and not really suitable for a 
speed limit of 50 km/h, especially with the stated high walk volume and land use 
type stated (shopping streets). In turn, the speed of these narrow streets could be 
lowered with the implementation of meeting zones at 20 km/h, in which the 
pedestrians have a complete priority over other road users as implied by (National 
Technical University of Athens 2001); 
 
As a significant share of the accidents occur on weekend nights, in narrow streets of 
city centres and close to restaurants, bars and night clubs, it could be envisaged to 
forbid the two and four-wheel traffic in inner city centres on weekend nights, thus 
providing an important and safe area for pedestrians when going out in weekends, as 
indicated by (National Technical University of Athens 2001). 
 
Lastly, the spatial distribution of cluster C1 clearly indicates that these measures should 
focus on the inner city centres of Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense and Kolding.

Table 44: Hit-and-run cluster C2 overview 
 
7.2.2.1 
Cluster composition 
Cluster C2 involves 10 – 19 years sober pedestrians hit while walking along or standing on 
the road, by cars going straight ahead or jumping off the road on urban road sections. The 
cluster is in line with the average severity level observed in the whole sample, with 3.0 % 
of fatalities, 19.2 % of severe injuries, 26.1 % of light injuries and 51.7 % of no injuries. 
Table 45 summarises the different elements characterising the cluster as  well as factors 
influencing its severity level. 
Cluster C2 exhibits an over-representation of pedestrians aged 10 – 19 years, which are 
reported to be sober. Moreover, an over-representation of pedestrians walking along or 
standing on the road can be unveiled in the cluster. 
==PAGE:213==
213 
 
The pedestrians are hit by cars, found to drive straight ahead or jumping off the road. 
Most accidents occur on medium and minor local roads, on two-way undivided roads with 
one lane per direction, and situated in urban residential, low buildings areas. Moreover, most 
accidents occur on road sections with a speed limit of 50 km/h, with an absence of low 
speed roads in the vicinity of the crashes. 
Most accidents occur on weekdays with daylight, good visibility, good weather and dry 
roads. 
The majority of the crashes are located more than 10 km of a hospital, in rural and remote 
municipalities, with an over-representation of South and North Jutland as well as Zealand 
regions. 
The population density in the surroundings of the accidents is medium (100 – 1000 
inhabitants/km²) and characterised by a low unemployment rate. 
Lastly, exposure factors indicate a very low road (< 5000 veh.km/km²/day) and walk traffic 
volumes (100 - 1000 walk.km/km²/day), which may create a false impression of safety. 
 
7.2.2.2 
Spatial distribution 
The spatial distribution of cluster C2 reveals that these accidents are present in small 
residential areas all over Denmark, and absent from the major cities of the country. 
 
 
7.2.2.3 
Proposals 
In brief, cluster C2 involves 10 – 19 years sober pedestrians hit while walking along or 
standing on the road, by cars going straight ahead or jumping off the road on urban road 
sections.  The following measures could be adopted: 
 
Education targeting children could be enforced, in order to teach them to walk on the 
sidewalk instead of being on the road. This could for instance be done through the 
Danish Council for Safe Traffic (Rådet for Sikker Trafik 2016b); 
 
The low road and walk traffic may create a false sensation of safety. Thus, signs 
could be implemented in the residential zones where accidents belonging to cluster 
C2 occur. For example, signs “Børn på vej” installed in some residential areas of 
Denmark by the Danish Council for Safe Traffic could be generalised (Rådet for 
Sikker Trafik 2016a); 
 
The speed limit of 50 km/h in residential zones, with an important presence of 
children, may be not appropriated. 30 km/h zones could be implemented in 
residential areas as suggested by (Hummel 1999) as well as other measures to 
reduce the speed of cars (for instance bumps, speed measurement “din fart” signs or

well as lowering the risk of hitting a child due to a too high speed; 
 
The important distance to the hospital could imply a longer response time of 
emergency services, even more increase due to the driver leaving the accident scene. 
Thus, automatic emergency call devices should be implemented in cars (these 
devices being compulsory in all new car models from 2018 in the EU (The European 
Parliament 2016)). It would indeed immediately notify the emergency services in 
case of an accident and reduce the response time, as well as it would prevent drivers 
from leaving the accident scene due to the identified call to the emergency services. 
 
Black boxes in cars could also prevent the drivers from leaving the accident scene by 
recording their actions, thus reducing the risk of hit-and-runs. 
Lastly, the spatial distribution of cluster C2 does not reveal specific areas in Denmark where 
to focus attention on: indeed, many residential areas spread all over the country are found 
to face this crash type.

Cluster composition 
Cluster C3 involves sober pedestrians hit while crossing by cars going straight on urban 
intersections. The cluster is in line with the average severity level observed in the whole 
sample, with 3.2 % of fatalities, 24.3 % of severe injuries, 20.3 % of light injuries and

as  well as factors influencing its severity level. 
Cluster C3 is composed of sober pedestrians that are primarily found crossing the road. 
The pedestrians are hit by cars, found to drive straight ahead.

Accidents in cluster C3 exhibit an over-representation of two-way divided roads with two or 
more lanes per direction, and situated in urban residential areas. Moreover, most accidents 
occur on intersections, with an over-representation of speed limit between 51 – 79 km/h, 
with a high share of low speed roads in the surroundings of the crashes. 
Most accidents occur on weekdays with daylight, good visibility, good weather and dry 
roads. 
The majority of the crashes are located within 5 km of a hospital, in urban municipalities, 
with a majority of the accidents occurring in the Copenhagen area. 
The population density in the surroundings of the accidents is high (1000 – 5000

Lastly, exposure factors indicate a high road (10000 – 50000 veh.km/km²/day) and walk 
traffic volumes (1000 - 10000 walk.km/km²/day). 
 
7.2.3.2 
Spatial distribution 
The spatial distribution of cluster C3 reveals that accidents are present in Copenhagen, 
Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense, as well as few smaller cities (Kolding, Roskilde, Slagelse, Lejre) 
and along the East coast of Zealand. 
 
 
7.2.3.3 
Proposals 
Shortly, cluster C3 involves sober pedestrians hit while crossing by cars going straight on 
urban intersections. Little information regarding the crashes is available in this cluster, 
limiting the number of countermeasures that can be proposed: 
 
Accidents in cluster C3 can be supposed to occur on major roads, as suggested by 
the over-representation of two-way divided roads with two or more lanes along with 
a speed limit between 51 – 79 km/h. Flashing signs in such large intersections could 
be implemented, in order to remind drivers of the presence of pedestrians as 
proposed by (Gitelman et al. 2012); 
 
Traffic lights for pedestrians should be implemented on such large intersections, if 
not already present.  
 
Islands at mid-way could be provided to make pedestrian cross two-way divided 
roads in a safer way; 
 
CCTV-monitored intersections could be implemented to reduce the risk of a driver 
leaving the accident scene (hit-and-run), as suggested by (Fischer et al. 2010). 
Lastly, the spatial distribution of cluster C3 reveals that efforts should primarily focus on 
Copenhagen, Aarhus, Aalborg, Odense, as well as the East coast of Zealand.

7.2.4.1 
Cluster composition 
Cluster C4 involves sober female pedestrians hit while crossing by cars turning on urban 
intersections. The cluster reveals a slightly higher than average level of severity. Indeed, if 
the fatality rate is only 0.6 % compared to 2.3 % in the whole sample, 29.7 % of the 
accidents lead to a severe injury compared to 25.8% in the whole sample. Table 49 
summarises the different elements characterising the cluster as  well as factors influencing 
its severity level. 
Cluster C4 primarily involves female pedestrians that are sober and primarily found crossing 
the road. 
The pedestrians are hit by cars, found to be turning. 
==PAGE:220==
220 
 
Accidents in cluster C4 mostly occur on two-way undivided roads with one lane per 
direction, and situated in urban residential, tall buildings or shopping streets areas. 
Moreover, most accidents occur on intersections, with a speed limit of 50 km/h. 
Most accidents occur on weekdays with daylight, good visibility and good weather; 
however, a higher than average share of accidents is linked to wet roads as well as autumn 
and winter period. 
The majority of the crashes are located within 5 km of a hospital, in intermediate and rural 
municipalities, with an over-representation of Mid and South Jutland, as well as Zealand 
regions. 
The population density in the surroundings of the accidents is rather high (100 – 5000 
inhabitants/km²) and characterised by a high unemployment rate. 
Lastly, exposure factors indicate a medium road (5000 – 25000 veh.km/km²/day) and walk 
traffic volumes (100 - 10000 walk.km/km²/day). 
Thus, it can be noted that cluster C4 shares several characteristics with the no hit-and-run 
cluster C7. 
 
7.2.4.2 
Spatial distribution 
The spatial distribution of cluster C4 reveals that this crash type is only present in 
residential areas of medium-sized cities in the country (Esbjerg, Roskilde, Fredericia, etc.) 
 
 
7.2.4.3 
Proposals 
In few words, cluster C4 involves sober female pedestrians hit while crossing by cars 
turning on urban intersections. Many similarities can be observed this cluster and the no hit-
and-run cluster C7, which calls for comparable countermeasures: 
 
Education for drivers could be provided, in order to warn them to watch pedestrians 
when turning in intersections, potentially via advertising campaigns as suggested by 
(Fischer et al. 2010); 
 
Flashing lamps could also be implemented to remind the presence of pedestrian 
crosswalk to drivers when turning, as proposed by (Gitelman et al. 2012); 
 
Accidents in cluster C7 occur on illuminated roads. Thus, the visibility of pedestrians 
could be improved with a better lighting system over the pedestrian crossing, as 
recommended by  (Zegeer et al. 2005); 
 
The autumn/winter period along with the bad weather conditions significantly 
degrade the braking conditions, due to the presence of leaves, snow or wet surfaces. 
Thus, better road cleaning could be provided as well as the development of roads 
==PAGE:221==
221 
 
surfaces with a better grip on the concerned in order to improve the braking 
conditions; 
 
CCTV-monitored intersections could be enforced, in order to reduce the risk of a 
driver leaving the accident scene. 
Lastly, the spatial distribution of cluster C4 reveals that efforts regarding this crash type 
should focus on medium cities of Denmark.

7.2.5.1 
Cluster composition 
The last cluster, C5, involves sober male pedestrians aged 50 - 59 hit while walking along 
or standing on the road, hit by cars or mopeds going straight ahead on rural unlit road 
sections, on weekend nights. The cluster reveals a higher than average level of severity. 
Indeed, if the cluster surprisingly does not yield any fatality, 38.2 % of the accidents lead to 
a severe injury compared to 25.8% in the whole sample. Table 51 summarises the different 
elements characterising the cluster as  well as factors influencing its severity level. 
Cluster C5 mainly involves male pedestrians that are sober, with an over-representation of 
the age category 50 – 59 years.  These pedestrians are primarily found walking along or 
standing on the road. 
==PAGE:223==
223 
 
The pedestrians are hit by cars or mopeds, found to be driving straight ahead. 
Accidents in cluster C5 mostly take place on express, national or regional roads that are 
two-way undivided with one lane per direction, and situated in rural areas with no roadside 
buildings. Moreover, most accidents occur on road sections with an over-representation of 
curves, with a speed limit ≥ 80 km/h. 
The cluster reveals an over-representation of weekends with unlit roads, poor visibility, good 
weather and dry roads. Moreover, spring seasons as well as the time period 18:00 – 06:00 
are over-represented in the accidents. 
The majority of the crashes are located more than 10 km of a hospital, in rural and remote 
municipalities, with an over-representation of North and South Jutland, as well as Zealand 
regions. 
The population density in the surroundings of the accidents is very low (< 100 
inhabitants/km²) and characterised by a medium unemployment rate. 
Lastly, exposure factors indicate an extreme low road (< 5000 veh.km/km²/day) and walk 
traffic volumes (< 100 walk.km/km²/day), which may create a false sensation of safety due 
to the absence of traffic. No bus stops are present in the vicinity of the accidents. 
Thus, it can be noted that cluster C5 shares several characteristics with the no hit-and-run 
cluster C8. 
 
7.2.5.2 
Spatial distribution 
The spatial distribution of cluster C5 reveals that these accidents are present in the rural 
areas of Denmark. 
 
7.2.5.3 
 Proposals 
Concisely, cluster C5 involves sober male pedestrians aged 50 - 59 hit while walking along 
or standing on the road, hit by cars or mopeds going straight ahead on rural unlit road 
sections, on weekend nights.  Many features are shared with the no hit-and-run cluster C8, 
thus calling for comparable countermeasures: 
 
Education targeting pedestrians should be provided, in order to instruct them to walk 
on the left hand side of the road with the purpose of seeing oncoming traffic, as 
proposed by the Danish Council for Safe Traffic (Rådet for Sikker Trafik 2016c); 
 
The false impression of safety due to the low traffic could be tackled by the 
implementation of flashing signs warning drivers about the potential presence of 
pedestrians;

==PAGE:225==
