 
The presence of pedestrians on weekend nights together with the absence of bus 
stops in proximity suggest that these pedestrians have no means of transport to 
come back home on weekend nights, hence compelling them to walk. In turn, 
establishing bus services during weekend nights may greatly reduce the number of 
pedestrians on the roads; 
 
More police patrols could be enforced during weekend nights on the major roads of 
Denmark to spot and assist potential endangered pedestrians; 
 
The visibility of pedestrians on unlit rural roads is very poor. In turn, the wearing of a 
reflective jacket for pedestrians at night should be compulsory, as suggested by 
(National Technical University of Athens 2001); 
 
Curves with low visibility could be lighted, in order to provide a better visibility of 
pedestrians; 
 
Express road, national and regional roads are most likely not designed to 
accommodate pedestrians (no sidewalks), thus forcing them to directly walk on the 
road. Establishing or widening shoulders could provide pedestrians a safer walkway 
on these roads; 
 
Measures to compensate the important distance to the nearest hospital reported in 
the cluster should be explored. Vehicles could be equipped with automatic 
emergency call devices, as already proposed in the no hit-and-run cluster C8. It 
would cut the intervention time of emergency services by immediately informing 
them of an occurrence of accident and its geo-location, but also preventing the risk 
of a driver leaving the crash scene due to the identified call. 
 
Lastly, black boxes in cars could reduce the risk of hit-and-runs, by forcing the driver 
to face its responsibilities in case of accident. 
 
The spatial distribution of cluster C5 does not reveal any specific region of Denmark where 
to focus attention, as the considered crash pattern is evenly spread over the rural areas of 
the country. 
 
Lastly, due to its higher than average severity level, cluster C5 should be prioritized with 
respect to other crash patterns. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:226==
226 
 
7.3 Summary 
 
The study of the different clusters revealed clear and distinct crash patterns as well as 
spatial distributions. Hit-and-run crash types have been found to be different in their nature 
compared to no hit-and-run accidents, with only two clusters overlapping with no hit-and-
run ones (C4 and C5, respectively related to female accident on urban intersections with 
bad weather, and rural accidents on weekend nights). 
In turn, specific measures have been proposed for each of the clusters, which can be split in 
several categories: 
 
Education of road users (targeting pedestrians or drivers); 
 
Enforcement of road safety rules (police patrol, CCTV, black boxes); 
 
Information of road users (flashing signs, signposts); 
 
Road design (islands, traffic lights, road surfaces with a better grip); 
 
Visibility (illuminated crossing, road surface reflectivity, yellow safety jackets); 
 
Vehicle design (reversing sensor, e-call, autonomous emergency braking); 
 
Traffic regulation (truck traffic restriction); 
 
Transport demand (rural night bus services). 
Lastly, the severity levels of the different clusters have pointed out specific crash patterns 
that should be prioritised: 
 
No hit-and-run cluster C3, related to reversing cars in remote residential areas (6.5 % 
of fatalities); 
 
No hit-and-run cluster C8, related to rural accidents on weekend nights (18.2 % of 
fatalities); 
 
Hit-and-run cluster C5, also related to rural accidents on weekend nights (38.2 % of 
severe injuries). 
 
 
==PAGE:227==
227 
 
8. Conclusion 
 
This last section aims at summarising the work accomplished in the thesis, as well as 
pointing out limitations and future orientations. 
 
8.1 Achievements 
 
The thesis provided a thorough analysis of pedestrian accidents in Denmark, and covered 
the different items that had been presented in the introduction: 
 
It proposed an important literature review regarding pedestrian safety, which 
unveiled the different modelling approaches employed in road safety studies as well 
as the latest trends and findings in the area of research; 
 
It developed pedestrian safety analyses including land use, socio-economic and 
spatial considerations; 
 
It unveiled distinct and meaningful pedestrian crash patterns by the means of latent 
class clustering; 
 
It provided insights regarding pedestrian hit-and-run accidents in Denmark, an issue 
that had not been studied before; 
 
It highlighted the aggravating and mitigating factors related to the severity level of 
pedestrian accidents, through the use of advanced severity models tackling the 
issues of heterogeneity and spatial correlation among observations; 
 
It provided an update regarding pedestrian safety in Denmark, which can be used by 
policy makers; 
 
It derived meaningful policy implications by bridging the results from the accident 
classification and the severity models.  
 
Results obtained from the LCA yielded 8 clusters for the non-hit-and-run dataset, and 5 for 
the hit-and-run one. This analysis revealed clear and distinct crash patterns for the two 
datasets, with only two clusters overlapping between the two samples (rural accidents on 
national road, and accidents in intersections with drivers turning), suggesting that the two 
types of accidents are significantly different in their composition and call for distinct 
countermeasures. Moreover, the LCA revealed clear spatial patterns within the clusters, and 
allowed identifying the most severe crash types. In turn, two crash types were found to be 
prioritized due to their severity level: 
 
Reversing cars in remote residential areas of Denmark; 
 
Rural accidents on weekend nights. 
 
==PAGE:228==
228 
 
The severity models shed new lights on factors influencing the severity level of pedestrian 
accidents. The most important results revealed elderly pedestrians as the most exposed 
population to severe accidents. Intoxication, both for pedestrians and drivers was 
associated with more severe accidents. Crossing from a hidden place for pedestrians and 
driving straight ahead for drivers were linked with the most severe accidents, while heavier 
vehicles were found to increase the risk of severe and fatal accidents compared to lighter 
vehicles. Major local roads were highlighted as being the most dangerous road category, 
with an increased risk of severe accidents associated with a high speed limit and the 
presence of curves. Night time and unlit roads were pointed out as the most dangerous 
lighting conditions. Spatial considerations revealed rural and remote municipalities as the 
most exposed areas to severe crashes compared to the rest of Denmark, with an important 
over-exposure of North Jutland. Regions located far from a hospital were also found to be 
more exposed to fatal accidents compared to the rest of the country. Lastly, socio-
economic and exposure factors highlighted deprived areas of Denmark as dealing with a 
more important risk of severe accidents, while places with a high walk volume were found 
to be safer compared to the rest of Denmark. 
 
The combination of the LCA and severity models provided a complete view of each 
uncovered crash type, through the analysis of its intrinsic characteristics, the factors 
influencing the severity level within the cluster and its spatial distribution. In turn, precise 
recommendations in terms of actions to be taken as well as geographical areas to focus on 
were provided, ranging from education of road users to road design suggestions or traffic 
regulation measures. 
 
The different results achieved during the thesis have led to the submission of two research 
papers for the Transport Research Board 96th conference, held on January 8 – 12, 2017 in 
Washington, D.C, USA, with the collaboration of Carlo Giacomo Prato, Sigal Kaplan and 
Thomas Kjær Rasmussen: 
 
“Integrating police reports with geographic information system resources for 
uncovering multidimensional patterns of pedestrian crashes in Denmark” ; 
 
“Considering built environment and spatial correlation in modelling injury severity for 
pedestrian crashes”. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:229==
229 
 
8.2 Limitations 
 
Beyond the different conclusions that have been drawn from the thesis, it is important to 
underline the limitations of the presented work, arising from the data quality or the 
methodology employed. 
 
8.2.1 
Data 
 
8.2.1.1 
Under-reporting 
A first limitation arising from the present work has been the well-known risk of 
underreporting of no injury crashes in police records ((Elvik & Mysen 1999), (Sciortino et al. 
2005)), which can bias the conclusions drawn from road safety studies. For instance, about 
20% of minor or no injury collisions involving pedestrians are missing from police reports in 
the US (Moudon et al. 2011), thus greatly influencing the reliability of pedestrian safety 
studies. This situation can be easily explained, as police intervention was less likely to be 
required for damage-only accidents compared to accidents involving injuries, for which the 
police request was almost systematic, hence leading to an under-report of damage-only 
accidents. 
This issue has been pointed out in Denmark, as the Danish Road Safety Commission 
explicitly acknowledged that pedestrian crashes were particularly under-reported in the 
police records compared to hospital records (Danish Road Safety Commission 2013) 
(International Traffic Safety Data and Analysis Group 2015).  
 
8.2.1.2 
Police records quality 
Some limitations can be highlighted regarding the data collection scheme proposed by the 
Danish Road Directorate, arising from its complexity and precision. On one side, more than 
90 parameters were collected by the police, thus providing a multi-faceted and really 
detailed description of road accidents. On the other side, this complexity may have 
increased the risk of the police skipping some variables or reporting erroneous values in the 
records, thus degrading the data quality. 
This issue has been encountered in the thesis, leading to the deletion of many records and a 
reduced available set of explanatory variables. 
Police records may have also been particularly unreliable with respect to hit-and-run cases. 
Due to their inherent nature, information regarding the driver’s characteristics was indeed 
solely based on statements from witnesses that could be uncertain. 
 
==PAGE:230==
230 
 
8.2.1.3 
Accident geo-location 
An important aspect of the study has consisted in geo-locating the accidents as well as 
matching them with the road network. Even if a great attention has been given to this task, 
the procedure employed in the thesis has led to a loss of 10 % of the records thus revealing 
its limitations. 
 
8.2.1.4 
Time coverage 
The present study has focused on pedestrian accidents in Denmark for the years 2006 – 
2015. Studying such a long time period of accidents may represent a challenge when it 
comes to the representativeness of explanatory factors as road network or socio-economic 
characteristics, which are likely to have greatly evolved over the decade. 
The road network in Denmark may have significantly changed between 2006 and 2015. 
Road extension, deviation of city centre, construction of pedestrian facilities or 
establishment of traffic calming areas may have brought improvements regarding road 
safety which have not been captured in the present study. Indeed, the NAVTEQ road 
network employed in this thesis dates back from 2008, and therefore does not accurately 
represent the state of the road network at the exact time of the crashes. 
The socio-economic characteristics of the population may also have greatly evolved in ten 
years, with respect to population composition and geographical location. For instance, 
recent developing areas of Copenhagen such as Vestamager, Holmen or Nordhavn may be 
badly covered, as the socio-economic characteristics employed in the thesis date back from 
2010. In a similar manner, land use characteristics may be badly covered with 2012 data. 
The road and public transport volumes have been simulated for the year 2010. On a finer 
temporal scale, these volumes have been estimated as daily average values and do not 
allow for a distinction between on/off peak periods as well as weekday/weekend times. In 
turn, these measures only provide a vague estimation of the traffic volumes at the date and 
time of occurrence of the accidents. 
Lastly, the road users ‘behaviour may have greatly evolved during the studied period and 
lead to biased conclusions. Indeed, road safety studies tend to show that roads in Europe 
are becoming safer from year to year (European Commission 2009). Therefore, areas of 
improvement in terms of road safety may have significantly changed during this time period. 
 
8.2.1.5 
Spatial coverage 
The use of 5 different levels of data aggregation has certainly led to a loss of information 
and inconsistency among variables.  
==PAGE:231==
231 
 
For instance, the chosen radius for the buffers surrounding the accidents may have greatly 
influenced the significance and value of the different spatial variables (road density, number 
of bus stops, land use, etc.). In a similar way, the uneven sizes of the NTM zones, ranging 
from 0.160 km² to 308 km² may also have introduced bias in spatial data by defining non-
comparable zones. 
 
Road, public transport and walk traffic volumes have been collected at zonal level. 
However, more disaggregate data at road link of public transport stop level would have 
provided more detailed and accurate information. Similarly, the employed land use cover 
only had a resolution of 100 x 100 m, which could be problematic in dense urban areas for 
which the land use scale should be much finer. 
 
8.2.1.6 
Omitted variables 
Despite the careful study of the body of knowledge in road safety, relevant explanatory 
variables may have been left out of the thesis due to limited information from the data 
sources or omission when reviewing the literature. This can have resulted in inconsistent 
parameter estimates if these omitted variables are correlated with other variables included in 
the model, or if they have an influence on the severity level of the accident (Savolainen et 
al. 2011). 
In the present thesis, a major source of omitted variable has been the limited information 
regarding the road network. Indeed, no information was available regarding the presence of 
sidewalks along the roads or facilities for pedestrian to cross the road (crossing, island, 
traffic lights, etc.).  
 
8.2.1.7 
Considered observations 
Due to the limitation of the different sources of data, as well as the issue of under-
reporting, the considered datasets represented a subset of the pedestrian accidents in 
Denmark from 2006 to 2015, only including exploitable and complete records. Moreover, it 
has been chosen to only consider accidents involving one pedestrian and one vehicle. In 
turn, the results may have provided a biased view of pedestrian accidents in Denmark. 
 
8.2.1.8 
Sample size 
Two datasets have been considered in the present thesis, one for non-hit-and-run cases, 
and the other for hit-and-run accidents. However, the second dataset was rather small as it 
only contained 930 observations. Such a small sample has led to difficulties, as it increased 
the uncertainty of the LCA analysis; moreover, it prevented the elaboration of severity 
models, as estimated parameters have been found to be far too uncertain to derive any 
conclusion. 
==PAGE:232==
232 
 
8.2.2 
Methodology  
8.2.2.1 
LCA 
The latent class analysis has constituted an important part of the thesis, and allowed to 
unveil interesting patterns of accidents. However, this classification technique also had 
some limitations. 
The choice of variables to include in the LCA analysis has been quite difficult to make. 
Indeed, the classification results were rather difficult to forecast and greatly depended on 
the variables included in the model. For instance, a correlation between two variables may 
have greatly biased the results of the LCA, which would have tended to group the accident 
based on these correlated factors. If the choice of variables related to the crash, pedestrian 
and driver characteristics has been quite straightforward, the selection of the land use and 
socio-economic variables has been more difficult in this regard. 
One particular issue has been related to variables aggregated at the NTM or municipality 
level. Indeed, spatially aggregated variables implied that accidents belonging to the same 
geographical zone were sharing a large amount of characteristics (all socio-economic 
factors, as well as the traffic volumes). In turn, the LCA tended to group accidents 
according to these spatially aggregated factors instead of the crash characteristics. This has 
represented a serious limitation of the use of spatially aggregated variables in LCA: in the 
final specification, a careful attention has been paid to this problem and many spatially 
aggregated variables have been left out to minimise it. The results of the LCA excluding the 
spatial variables are provided in appendix (page 251) to study this issue. The comparison 
between the two approaches revealed that the same number of clusters is yielded in the 
two specifications. If the LCA excluding spatial variables unveiled clusters that were much 
better separated and are easier to understand due to the parsimony of the specification, it 
however suffered from a lack of important variables, especially related to the road 
characteristics. Hit-and-runs would have been especially been difficult to interpret, as very 
little data would have been available.  It can also be noted that the clusters’ composition 
would have been significantly different between the two specifications, thus proving the 
influence of land use and socio-economic characteristics on the crash patterns. 
Lastly, the LCA has implied the use of categorical variables. The adaptation of continuous 
variables into categorical ones has certainly been a source of problem, as it inevitably has 
led to a loss of information. Moreover, the number and the definition of the categories 
within each variable were subjective and may have greatly influenced the outcomes of the 
LCA. 
==PAGE:233==
233 
 
8.2.2.2 
Severity models 
Different severity models have been used in the present thesis, all of them having pros and 
cons. The structure of the MNL, MXL and spatial logit models were quite similar, as the 
MXL and spatial logit models were based on the principles of the MNL model. Therefore, 
similar issues have been encountered for the three models. (Savolainen et al. 2011) listed 
some issues specifically related to severity models, which have evidently applied to the 
models used in the present thesis: 
 
Ordinal nature of the crash severity: the MNL, MXL and spatial logit models were all 
unordered models by nature, as the dependent variable was only considered as a 
categorical variable. However, injury-severity levels are ordered, as it can easily be 
understood that there is a gradation when shifting from the no injury state to light 
injured, severe injured and fatal outcome. A great discussion among road safety 
researchers has dealt with the choice of non-ordered or ordered models for accident 
severity studies, without showing a clear superiority of one of the alternatives 
(Mannering & Bhat 2014). 
 
Fixed parameters: the MNL and spatial logit models have used fixed parameters, 
restricting the effects of explanatory variables to be the same across accident 
observations. However, unobserved heterogeneity could have existed among victims 
of accidents (differences in risk-taking, psychological factors, etc.), suggesting that 
these parameters may have varied across accident observations. Not accounting for 
these effects may have resulted in potential bias of parameter estimates (Savolainen 
et al. 2011). In the present study, the elaboration of the MXL model aimed at 
accounting for this issue, by allowing some parameters to be distributed: in turn, 
eight parameters were found to be distributed. 
 
Endogeneity: this issue has arisen when explanatory variables were potentially 
correlated with the injury-severity outcome. This was for instance the case for the 
distance to the hospital variable: indeed, this variable only had an influence for the 
higher severity levels of accidents (light injury, severe injury and fatality), as these 
severity levels would have most likely required a hospitalisation for the victim. 
Therefore, the distance to the hospital influenced severe accidents; in the contrary, 
an accident leading to the no injury outcome would have most likely not required a 
hospitalisation and be affected by the distance to the hospital. Endogeneity may also 
has 
arisen 
from 
correlated 
variables 
or 
unobserved 
effects 
(unobserved 
heterogeneity). For instance, the conclusion that elderly drivers tended to be involved 
in less severe accidents may have hidden several considerations: this lower severity 
level could be due to the fact that elderly people would tend to driver at a lower 
speed as a result of a different risk perception or lower cognitive abilities compared 
to young drivers. 
==PAGE:234==
234 
 
 
Spatial and temporal correlations: crashes happening in close proximity in time or 
space were likely to share unobserved effects. Not accounting for these correlations 
may have resulted in biased parameters estimation. In the present thesis, temporal 
effects were only accounted for in the three severity models by the time explanatory 
variables (time slot, weekday/weekend and season), without a specific model 
structure developed to further tackle this issue. However, the spatial correlation was 
specifically targeted by the spatial logit model, which proved the relevance of taking 
into account this effect. 
As stated previously, the limitations from the three severity models have been globally 
similar due to their closeness in terms of methodology. However, a last limitation could be 
raised with respect to the spatial logit model, which implied a shift from a four outcome 
model towards a binary outcome. Thus, the MNL and MXL models provided more details 
regarding the effects of the different variables on the severity levels of the accidents, while 
the spatial logit model was much more limited in this regard.  
 
8.2.3 
Results 
 
The different objectives of the thesis have been globally met. However, different limitations 
can be raised regarding the different results obtained. The LCA provided somewhat blurred 
results regarding the clusters’ definitions, with several variables only lightly varying among 
the clusters. In turn, the results were sometimes quite difficult to interpret, especially with 
respect to the hit-and-run sample. This would suggest that the choice of variables included 
in the LCA could have been improved in order to get clearer results. 
The severity models required numerous variables’ adaptations in order to make parameters 
significant, such as the use of unique beta parameters across severity levels or the 
combination of several response categories within categorical variables, which probably led 
to a loss of information or biased results. Moreover, the small sample size of the hit-and-run 
sample prevented the elaboration of severity models. 
Due to the limitations of the LCA results and the impossibility to derive severity models, hit-
and-runs were only partially covered by the study: only crash patterns could be identified, 
while no insights regarding the factors contributing to the severity level of these accidents 
could be provided. 
Lastly, if the introduction of land use variables proved its relevance in the LCA analyses as 
well as the severity models (walk density, unemployment rate or land use diversity), the 
study also spotted variables that were not significant such as the road density or the 
presence of school in the vicinity of the accidents, thus disproving some intuitions regarding 
factors influencing pedestrian crashes. 
==PAGE:235==
235 
 
8.3 Further directions 
 
The work provided in the present thesis has contributed to the body of knowledge in 
pedestrian safety, and calls for further actions. 
Regarding data, the thesis proved the relevance of including land use, socio-economic and 
spatial considerations in order to provide a better understanding of pedestrian accidents. 
Thus, such considerations should be included in further pedestrian safety studies, and may 
be extended. The thesis also unveiled limitations with respect to data collection, which calls 
for specific actions. Efforts should be made in order to ensure a better quality of police 
records and tackle the issue of under-reporting. Several improvements regarding land use 
and socio-economic characteristics should also be investigated. For instance, the road 
network matching could be improved by employing advanced algorithms developed by (Loo 
2006) and (Deka & Quddus 2014), while more precise estimates regarding traffic volumes 
could be obtained by using local and detailed transport models such as  the Copenhagen 
one. Panel data, as well as more disaggregate information should be investigated to improve 
the quality of socio-economic and land use characteristics. Lastly, further studies regarding 
pedestrian accidents should also include variables regarding the presence of pedestrian 
facilities (sidewalks, crossings, etc.). In Denmark, this could be done thanks to the 
GeoDanmark program, developed by the Danish “Styrelsen for Dataforsyning of 
Effektivisering” (Styrelsen for Dataforsyning of Effektivisering 2016) aiming at providing a 
detailed GIS network of all roads in the country. 
From a methodological point of view, the thesis proved the relevance of employing the LCA 
analysis to unveil crash patterns. Moreover, advanced severity models tackling the issue of 
heterogeneity in the data and accounting for spatial correlation have proven their 
effectiveness. A further step would be to estimate a severity model accounting for both 
issues at a time, as proposed by (Castro et al. 2013). The thesis proved the soundness of 
bridging the results from the classification model and the severity models. Thus, this 
approach could be adopted in further road safety studies in order to provide a complete 
view of accidents, in terms of spatial and crash type patterns as well as severity factors. 
More insights could also be provided regarding hit-and-runs, by deriving models investigating 
the factors that influence the driver’s choice to leave the accident scene as implied by 
(MacLeod et al. 2012). A careful choice of land use and socio-economic variables should 
also be provided, in order to only include significant factors in road safety studies. 
 
Last, and most importantly, the achieved results in the present thesis should be employed 
by Danish policy makers in order to reduce the risk and the severity of pedestrian accidents 
throughout the country.

LCA code example 
 
PROC LCA DATA=mylib.final_dataset    /* dataset used for the analysis */ 
         OUTEST = mylib.oest2        /* definition of several outputs files */ 
         OUTPARAM = mylib.oparam2  
         OUTPOST = mylib.opost2  
         OUTSTDERR = mylib.sderr2; 
 
TITLE 'Analysis single pedestrian-single vehicle accidents in denmark (no-hit-
and-run) 2 clusters'; 
 
/* number of clusters considered */ 
 
NCLASS 2;                             
 
/* definitions of response variables */ 
 
ITEMS p2 p3 p4 p50 p6 d100 d11 d12 d13 d14 d15 d16 d17 d18 r1 r2 r3 r4  r50 r6 
r7 r8 r9 r10 t1 t2 t3 en1 en2 en3 en4 en5 l3 l2 l4 s1 s20 s3 s4 s5 s60 s80 ex1 
ex2 ex3 ex4 ex5; 
 
/* definitions of responses categories for each variable */ 
 
CATEGORIES 2 9 2 7 2 6 2 9 2 3 6 5 4 2 6 3 2 2 7 4 6 3 4 4 4 2 5 4 2 2 2 6 4 4 
2 4 3 3 3 4 4 4 5 4 2 3 3; 
 
/* primary key for the observations */ 
 
ID UHELDS_ID;   
         
/* stabilization parameter */ 
                 
RHO PRIOR=1;  
 
/* number of replications */ 
                           
NSTARTS 50;  
 
/* random starting values */                            
 
SEED 518489;     
                        
RUN;

Spatial logit code example 
 
# set working directory 
setwd("C:/Users/Alexandre/Dropbox/Thesis/Models/R ") 
 
# load input files 
load("xcor.rda")     # load X and Y coordinates of accidents 
load("ycor.rda") 
load(“data.rda”)     # load data file 
 
# number of observations 
N<-nrow(data) 
 
 
#--------------------------compute weight matrix------------------------------ 
 
# compute distance matrix 
dist<-matrix(0,nrow=N,ncol=N) 
 
for(i in 1:N)  # for each row 
{ 
  for(j in 1:N) # for each column 
  { 
    temp<-sqrt((xcor[i]-xcor[j])^2+(ycor[i]-ycor[j])^2) # compute distance  
    dist[i,j]<-temp 
 
  } 
} 
 
dist[dist<1]<-1 # if distance <1 m, value set to 1 (need for matrix inversion) 
 
# compute 1/distance weight 
 
weight<-1/(dist)  # inverse the matrix to the power of 3 
 
weight[row(weight)==col(weight)]<-0 # place zeros in the diagonal 
 
sumrows<-rowSums(weight) 
 
# sum the rows to normalise 
 
for(i in 1:N)  # for each row 
{ 
    weight[i,]<- weight[i,]/sumrows[i] 
# normalise values 
} 
 
save(weight, file="weight.rda") 
 
 
#---------------------------estimate spatial logit model---------------------- 
require(McSpatial) 
 
model<-splogit(PinjuryBINARY~Pmale+Pdrug+bike+bus+… ,wmat=weight, data=data) 
Model formulation 
Weight matrix 
Input data

# ---------------------------produce J-test value----------------------------- 
 
#Prepare X matrix 
xmat<-subset(data, 
select=c("Pmale","Page09le","Page10_14","Page15_19","Page20_29","Page60_69","P
age70pl","Pdrug","Phidden","Pwalkroad","Pwalkpav","Pstand","Pforeign","bike","
moped","van","heavyveh","bus","Dmale","Dage24le","Dage60pl","Ddrug","Dturnrigh
t","Dturnleft","Dreversing","Rexpnat","Rregional","Rmajorlocal","Rintersection
","Rcurve","Rspeed50","Rspeed50_80","Rspeed80pl","Rshopping","Rindustrial","Rr
esidentialtall","Rresidentiallow","Twinter","Tautumn","Tweekend","T21_06","T06
_09","T15_18","T18_21","ENdark","ENillum","ENpoorvis","ENwet","ENmidjut","ENno
rthjut","ENzealand","ENsouthjut","ENfyn","Lintermediate","Lrural","Lremote","R
_lowspeedroad","S_unemploy_log","EX_walk_log","EX_hospital")) 
 
#convert xmat to a matrix format 
 
xmat<-data.matrix(xmat, rownames.force = NA) 
 
# prepare matrix of spatially weighed variables WX 
wxmat<-weight_3 %*% xmat 
 
# create Z vector Z=(X, WX) 
Z<-cbind(xmat,wxmat) 
 
#retrieve error vector for model output 
v<-unlist(model["u"]) 
 
#create g  
g<-(1/N)*t(Z)%*%v 
 
#create omega (heteroskedastic error variance matrix) 
omega<-diag(v^2) 
 
#compute J-test value 
require(matrixcalc) 
jtest<-N*t(g)%*%matrix.inverse(t(Z)%*%omega%*%Z)%*%g 
 
jtest 
 
#------------------------- derive goodness-of-fit measures-------------------- 
 
# create binary variables: severe/fatal, the other one for no/light injury 
severe_fatal<-data[,"PinjuryBINARY"] 
dim(severe_fatal)<-c(N,1)   # change format to matrix 
 
no_light<-severe_fatal 
no_light[no_light==1]<- -1 
no_light<-no_light+1 
 
# extract the parameters 
temp<-unlist(model["coef"]) 
==PAGE:250==
250 
 
cst<-temp[1]                    # intercept 
beta<-temp[2:(length(temp)-1)]  # make vector of beta parameters 
dim(beta)<-c(length(beta),1)   # convert beta to a matrix 
rho<-temp[length(temp)]  
  # retrieve rho parameter (spatial lag) 
 
# compute logit utilities 
logit_u<- cst+xmat%*%beta 
 
# compute spatial logit utilities 
invmat<-matrix.inverse((diag(N)-rho*weight_3)) # I-rho*W 
splogit_u<-invmat%*%logit_u  
 
# compute probabilities 
prob_splogit<-exp(splogit_u)/(1+exp(splogit_u)) 
 
# compute log likelihood for spatial logit 
log_likelihood_splogit<-
t(severe_fatal)%*%log(prob_splogit)+t(no_light)%*%log(1-prob_splogit) 
log_likelihood_splogit 
 
# compute log likelihood for logit 
modellogit<-glm(PinjuryBINARY~Pmale+Pdrug+bike+…,data=data,family="binomial") 
 
temp<-unlist(modellogit["coefficients"]) 
cst_logit<-temp[1] # intercept 
beta_logit<-temp[2:(length(temp))] # make vector of beta parameters 
dim(beta_logit)<-c(length(beta_logit),1)  #convert beta to a matrix 
 
# compute logit utilities 
logit_u<- cst_logit+xmat%*%beta_logit 
 
# compute probabilities 
prob_logit<-exp(logit_u)/(1+exp(logit_u)) 
 
# compute log likelihood 
log_likelihood_logit<-t(severe_fatal)%*%log(prob_logit)+t(no_light)%*%log(1-
prob_logit) 
log_likelihood_logit 
 
#verify results 
require(support.CEs) 
out<-gofm(modellogit) 
init_loglik<-as.numeric(out["LL0"]) 
 
# compute rho square and adjusted rho square for spatial logit 
rho_splogit<- 1-log_likelihood_splogit/init_loglik 
adj_rho_splogit<- 1-((log_likelihood_splogit-
length(unlist(model["coef"])))/(init_loglik))
